worldwide.Itprovidesauniqueandinnovativeresourceforthelatestdevelopments
in the field, nurturing a comprehensive and encompassing publication venue for
urbanstudies,urbangeography,planningandregionaldevelopment.
Theseriespublishespeer-reviewedvolumesrelatedtourbanization,sustainabil-
ity, urban environments, sustainable urbanism, governance, globalization, urban
andsustainabledevelopment,spatialandareastudies,urbanmanagement,transport
systems,urbaninfrastructure,urbandynamics,greencitiesandurbanlandscapes.It
alsoinvitesresearchwhichdocumentsurbanizationprocessesandurbandynamics
on a national, regional and local level, welcoming case studies, as well as
comparativeandappliedresearch.
Theserieswillappealtourbanists,geographers,planners,engineers,architects,
policy makers, and to all of those interested in a wide-ranging overview of
contemporary urban studies and innovations in the field. It accepts monographs,
editedvolumesandtextbooks.

Chapter 1
A New Vision Regarding Spatial Tensions
in Urban Design
MicheleCerrutiBut,AgimKercuku,GiuliaSetti,andIaniraVassallo
Abstract Together with the urban movements from the 1970s, the 1980s or the
contemporary ones, with conflicts regarding the right to the city, social emancipa-
tion,politicaloppressionorenvironmentalissues,adifferentkindofconflictisalso
entanglingthecontemporarycity:spatialtensions.Itisaweakerbutmorepersistent
formofconflicthappeninginanycity’severydaylifebetweenopposingforces.Far
away from violent conflicts and riotous surges, contemporary tensions are usually
hidden,sometimesimplicitorexplicit:theopposingforcesdonotturnintoastruggle
butremainnoiselesslyanddynamicallytense.Alsoifsuchaspecificformofconflict
is many times weak and invisible, itgenerates strong and relevant transformations
ofthecity:spatialtensionsarerepresentedbyminute,continuous,overlaid,dilated
overtimeandstillinprogressmodificationsofspace.
· · ·
Keywords Spatialtensions Leanconflicts Urbandesign Vision
1.1 TheBeginningofaLongResearchProject
This book is the result of a collective research performed over an extended period
of time; it involved individual research and moments when the curators met to
discuss and share their work. It all began in 2016 in the wake of the study enti-
tled Territories in Crisis. Architecture and Urbanism Facing Changes in Europe

(Bianchettietal.2015)undertakentodemonstratehowthe2007economiccrisishad
necessitatedtheresignificationofseveraltermsofarchitecturalandurbanplanning
design.Anewsemanticsbasedontheobservationofterritorieshadtobedeveloped.
Consequently,thisresearchmarkednotonlythemomentofitslaunch,butalsothe
commongroundoftheprojectsharedbythecurators,structuringaworkmethodto
measure the implications of the significant “change inthe grain of the world” that
wasunderway(Bianchetti2016).Thiswastheresearchapproachthatinspiredthis
bookandtheconceptofspatialtensions.
Weconsiderspatialtensionsaconceptthatemergedwhenwequestionedourown
personalstudies.Byexchangingideasandmaintainingconstantdialogue,basedon
theobservationofthedynamicsunderwayinthecontemporarycity,wethoughtitis
possibletodevelopalenswithwhichtointerprettheassonances.Thelensofspatial
tensionsistherefore,firstandforemost,atooltointerpretthespatialfalloutofcertain
social,economicandpoliticalprocesses.Itisawayforustotacklethecomplexity
of territories and attempt to define design’s operational margins. Our research on
this concept produced an initial publication (Tensioni urbane. Ricerche sulla città
checambia,LetteraVentidue,2017)followedbyseveralmomentswhentheconcept
was“tested”inothercontexts.Thebookwasthenrepeatedlypresented;duringthese
presentations wewelcomed comments by scholars,researchers and colleagues1 as
well as different territorial stakeholders. After assembling these observations, we
organized a special session during the AESOP Conference in 20192 in order to
test the spatial tensions topic in very different territorial contexts. This not only
prompted us to explore the concept further, but also opened up new avenues: it
demonstratedhow“urbantensions”areanopportunityforustofocusonthecityand
itsinhabitants.Acityoftenconsideredforeign,incapableofreflectingsocietyand
fromwhichweperhapsnolongerexpectanything,likeMr.Palomarwho“realised
that things between him and the world are no longer proceeding as they used to;
beforetheyseemedtoexpectsomethingofeachother,heandtheworld;nowheno
longerrecallswhattherewastoexpect,goodorbad”(Calvino1983).Howevereven
if a city is unable to capture the nuances of society, it does capture its aspirations,
forcesandinterests;thisiswhyitsspacesareonceagainamalleableopportunityto
rethink the future. So in this book, we study a dual interpretation: How do spatial
tensionsmodifyspaceanditsuse?andatthesametime:Howdoesurbanplanning
designgeneratespatialtensions?
Webelievethattherelationshipofconnectionanddependencybetweentensions
andspatialdesignismorethanrelevanttodesign.Thisistheissuewestudyinthis
book,notleastbecausethehistoricalcontextinvolvesimportanttransformations.

1.2 WhatAreSpatialTensions?
Thecityhasalwaysbeenthespaceofconflicts,andurbanplanninghasalwaysbeena
conflictualpractice(Routledge2010;Pasqui2017:25).Inthe1970s,themovements
fortherighttothecitystronglyclaimedanewspacestatusandopenedanewseason
ofurbanstudies(Lefebvre1968).Variouswavesofmovementsandrebellionscycli-
callytookturnsontheurbanstagesincetheeighties,embodyingdifferentpurposes,
adopting distinct modalities and entangling an exacerbating societal discomfort
gettinggraduallyworst(Donzelot2004).
Whilethesemovementsdemonstratehowthecityisstillakeywitnessofacommu-
nity’ssocialandeconomicissues,nowadaysothertypesofemergingconflictsoccur
inurbanspace.Althoughsuchsituationsarenotasviolentandaggressiveasmany
conflictsare,thoseforcesembodyapowerfulagencyingeneratingspatialandsocial
transformationsandbringtolighttheongoingtransformationsofeconomicmodels,
thecrisisofpoliticsandinstitutionsandthedevelopmentofincreasingsocietalaware-
ness.Becauseoftheirlightnessandlong-lastingduration,suchundergroundurban
forcesarehardtoperceiveandinterpretintheurbanfabricanddonotexplodeinto
violence and disruptive actions. On the other hand, however, they are even more
presentanddiffuse;theygenerateinasubtle,lessdeclaredway,buttheiractionhas
deeper repercussions in space, and their effect is even more persistent over time.
Thanks to the pressure of those forces, which differ in terms of relevance, goals,
frequencies and directions, the territory shows a new dynamism and an unprece-
dentedcapacityfortransformation.Wecalltheseforces“urbantensions”tospecify
theirdifferencefromtheaforementionedconflicts.
Soweaskourselves:Canthesetensionsportrayanewseasonofurbantransfor-
mationsornewdesigndynamics?Canthesetensionsbenotonlystudied,butalso
becomeanurbanandarchitecturaldesigntool?
1.2.1 LeanConflicts
Theimpulsiveandoftenviolentnatureofconflicts,whethertheyhavethecharacter
ofrecognition,interest,orideology(Pizzorno1993:195–200),oftenrequiresimme-
diate responses from urban planning and politics. The political effort to deal with
thoseeventsof“civildiscordandintrinsicenmity”(Machiavelli1532:I)hasacquired
ampleevidence,whetherit“takesthemseriously”bycopingwiththe“asymmetries
ofpowerasanunavoidableconditionofurbanspace”(Pasqui2017:26),orwhether
it considers argumentation or negotiation as a concrete solution to treat or reduce
conflicts.
The tensions this book describes are lean conflicts: indeed, they maintain the
“significant uncertainty of value” as conflicts do (Veca 1997: 120), but they differ
inspatialimplications.Whileconflictsactonspacebyradicaldisruption,tensions
insteadtransformthespacebydynamic,continuousandminutemodificationsthat

### Page 12

6 M.CerrutiButetal.
occur over time. Such frictions are sometimes visible in the urban morphology of
places,othertimestheymaybeseenintheusesofspace.Theyaregeneratedbylatent
competing economies, by stakeholders involved in stating the value of a building,
by communities that redefine the ownership of places with their uses. For these
reasons,tensionsdefineamicro-geographyoftransformationsthatpoliciesusually
do not investigate or regulate. Urbanism itself hardly explores and recognizes the
phenomenon. In other words, tensions often occur in anonymous places that the
researchersignorebecauseoftheircomplexityorbecauseoftheirdistancefrommajor
urbantransformations.Nevertheless,tensionsformastateofdynamicequilibrium
claimingnewformsofurbanplanningbyitsinstability.
Theconditionoftension,infact,allowstheforcesatstaketocoexistincomplex
situationsofnegotiation,dialogueandmutualindifference,replacingtheroleofspace
at the centre and rewriting new uses, meanings and values. They show a wrinkled
space,withstratificationsanddepressions.Adifficult,denied,conqueredspace—a
spacethatisadirectexpressionoftheneedsofitsactorsandistheprotagonistoftheir
actions.Inshort,itisa“dense”spaceinwhichtensionsactandareimplemented,one
thatisatthecentreofmutationsandconvergencesofusesandvalues.Thetensionsat
playshowlatentorobviousconflicts,theydescribeprocessesinadelicateandfragile
balance,butneverthelessareabletoholdtogethertheissuesexpressed.Theproject
that moves within this complex terrain should aim to accommodate changes and
transitionsgeneratedbythetensionsinthefield;itisafleetingproject,intervening
inpointsordisputedandforgottenspaces.Observethecitythroughdifferentscales,
talking about the transformation processes underway and how these can highlight
andhighlightnewcontradictions.
1.2.2 AboutConflict
Conflicts, demands, protests, revolutions and tensions are quite rightly considered
amongst the most important “critical reasons” of design (Gregotti 1988), not just
becauseurbandisruptionsareconsideredasmomentsofchange,pointsofnoreturn
that design is forced to solve, repair and reorder as well as spark “modern urban
planning”(Benevolo1963).Norbecauseconflictsrepresentthedemandsforintimate
vitalities,inotherwordsthehopethatMarcusesearchesforamongstthe“desperate”,
proposinga“mythical”modelwheremanexitsasingledimensionthankstothepower
ofimaginationthatbecomestangibleinthestrugglesofthesixties(Marcuse1964).
Norbecauseconflictsanddemandsarecapableof“producingspace”(Indovina2017)
and demanding an incontestable and sacrosanct right to the city (Lefebvre 1968,
1974).Severalexemplarycasesfromamongsttheepisodesofconflictthattookplace
incitiesinthesecondhalfofthetwentiethcentury,aswellasthemajorinterpretative
trendsillustratedbyurbanplanningandsociologicalandeconomicliteratureofthe
lastpartofthetwentiethcentury,demonstratethatconflicts,tensions,demandsand
urbandisruptionsarerelevanttodesignbecausetheyhavetrulytransformed,more
orlessrapidlyandmoreorlesseffectively,thecitieswelivein(Gualinietal.2016).

### Page 13

1 ANewVisionRegardingSpatialTensionsinUrbanDesign 7
This work, focusing on the multifaceted category of “urban tension”, is part of
this disciplinary field and owes much to this specific literature. Literature about
conflictbecomesausefultooltonarratethenewdimensionofurbandemandsthat
hasemergedinthelastfewyears.Infact,ontheonehand,itenablesustocomprehend
thereasonsthatpromptedthisstudy,differinginparttothoseofliterature;thisstudy
proposesamoreprecisevisionofaconflictualmodelofourcitieswhich,nevertheless,
hasnotbeenstudiedbymanyoftheaforementionedauthors.Ontheotherhand,it
allowsustounderstandhowcloseandsimilarthecurrentperspectiveistotraditional
studies on urban conflicts, basically describing “tension” as a category that is not
completelynovel,albeitwithimportantpeculiaritiesthatareoutlinedbelow.
1.2.3 Seasons
WithregardtotheepisodesofconflictthattookplaceinEuropeancitiesinthesecond
half of the twentieth century, we believe it best to concentrate on the period after
thecrisisoftheseventies,adoptingtheviewalreadyindicatedbyDonzelot(2004)
whonotedacleardifferencebetweenthemovementsordisruptionsinthesixtiesand
seventies and the ones that took place in the eighties and nineties. It is interesting
to take as examples the episodes that occurred in 1981 (especially the riots in the
MinguettesneighbourhoodinVénissieux—Rhône),secondlytheviolentriotsinthe
banlieueinFrancein2005,thatstartedinClichy-sous-BoisonOctober27andspread
toothercitiessuchasRennes,Marseille,Nice,etc.,andfinallytheepisodesoftension
publiclysparkedbythepoliceintheautumnof2016,alsoinFrance.Ourfocuson
Frenchconflictsisbecausewebelievetheydemonstratehowtheconceptof“conflict”
haschangedovertimeandbecausetheseepisodestriggeredaninternationaldebate
andledtoarathervariedandmultifaceteddisciplinaryliterature.Thefirstepisodeswe
refertochieflyinvolveprotestsagainsttheisolationandpoorqualityoflifeinFrench
suburbs(thebanlieuethatexpandedexponentiallyinthegloriousthirtiesinpost-war
France),buttheyalsoincludedemandsforcitizens’rightsandthefightagainstracism
(thatendedinthebigmarchinParis“pourl’égalitéetcontreleracisme”in1983).
Insteadtheuprisingsin2005wereallabouttheexclusionofthebanlieue,segregation,
and distance from an increasingly rich and indifferent middle class, as well as the
substantivealmostethnicoppositionofseveralpartsofthecity.Inthecentre,there
werenolongeranyrightsordesiretorequalifythecityanditssociety,butratherthe
assertionofasocialmalaise.Theseinterclassmovementsgroupedindividualswho
fraternizedbecausetheysharedthesamefeelingofexclusionandexpulsionfromthe
city.Althoughthedemonstrationsin2016didnotinvolveextremeactsofviolence,
theyrepresentthewayinwhichtheFrenchpolice—andmanycitizens—reactedtothe
increasingindependenceofthebanlieueandtheaggressivenessofthosecommunities
towardstheauthorities.Nevertheless,therewerequestionsof“identity”behindthese
demonstrations(quicklyfollowedandimitatedinmuchofEurope)thatvindicatedthe
collectivefeargeneratedbyterroristactsandalleged“ethnicinvasions”.Butabove
allwhatemergedwasthecrisisofthestateandregulations,onceagaintriggeredby

### Page 14

8 M.CerrutiButetal.
opposingpartsofthecityandthespatialfragmentationthatBernardoSecchinotedin
hisvisionfortheGrandPari(s)withanembittered“charterofLucifer”(Secchiand
Viganò 2011:156).Conflicts verydifferenttotheones examined here.They were
constantly present in debates and cited in literature; they mobilized philosophers,
politicians and writers who referred to political, cultural or economic causes (e.g.
theworksbyBadiouorMichelHouellebecq)whichhoweverdidnotinvolvespace
andsocietyinextremeconditionsofexasperation,inequalityandthedissolutionof
solidarity (Donzelot 2004). Conflicts in which spatial conditions were the reason
behindthestagingofdemandswhichunleashdestructiveforcesorviolentpressure
on space. The news coverage and literature about these urban movements or riots
highlight the existence of three successive seasons, not only characterized by the
referenceperiodofthedisruptions,butalsoandabovealltherolethattheprotests
had within the city; they emphasized the riots or movements linked to the explicit
demandforrights,expulsionandsafety.Duringthefirsttwoseasons,focusingfirst
on the demand for rights and then forms of exclusion, urban space was used as a
stage and, in some cases, became the reason for the protests. During that season,
urbanspacewasthescenarioandsiteofexpressed,declaredconflictsthattherefore
remainedunsolved.Ontheonehand,thesekindsofconflictsareanecessaryelement
forthecityanditsgovernment(Hirschman1977),buttheyarealsoandalwaysthe
thermometerofwhatthegovernmentisunabletorestrain,orwhatescapesitscontrol,
andwhichneverthelessisforcefullymadevisibleinordertoattractattention.This
is why “tension” is a crucial key to understanding the transformations that took
placeduringthatperiod;itallowsustointerprettheinstabilityofthosesitesandthe
forces at play. In the last ten years, with the advent of the global economic crisis,
the city is once again an explosive force, a place where it is possible to display a
feeling of exclusion and social disaggregation, a place of intense conflict, where
spacewitnessesnotonlytheclashes,butalsoalevelofsegregationexacerbatedby
thegapbetweentherichandpoor(Secchi2013).
As we will see, what is examined here is not this extensively studied kind of
demand,butratherthenegligibleeverydayconflictsthatare,however,ableto“build
space”astheresultofthe“combinedandoftenconflictualactionofnumerouspower
groups”involvedintherewritingandmoreorlessnegotiatedmodellingofthecity
andyetcapableofprotectinguses,ritesandspatialpractices(DeCerteau1980).
1.2.4 Literature
Literature about the conflict refers to a genre of Marxist studies considered as a
usefullenstomeasurechangesinsocialrelationships,tacklesocietyandeconomic
and anthropological relations and develop philosophical issues. Naturally, we are
talkingaboutHenriLefebvre’sworksregardingtherighttothecity(1968)andthe
productionofspace(1974),DavidHarvey’sideasabout“rebelcities”(2012),Robert
Castel’spublicationsconcerningsocialinsecurity,andJacquesDonzelot’sworkson
urbanriots.Allcrucialinordertounderstandthecontradictionsandtensionsofurban

### Page 15

1 ANewVisionRegardingSpatialTensionsinUrbanDesign 9
living, democracy and the structural mechanisms that determine it (as in Harvey
2012).
This trend became an influential background against which to tackle the topic
of conflict in several disciplinary fields. In fact it is the framework within which
numerousstudiesandresearchesusethenotionofconflicttounderstandthefunc-
tioningofthepoliciesgoverningthecity,thedesignofspaceandthewaysinwhich
tensionsandrewritingstransformtheterritory.Referenceismadeheretotheworks
by Gualini, Mourato and Allegra, specifically policies, but also Bernardo Secchi’s
studies and projects, e.g. his work on Paris and his book about urban inequalities
(2013).Thesestudiesfocusonthewayinwhichthecitycanreactandallowitself
tobemodelledandrewritten(undoubtedly,regardingthelatter,theresearchbyDe
Certeau).Theyfocusonthecity’sresilienceorfragility,balancedbetweenunstable
models (Bourdin 2016) and the ability to adapt and respond to the great traumas
inflicted on it (Taleb 2012; Blecic and Cecchini 2016); however, the rhetoric of
resilience and antifragility conceals old determinisms compared to the interpreta-
tion of space and its transformations. This series portrays the city as the complex
result of conflicts, tensions and re-appropriations of uses and values, so much so
that the “text” of the city is in a state of permanent change; the outcome of its
continuous transformation is a sort of “urban bricolage” (Volli 2008). A construc-
tionusingsmallelementsandrecomposingpartstowhichColinRowehadalready
dedicatedachapterinhisbookCollageCity(1978).In“CollisionCityandthePoli-
tics of Bricolage”, he describes the forms of conflict that influenced the definition
of the city in the modern movement and de facto sanctioned the conflictual and
neverresolvedformswithwhichdesignhelpedtodefineurbanform.Whatemerges
forcefully and deliberately is that any urban act, any delimitation of land use, can
generateconflictsanddangerousinstabilitiesintherelationshipsbetweentheplayers
inquestion,prefiguringdifferenturbaninterventionscenarios.
Inshort,inspiredbythisliteraturewebelievethefocusshouldbeplacednotonly
on violent conflicts and sensational and uncontrolled demands, but also on looser,
less explicit conflicts which can, however, profoundly modify the city. We believe
wecansaythattensionisnotacompletelynewcategorywhenitcomestodescribing
urbantransformations.Apartfromhighlyconflictualurbanevents,wecanusespace
tointerceptthetensionsunderpinningthefragilebalancesthataredecisiveforthe
future of urban fabrics. As illustrated below, urban complexity makes it possible
to identify several categories of tension that “animate” spaces: tensions that have
generatedconflicts,fractures,discontinuitiesandseparations;tensionsthatmodify
spacethroughaseriesofmicro-transformations;tensionsthathighlightproximities
anddistancesbetweenpopulationsandindividuals,withoutevereruptingintoopen
conflict. Due to changes in the economy, culture, politics and society, this kind
ofconflictappearstobeincreasinglypresent,rootedandexperimented;itpresents
opportunitiestointerpretsocietyandrecomprehenddesign—issuesthatliteratureon
urbanriotshasonlypartiallytackled—andmakesitpossibletoprovideadifferent
interpretationoftheconceptof“urbantension”.

10 M.CerrutiButetal.
1.2.5 TheRoleofSpace
The debate on urban conflicts was founded on the existing, deep-rooted, albeit
unstated conviction that the city was stable and that it functioned based on linear
systemsandprocesses(Indovina2017).Thatspacewastherepresentationandstage
ofthegreatdramaoftheworldaswellastheobjectofdisputesandconfrontations
demanding urban rights; that it was measurable, restricted, controllable and regu-
lable,basedonthesolidarchetypesofterritorialgovernmentsuchasmeasurement,
footprintsandtheHippodamianplandividingrights,regulatingusesandredefining
values(Mazza2013).Thecitywasthelasttwentieth-centurycertainty:theseatof
democracy,ofthehorizontalexplicationofrights,ofsolidspatialstability,andthe
figureandimageofthesocietythatlivedinit(Bianchetti2011).
Reviewingthespaceofthecityrevealedanddisplayedby(urban)meansadopting
adynamicperspectivethatexaminesthechanges,themutations,andtheabsenceor
hypertrophy of the boundary rather than its static standardization processes rather
thanevents.
Thismindset,andtheresearchmethodadoptedbythisstudy,capturesunstable
balances,relationshipsbetweenimaginedandsometimescontrastingconditionsand
aspirations, forces that exert their influence in different ways, but share a single
medium:space.
We have already mentioned that the space we are studying is both dense and
markedbyregulations,rightsandvaluesthatbecomeameanstoexpresstheforces
atplay,aplacewherewecanobservethesocial,culturalanddesign-orientedresults
of the observed tensions. Space emerges as a study tool, valuable to think about
concepts (Foucault 1984), and as a decisive category with which to reason about
thefuture(AriasandWarf2008).Spacebecomesamediumoftherelationshipsof
tension; sometimes it coincides with tension itself (McLuhan and Fiore 1967) or,
in all cases, strongly characterizes it (Eco 2003); it essentially opens up a field of
experimentation capable of rewriting and rethinking the balance between entities,
players,andforcesatplay;awaytoreturntothedesignofthecityanditsplaces.
Space becomes a field of action involving a new way to describe and inter-
pret cities. Just changing the description is not helpful; instead it is important to
redefine the terms used to narrate the city. Compared to the past, interpreting the
implicationsoftensioninthecontemporarycityiscurrentlymorefeasiblebecause
economic,institutional,socialandothercriseshavemadetheboundariesmorefluid
andlessrestrictive,pavingthewayfordifferentwaystoexpressthetensionandits
consequences.
We will reflect on what it means to design the city today, at a time of radical
change, where there are no explicit conflicts but latent forms of opposition, and
where many rights and values seem to be guaranteed; as a result the role of urban
and architectural design has to be repositioned. The design of the city and for the
cityshouldsheditsomni-comprehensivevisionandinsteadtrytoredefinetherole
ofpublicassets,reconstructcollectiveformsofwelfareandencouragetheadventof
adifferentsetofskillsandactorsresponsibleforregulatingaction.Thedesignofthe

### Page 17

1 ANewVisionRegardingSpatialTensionsinUrbanDesign 11
contemporary city must be able to capture current, significant phenomena; it must
understandthecriticalissues,differencesandmechanisms.Inthecasesinquestion,
economic mechanisms reveal how transnational production models are linked and
superimposedontraditionallocalproductionsystems,highlightingthewaysinwhich
itispossibletodevelopdialoguebetweengenerationsandpopulationswitheconomic
means, values, intentions and divergent life aspirations. Financially speaking, it is
necessarytoidentifytheeffectsofaself-referentialandprivatemodelofdevelopment
vis-à-visthepubliclifeofsociety.Culturallyspeaking,itisimportanttounderstand
theburdensomeheritageandproblematiclegacyofthetwentiethcentury.
1.3 WhatisTensionToday?
1.3.1 Forces,Balance,andSpace
Bytensionwemeanastateofdynamicbalancebetweenforcesactinginspace.We
donotreferheretothetensionsthatariseafterthemoreorlesslatentcontrastsand
hostilities that lead to exacerbation, hardening and open conflicts. Nor to the rigid
confrontations between religious cultures, ideologies and populations. Instead we
refer to the subtle form of relationship which, without ever becoming conflictual,
bringsintoplaythecoexistenceandunstablebalanceofforcesandinterests.
Thisstateofdynamicbalanceisnotapeacefulcondition,butaslow,continuous,
persistent search for a mobile barycentre; it is the end result of negotiations and
the more or less explicit concerted actions that are triggered on a suspended and
always poised plane. It is a precarious state involving continuous adjustments and
subtlealterationsthattakeplaceinspaceand,atarelationalandsociallevel,within
a complex system of players and processes that are not always easy to identify or
describe.Abalancecharacterizedaboveallbyaratherdilatedtimeframe,byasome-
whatslowtrendandyetbyprogressinvolvingsubtlepostponementsandrewritings
which,withoutdeconstructingthestructureexabrupto,graduallytransformsit.This
condition permits coexistence, intersections and superimpositions by virtue of its
developmenttimeandthecautiousrelationshipbetweenforceswhich,forthesame
reasons,isnotregulatedorgovernedbyspecificpolicies.Publicresponseisinfact
sloworabsentduetothefactitisimpossibletosystematicallyinterpretcomplexity
and because tension does not dissolve in confrontations or in the manifestation of
socialneeds.
As per the case studies illustrated in this book, forces are exerted by several
players—differentpopulations,specificprivateandpublicentities,localormigrant
communities, etc. Often these players do not interact openly and yet they move
different interests in space, exerting interacting forces. Sometimes this pressure is
imperceptible, long-lasting, constant and yet delicate and non-invasive. At times,
itinvolvesexplosivefleetingforcesthatrequiretheplayersandspacetoadapt—an
adaptationthatnecessitatesadilatedtimeframe.Unlikeconflicts,theseforcesdonot

### Page 18

12 M.CerrutiButetal.
involvedemandsforrightsorexternalizeasituationofunease:mostoftheminvolve
pressurepromptedbyindividualorcollectiveinterestsandarethereforecapableof
bringingintoplaydifferentvisionsofthecityandeffectsthatdifferfromthefallout
ofthecapital.Furthermore, interaction between forcesoccurs inspace, and thisis
whattriggerstransformation.
Space is the condition and channel of interaction between the forces. Far from
beingthestageofconflicts,asitwasintheseventies,spaceisalsofarfrombeing
the spark behind demands, as it was during the disruptions in the eighties and
nineties.Spaceis,fortensions,theconditionbywhichitexists:theplayers,inter-
estedpartiesandexertforcesthatcreaterelationshipsbyvirtueofspace.Theyare
situatedplayersandforces,anditisthissituationthatinvolvesinteraction.Spaceis
alsothechannel,themediumofthisrelationship.Theforcesexertthemselvesina
spaceandthroughaspace,initsmorphologicalandphysicalconditions.Theforces
presentthemselvesassomethingthatgraduallytransformspace:theyoccupyit,they
useinunexpectedways,theyimbueitwithdifferentvalues,andtheybendittoever
differentwaysofdwellingandlabour.Thealterationbydegreesoftentakesalong
time and involves rewritings, removals, revivals, re-conceptualizations; the way in
whichcontrastingforcesandplayersreacttothesetransformationsbynegotiating,
enlarging,contractinganddilatingisanexpressionofthedynamicprocessinsearch
of balance. From this point of view, space is an integral part of the tension and
emergesasadenseentity:itordersrelationshipsanduses,itincorporatesmultiple
values, it highlights a plurality of values, and it is regulated in processes of small
successivepostponements.Thetensionweseedoesnotactbytransformingevents
intoasuddenandviolentform;itproceedsusingconsecutiveconservativephases,
maintainingandredefiningonacase-by-casebasistheweightoftheforcesinvolved,
their relationship and the space in which they act. It involves nonlinear processes
which, as such, suffer sudden setbacks or abrupt accelerations, leading to results
whichwecoulddefineasunexpectedandunusual.
1.3.2 TheObjectiveandStructureoftheBook
Theobjectiveofthebookistoconsiderurbantensionsasamatterofspaceanddesign,
withimplicationsondifferentscalesandinvariouscontexts.Afterastudyentitled,
TensioniUrbane.RicerchesullacittàchecambiapublishedbytheauthorsinItaly
in2017,thebookdefines“spatialtensions”asthecatalystforspecificcontemporary
social,spatialandurbandesignchanges.
The study had a dual objective. On the one hand, to reason and select projects,
placesandcasesthatdescribetensionsandspecificvariationscomparedtoconditions
wereverydifferent.Ontheother,thebooktriestodefinetheconceptof“tension”
and how it can become an important design tool in current, ongoing and future
transformations and projects. The work illustrated here presents the preliminary
resultsofastudy,stilltobecompleted,andbeginstoestablishtheboundariesofthe
roletensionplaysinurbanandarchitecturaldesign.

### Page 19

1 ANewVisionRegardingSpatialTensionsinUrbanDesign 13
Wecouldsaythattogetherwiththeurbanmovementsoftheseventies,eighties
or even contemporary movements, conflicts regarding the right to the city, social
emancipation,politicaloppressionorenvironmentalissuesnowinvolveadifferent
kind of conflict that is enveloping the contemporary city and producing stronger
effects:spatialtensions.
Itisaweakerbutamorepersistentformofconflictbetweenopposingforcesthat
istakingplaceineverydaylifeincitieseverywhere.Urbanandsocialtensionsrise
forinstanceinbetweenclashingmodels,contrastingvisionsandconversescenarios,

withindifferentusesofpublicspaces,amongstdifferentproductivemodelsoverlap-

a specific form of conflict is many times weak and invisible, it generates strong

Throughoutaninternationalcall,opentocollectexperiences,projectsandcasesfrom

toregeneratethecityitself,theirprojectsanddefiningnewdesigntools.Explicitor
implicittensionsaretheverycatalystofcontemporaryurbantransformations.
Giventheabove,thethree-partbookproposesaninterpretationof“tension”,its
importanceasadesigntoolcapableofnotonlyinterpretinganddescribingsituations
of latent conflict, but also of understanding how it is possible to work around the
frictionsbetweencommunitiesandterritories,architecturesandspaces.Urbanand
architectural design can represent real, tangible and expressive forms of the trans-
position of the concept of “spatial tension” in the field of design. The challenging
question, which this book tries to answer, is to define what we mean by “spatial
tension”usingon-sitestudies,literatureandprojects.
Thisessaypresentsseveralpossiblemeaningsfortheterm“spatialtension”;the
meanings described in the previous paragraphs highlight the nuances of a concept
whoseboundariesaredifficulttoestablish.
Theeffortwetrytoexplaininthisbookfocusesonusingdesigntodefinetensions
andthewaysandforms“tension”canusetoexpressitself.Itisanambitiousobjective;
itspremiseliesinthetheoreticalconstructionofthisconceptbasedontheprevious

2.1 TheExtractivist Discourse
Duetoitswealthofrenewablenaturalresources,theEcuadorianAmazonianregion
isbothastrategicplaceforthecountry’seconomyandaterritoryintension.Here
arethreeofthefivelarge-scaleminingprojectsconsideredstrategicforthecountry’s
economy.Theirlocationcoincideswithspacesofinformal,small-scaleextraction,
whichhavebeentakingplacesincethepre-Hispanicera.Thecoexistenceofthese
two extraction processes in the same territory is triggering socio-spatial processes
thatareeconomicallyandspatiallyintertwined.
Miningactivitieshavetriggeredtransformations,oftenwithdramaticecological
(pollutionofwater,soilandair),social(expulsionofindigenouspopulations,intro-
duction of new settlers),economic (land-use changes, new economies) and spatial
(landexpropriation,spatialsegregation,triggeringofdearurbanizationphenomena).
Theterm“extractivism”indicatesatthesametimeasocio-economicsystemand
amodelofterritorialappropriation,inwhichprocessesofsubtractionofresources,
which can be both material (raw materials, agricultural products) and intangible
(cultures, knowledge, local traditions), and they reorganize spaces, ecologies and
lifestyles.Theextractivistdiscourseisthemainsubjectofavastliterature,especially

ofasocio-economicandpoliticalmatrix(Acosta2013;Gudynas2010;Svampaand

Studies on extractive phenomena conducted from the physical-spatial point of
view are quite rare. In the Latin American context, in particular, the concept of
extractivismhasrecentlybeenusedtoanalyzesomeurbanphenomenalinkedtothe

prototypes,formsoftransnationalintegrationandinfrastructuralsystemsproduced
bylarge-scaleextractiveindustriesinSouthAmericaduringthetwentiethcentury.
Sordietal.(2017),inthebookThecampandthecity:TerritoriesofExtraction,has
studiedthetensionsthatarisebetweencitiesandminingfieldspopulatedbycommu-
nitiesofminers,analyzingtheirecologicalaspectsandpoliticaldynamicsattheterri-
torialscale.SantiagodelHierro(2017),observingthenorthernEcuadorianAmazon,
described,throughcomplexterritorialmaps,theincrementalcharactersofthespatial
colonizationprocessestriggeredbytheconstructionofextractiveinfrastructures.
These three forms of research, in which attempts have been made to establish
linksbetweensocialstudiesandterritorialanalyzes,arecentered,respectively,onthe
identificationofurbanexperimentsaroundtheminingtown,socio-politicalconflicts
between mining space and context and incremental colonization processes. These
studiesarerelevanttermsofreferencebut,insomeways,seemexcessivelyaimedat
theanalysisoflarge-scaleextractivephenomenaandpolicies.Moreover,atendency
to focus only on large mining or oil extraction processes prevails, neglecting to
analyze functional and spatial relationship between extraction “from above” and
“from below” in Amazonian contexts. Little has been said about the so-called
phenomenaofinformal,“frombelow”andonasmall-scaleextractionandinpartic-
ularontheoftenopaqueformsofinteractionbetweendifferentextractionscalesand
relatedlogicsofspaceproduction.
In this sense, this text tries to return the results of a survey on the socio-spatial
characteristicsthatmininggeneratesintheAmazoniancontextsofsouthernEcuador,
where“artisanal”or“informal”small-scaleminingprocessesoverlapandlarge-scale
formalorindustrialextractionthatsharesthesameterritory.1

2.1.1 Places
TheEcuadorianAmazonisaplacein“tension,”inwhichdifferenteconomic,polit-
ical,socialandenvironmentalvisionsareconfronted.TheEcuadorianeconomy,it
canbesaid,ischaracterizedbyasubstantialdependenceontheexportofrawmate-
rials,inparticularoffossilfuels.Thisconditiontriggeredintheseventiesaneraof
so-calledoilboomwhichinitiallyproducedgreatbenefitsforthecountry’seconomy.
Fifty years later, this territory continues to be a strategic and disputed place. Here
arethreeofthemainfivelarge-scaleindustrialminingprojects,consideredstrategic
atnationallevel:“FrutadelNorte,”“ProyectoMirador”and“Panantza-SanCarlos.”
The activity of these plants is considered capable of triggering the country’s most
importantminingcycleforthenext40years(SacherandAcosta2012).
Ineconomicterms,bothinformalorartisanalminingphenomenaandthoseofthe

2.3 ‘Artisanal’Mines,StratifiedSpaces
Nambija is an informal small-scale deposit located in the canton of Zamora-
Chinchipe. The mine, composed of a system of veins and shallow gold pockets,
was discovered in the eighties of the twentieth century attracting a multitude of
smallminerswhotransformedthatplaceintoanewfrontierofthe“goldrush.”This
fieldhadaboomperiodinthe1980swhenitwaspossibletoextract40gof24carat
goldpertonofstone.Currently,theaverageyieldisaround6gperton.9

On the surface of the mines, according to incremental processes, the miners’
houseswerebuilt.10 Thesearealmostalwayswoodenbuildingsrealizedonstiltsin
order to adapt to the unevenness of the ground and to remove humidity. The free
space between the pilotis often serves as a storage space. The toilets are almost
always external, and the openings also often correspond to outdoor service areas
equippedwithwashbasinsfacingoutward.
The infrastructures for the processing of mineral rocks are located within the
urbanfabric,generating,withtheirwastewater,problemsofrivercontaminationbut
alsoconfiguringasystemofsmallpublicspaces.
The water and electricity networks are configured as “cobwebs” hanging from
the facades and roofs of the houses, identifying a particular relationship between
residentialspacesandsanitationinfrastructures.
Ingeneral,theentireurbanstructurehasadendriformcharacter,markedbystair-
ways that connect the upper and lower parts of the settlement and articulated by
branchedconnectionsystemsthatendinsmallpublicspaces.Thegeneralsettlement
structurehasaparticularcharacterofdomesticityresultingfromthespatialinterpen-
etrationbetweenurbanconnectionsystemsanddomesticspaces.Thisstratification
allowsustoconsiderNambijaasaspacethatisbothprecariousandwelcomingat
thesametime.
Theinformalextractiontriggeredsettlementprocessesarticulatedaccordingtoa
superimposition of layers. Some of these are visible and correspond to temporary
houses, urban aerial infrastructures and public or shared “floating” or suspended
spaces. The invisible layers correspond to tunnel systems that can exceed 50 m in
height.

bandsofforestareassubjecttocutting,urbanfringes,productiveruralareasandriver

However, the city of the Troncal Amazónica is not a continuous constructed
filament. This apparently linear ribbon is actually strongly intertwined with the
surrounding forest through an articulated system of green spaces, interstices, with
sectionsrangingfromafewhundredmeters,inthedensestareas,to3–4km,inthe
mostdilated.Theseintersticescoincidewithportionsofresidualforest,areaswith
anuncertainstatusorinabandonment,cultivatedareasandthinstretchesoflinear
forest,correspondingtominorhydrographicnetworksthatcuttheurbanfilamentin
atransversedirection.Thissystem,asortof“barcode”withavariablerhythm,is
a device that on the one hand separates the various environments of the filament;
on the other, it functions as an ecological connection system on a territorial scale
betweenmultipleforestandruralenvironments.
Four types of interstices or spines can be recognized. The beds of the smaller
streams and rivers work as connecting roads with the surrounding territories, but
also as thin strips of agricultural areas, allowing access to the Zamora River and,
occasionally, to some places for the extraction of gravel and sand. Their section
ranges from 50 to 100 m. The rural spines, mostly banana groves or sugar cane
fields,representatthesametimeplacesofproductionandsupplyonasmallscale
andareasofhighsocialvalueastheyhostcommunityorsemi-publicfacilitiessuch
astheso-calledcanchas,smallareasdiscoverieswithoutspecificuses,fromthesize
ofaplayground,whicharesociallyactivated,onweekends,hostingsportingevents
andsmallinformalmarkets.Thesespinesaretypically2to3kmwide.Athirdtype
of interstice is constituted by the portions of the residual Amazonian forest. Their
sizevariesfrom2to3km.Alasttypeofplugisgivenbyaminutesystemofresidual
spacesthatdefinesthecontactzonesbetweenresidentialareasandruralsettlements.
Thesespacesgenerallymeasurefrom500mto1km.
Thisvarietyofintersticescanbeinterpretedasasetof“contactzones,”asMary
Louise Pratt (1992) would say, of spaces that at the same time separate and relate
appropriateenvironments,ecologiesorpartsofcitiesfromdifferentsocialgroups,
communitiesofminers,mestizosandnatives.11Allthismakesitpossibletoconsider

tructuresformobility,hydrographicnetworks,urban,ruralandforestenvironments
regulated by a system of devices which at the same time operate a separation and
arelationshipbetweenenvironments.Thislinearcityisthephysicalsupportwhere
economic and social flows linked to both transnational and local mining activities

2.4 BaroqueTextures
IntheEcuadorianAmazon,industrialextractionprocessesandartisanalextraction
processes overlap on the same territory, establishing complex and often invisible
economic,socialandspatialrelationsbetweenthem.
Large-scaleindustrialminesmanagedbyforeigneconomicgroupsareoftenbuilt
interritorieswheretheinformalminingpracticehasbeenunderway,insomecases,
fordecadesandthroughbottom-upinitiativeslinkedtotheinitiativeofindividuals
orsmallgroups.Thepresenceofthisworkforcealreadyspecializedinacertainarea
favorsthearrivaloftransnationalinvestorswhotendtomakeuseofthisworkforce
willing or induced to accept low wages and working conditions characterized by
subordinationtoChineseorWesternmanagementpersonnel.The“landingplaces”
ofthelargeminegenerallytendtobelocatedatadistanceofbetween30and50km
frominformalminingareas.
Thephenomenaofextraction“frombelow”areatthesametimeanexpressionof
aformofresistancetotheseformsofterritorialappropriationandofresourcesofa
neocolonialmatrix,andatthesametime,theyrepresenttheirdistortion.Theinformal
miningsettlementis,fromsomepointsofview,aspaceoffreedomforsubjectswho
do not accept production conditions or overly defined styles of living, a place of
escapeforsubjectsinsearchofeconomicredemptionwhereinfernallivingconditions
aremixedbutalsoformsofeconomicandmaterialself-supporttowardweaksubjects
andgroups.Intheextractivefrontiers,thelogicofprofitandcommunitysolidarity

### Page 37

2 ExtractivismasaFieldofTensions 33
ismixed.Informalminingcommunitiesutilizethefinancialandcommercialservice
infrastructuresassociatedwithlargeminingactivitygeneratingaparallelmarketfor
minerals.TheurbanfilamentsarrangedalongtheTroncalAmazónicaarefromthis
point of view a great connecting device or cog between the economies linked to
formalandinformalextraction.
Independent miners can decide, for a few months a year, to work at the large
mine,thusintegratingmultiplesourcesofincome,configuringthemselvesashybrid
subjectsbetweenformalandinformalformsofproduction.Atthesametime,groups
ofworkersfromthebigminehave,insomecases,chosentostartsmallillegalmining
activities.
The discourse on extractivism, in particular in the context of socio-economic
studies, is often analyzed according within the dynamics of the so-called neolib-
eraleconomies;however,theinvestigationonextractivephenomenaintheEcuado-
rianAmazonallowsustoquestiontheclassicreductionofthephenomenonextrac-
tionasaproductofmacro-economicpoliciesimposed“fromabove.”Thecommon
discourseaccordingtowhichitwouldbepossibletodefusetheneoliberalextractive
logicthroughthepromotionofnewpoliciesfrombelow,formsofself-government
capableofpromotingtheprotectionofplaces,ecologiesandlocalsocietiesis,from
somepointsofview,questionable.Whatisaffirmedhereisadifferentpointofview
thatcloselylinksneoliberalismwithprofoundtransformationsthathavetakenplace
within the capitalist mode of production in the Amazon. Neoliberal extractivism
hereappearsasaspecific“rationality,”inthesenseattributedtothistermbyMichel
Foucault,therefore,asaformof“governmentality”andspatialproductionthatmust
beinvestigatednotonlyfromabovebutalso“frombelow,”lookingatthoseatthe
processesofsubjectivation,atthedailybehaviorsintowhichitresults.Inotherwords,
extractive “rationality”and“governmentality” areanalyzed herefromthepointof
view of the ways in which they are appropriate, distorted, re-launched and altered
bylocalsocialsubjectsandgroups.
Both Proyecto Mirador and Nambija are, in this sense, border areas, “migrant
territories” marked by a multi-located dwelling that brings together mining settle-
ments, proximity urban centers and regional capitals or distant financial centers.
Theseareplacescrossedbyconnectionsthatconcernboththemovementofgoods
andthemigrationofwomenandmenwhoinhabittheareasofextraction.
It is a real “globalization from below” marked by the proliferation of “baroque
economies” that express Amazonian popular pragmatics. It is something, as the
EcuadorianphilosopherEcheverría(1998)states,deeplyintertwined,whichunites
indigenous and popular practices of resistance, and which allows us to name the
heterogeneityoftheformsofeconomicactivityonwhichtheyarebasedtodayinthe
Amazontheenhancementofthecapitalandthepersistentconditionoftheextraction.
InNamibiaandProyectoMirador,wecanseepopularproductiverationalitiesaccom-
modating a variety of labor and entrepreneurial practices. These spatial, temporal,
andmigratoryfactorsredefinetheserationalitiesasaborderepistemology,ie.,amode
ofknowledgeemergingfromthedisplacementofbodies,territoriesandlanguages.
Whatisatstakeisthepossibilitytore-theorizetheneoliberalorder,economically
andspatially,becausesuchanepistemologyallowsfordescribingpopulareconomies

### Page 38

34 A.DiCampli
as a way of mapping neoliberalism as a field of tensions among competing forces
ratherthanassimplyahegemonic order.Theconceptofbaroque economy allows
ustoexposethefragmented,opaquecharacterofneoliberalisminordertoidentify
innovative spatial polities and design strategies. The dominant narrative conceives
neoliberalism as a top-down process developed by corporations, governments and
internationalfinancialinstitutions,andembodiedinextractivist,dispossessionand
spatialappropriationpolicies.
ObservingtheEcuadorianAmazon,itispossibletoseehowextractivismisalso
developedfrombelowasarationalitythatnegotiatesprofitsinacontextofdispos-
session,inconflictdynamicsmixingformsofservitudeandresistance.Inthissense,
itispossibletoaffirmthatpoliticallegitimacyisnotnecessaryforthepersistenceof
extractivism processes and policies. Instead, its strength and persistence lie on the
diffusion and proliferation of socio-spatial practices and ecologies that reorganize
notionsoffreedom,calculationandobedience,relatedtoanetworkofpracticesand
cunningswhereextractivismcanbefinallyseenasarationality,andnotsimplyaset
ofpolicies.
Thisinteractionbetweenextractivismfromaboveandfrombelowallowsreartic-
ulating knowledge, design strategies, ecologies and urban infrastructures, making
lifepossible.
Nambija and Proyecto Mirador, from this point of view, are at the same time a
spaceofresistanceandaplaceofexploitation.Spaces,economiesandlogistics,mate-
riallyconstructedintheexperienceofmovement,ofmigration,areintertwinedhere
with often extremely violent forms of coercion to work, mobilizing “community”
resourcesnotdifferentfromthoseonwhichtheprocessesofresistanceleverage.
Analyzingtheseconstitutivelyhybridspacesallowsustohighlightthemultiple
and contradictory thrusts through which subordinate subjects negotiate the extrac-
tive rationality trying to bend it to their own strategic projects. They are certainly
sometimesoppressedwhennotcrushedwithout,however,everreducingthemselves
tooccupyingexclusivelythepositionof“victims.”
Theresultisaninterpretationofextractivismasafieldoftensionsandconflict,
asamachinethatcontinuallytearsapartplacesandsubjects,yetonecannotavoid
recognizing as an essential productive power that common of cooperation which
presentsitselfasatrueanalyticasapossiblepoliticalhorizon.

3.2 FramingNeoliberalisminSantiago
Santiago witnessed the emergence of neoliberalism in 1975 (Brenner et al. 2012;
Harvey2005;Klein2011)andbecamesincethenthemainexample,thepreferred
reference, in the planet to study neoliberal urban practices (Aguilar et al. 2016;
Janoschka and Hidalgo 2014; López and Meza 2014; Rodriguez and Rodriguez
2009). Two foundational events marked such urban history of Santiago: the visit
of Milton Friedman to Chile on the 21st of April 1975 and the implementation of
the National Policy of Urban Development in 1979. The latter is to be considered
thefirstneoliberalurbanpolicyimplementedintheworldwhoseobjectivewasthe
completetransformationoftheurbanplanninginstitutionsandregulationstomake
it a profit-oriented disciplinary field whose main ethos is ensuring the earnings of
investment and foster the idea of land as a commercial good. From then onwards,
Santiagobecameafieldofwealthextractionforrealestatedevelopersandconstruc-
tionindustry,makingoftheurbanlifeasortofresidualofurbanextractivism.This
extractivismisspecial;itisnotaboutrawmaterialsornon-humanresources,rather
is extracting capital fromthe very deficit of housing and the spatialities of uneven
urbanisation,fromtheveryessenceoftheurbanconditionanditstensions.
Despiteanevidentsimplificationduetothespaceavailability,forthesakeofour
argumenthere,urbanneoliberalismreferstotheinteractionofprocessesofneolib-
eralization and urbanisation and how such ideology is shaping and producing the
form,theimageandthelifeinthecities.AsKeilsuggeststhaturbanisationunder
neoliberalism has a material and discursive practice through which modern capi-
talistsocietiesarebeingreproduced(KeilinSpringer2016).Neoliberalurbanismis
then a descriptive category that can depict the spatiotemporal material and discur-
sivepracticesanditsoperativeanalyticalcapacityofproducingurbanspace.Under
neoliberalism, the push for homeownership by governments has been a common
feature(RossiandVanolo2015a),sounderstandingitsspatialfeaturesfromhousing
policies is one of the common strategies followed by its critics. Also, for global
financialinstitutions,thehousingprovisionunderneoliberalismhasbeenstrategic
forincreasingwealth.Startingintheearly1990s,aftertheWashingtonConsensus,
bankshavereducedtherequirementsforcreditsonmortgageswhichfacilitatedthe
accesstohomeownershipatthetimethatinvolvedthefinancialsectormoreactively
intheproductionofspaces(FernandezandAalbers2016).Anewaestheticsofcities
of neoliberalism depends, in a good share, on the way financial institutions have
participated in financing urban development, favouring certain typological spaces
that ensure the profitability of investments (Cattaneo Pineda 2011). The access of
low-income communities to multiple mortgages without financial background and
thecoercionofthefinancialsectortomaximisetheearningsfromthehousingmarket

### Page 44

40 J.F.Vergara-PerucichandC.Boano
(Vargas2016)werethemaincausesofthegreatfinancialcrisisof2008(Fernandez
andAalbers2016;FollainandGiertz2012;RossiandVanolo2015b).
Acriticalreflectionofhowneoliberalismoccupiedthedisciplinaryfieldsrelated
tourbandevelopmentisamuch-neededapproachtounpackinghowsuchideolog-
icalprojectaffectedcitiesanditsinhabitants.Neoliberalismexpandedtheideaofan
entrepreneurial society, and produced several effects in cities. David Harvey illus-
trated the idea of entrepreneurial cities as spaces where politico-economic elites
work together for experimenting through innovative ways of capital accumulation
which implied a series of socio-spatial restructuring processes as a consequence
of the speculative dynamics that had driven these entrepreneurial strategies (Rossi
andVanolo2015).Urbanentrepreneurialismwasoneofthemainconsequencesof
the implementation of neoliberal urban policies (López Morales 2009), and in the
present,thedesignofcitiesisorganisedmainlytotheinterestsofprivateinvestors.
The neoliberal regime of institutional governance that facilitated capital accumu-
lation is a process in which the capitalist state is reshaped following the logic of
entrepreneurialisminwhichbusinessandpoliticalinterestsarealigned(Tickelland
Peck2002).Neoliberalideologyandspatiologyisalsoa‘truthgame’(Spencer2016)
as ‘its accounts of human knowledge, social complexity and the economic market
legitimateitsmanagementofindividuals[…]thattheeconomicmarketisbetterable
to calculate, process and spontaneously order society than the state is able to […]
Thefunctionofarchitectureprescribedbythispositionisthatofproducingendlessly
flexibleenvironmentsforinfinitelyadaptablesubjects’.(Spencer2016,p3–4).The
breakpointbetweenapre-neoliberalwayofmakingcitiesanditsneoliberalisation
isnotsoclearinliteratureandrequiresfurtherdevelopment.
Thepre-neoliberalmodeofcityplanningandhousingproductionwasmarkedby
thestrongroleofthestateindesigningtheurbanspacebasedontheconvictionthat
thestateshouldensuredignityforpeoplebyprovidingbasicservicessuchaseduca-
tion, health and housing (Hidalgo-Dattwyler 2019). Prior to neoliberalization, the
ChileangovernmentsofFreiandAllendeadvancedtowardawelfarestateinwhich
the development model intensified the investment of public funds in the internal
economy (Salazar and Pinto 2014). In relation to housing, to organise the supply
ofhousingsolutions,theHousingandUrbanismMinistryandtheHousingCorpo-
ration(CORVI)andtheUrbanEnhancementCorporation(CORMU)werecreated.
Both institutions had technical functions to assess and define the priorities of the
stateinrelationtourbandevelopment,planninglanduseandhousingallocationand
articulatingpublicbidstoengagewiththeprivatetoconstructtheprojects.Inthis
relationship,theprivaterentswereagreedwiththepublicinstitutionstoensurethe
correctassignmentofthenationalbudgetinurbaninitiatives.Also,thefreemarket
operatedinotherareasofthecitybasedonprivaterealestatedevelopmentaswell,
andthestatefocussedonlytoorganisethesupplyforlow-incomeandmiddle-class
households (Boano and Vergara-Perucich 2017). After neoliberalization, the free-
marketeconomyundertookthewholeurbandevelopmentprocess,andtheleading
roleofthestatewasdismantled(Valencia2007).

Sincethereturntodemocracyin1990,Santiagoasanurbanphenomenonhasexpe-
riencedsignificanttransformationsintheurbandevelopmentprocesses,themarket-
oriented predominance in deciding the future of urban areas, changing lifestyles
of inhabitants, increasing needs of mobility and the new means of social commu-
nication systems. Because of the speed of such changes, the city of Santiago has
becomemorefragmentedanduneven(BrescianiinGreeneetal.2011).Theorigins
of this particular transformation can be found in the dictatorship period when the
urbanplanninginstitutionschangeddrastically(Valencia2008).Thenationalurban
developmentpolicyof1979handedcontroloverurbangrowthtothemarket,which
encouragedcitydevelopmentthatpursuedprofitabilityratherthanthecommongood
(Gross1991).Asanexpectedresultforasubsidiarynation,thesectorsofthecities

while the rest of the city where the market was not interested in intervening was

Thisspatialsegregationincreasedwiththereformofthefinancialmarketthatallowed

cialcapitalandhousingbecamealong-termfixedincomefinancialasset(Cattaneo

tices in the space, living in poor neighbourhoods diminished the quality of life of
the people, generating a seed of indignation that accumulated until it exploded on

cityoftherichwereunabletounderstandsomuchanger.Residentialsegregationin
Santiagois0.58measuredbydissimilarity,higherthanJohannesburg.
Althoughcriticalreflectionsontheneoliberalismandurbanpracticesareabun-
dant(Brenneretal.2011;Fezer2013;Harvey2012;Spencer2016),theimageand
theobjectivesthatmotivateditsimplementationintheurbanrealmarestillopento
discussion.TheneoliberalrevolutionoccurredinChilehasbecomesubjectofabun-
dantscholarshipbutalsohadprovedreaction,rebellionanddissensus.Inthestreets,
andmorevisuallystrikinginPlazaBaquedano,theneoliberalturnwascrackedby
a massive movement of people claiming to overcome neoliberalism and produce
a fairer society, where everyone can live without the feeling that they are being
sacked.Contemporaryplanningandotherpost-positivisticdisciplinesarecaughtin
the wake of multiple overlapping crises that question in essence their theories and
modes of practice. These crises include global climate change, hegemonic neolib-
eralism, growing inequalities, the surge of a fascist and far right extremism; all of
which overlap with the structural complicities of racial capitalism, settler colonial
globalapartheidandtheotherdiverseissuesofglobalisation.InthewakeofTrump’s
electionintheUSA,Roy(2017,np)hadpositedthat‘wemustthinkcriticallyand

power[…]complicitwithcolonialismandimperialism’.Thisurgentcalltoarms‘will
require relinquishing the cherished myths of neutrality and innocence and instead

ence[…]beinginoppositiontostatepowerratherthanseekingitspatronage’(Roy

2017, np). Inspired by radical black scholars, Roy sees a sort of destituent power
thatshecallsadoubleagentasonewhoisembeddedinsystemsofpowerandyet
isabletostagemomentsofrebellionagainstandwithinsuchsystems(Roy2017).
Notareturntothefoundationsofthediscipline—fullycompromisedinthecurrent

alwaysexcludesandoppress.
InPlazaBaquedano,apoliticsasarefusalwasstageda‘politicaldisputesthatfor
Rancière“iswhatevershiftsabodyfromtheplaceassignedtoitorchangesaplace’s
destination”(1999,p30).Politicsdisrupts“waysofdoing,waysofbeing,andways
of saying” (1999, p 29) that are allocated within a dominant, extant order’ (1999,
p 29). Politics then is never an assignation of places, not even the maintenance
or the modification of the rule of law. It is instituting an order in which no one
needstospeakanymorebecausethegovernmentisalreadyrespondingtothenature
of man and therefore all that needs to be done has been put in place. His central
spatialreferenceisareconfigurationofaspace‘whereparties,partsorlackofparts
have been defined… making visible what had no business being seen and makes
heardadiscoursewhereoncetherewasonlyplacefornoise’(Rancière1999,p30)
remain heavily illustrative for a newly conceptualised version of power that is not
static,transformativeandalwayscontested.Oneofthewaysinwhichpoliceavoids
the disturbance of politics is to name phenomena and assign them to their ‘proper
places’ in the established order, thereby de-politicising them (Dikeç 2002, 2005).
InRancière’sapproach,thisisnotaquestionofpolitics;itisaboutalterationsina
police order. Politics, therefore, is not about identifying the ‘excluded’ and trying
toincludethem.Thelogicofidentificationbelongstopolice.Politicsisnot,about
the negotiation of interests by identified groups. Politics proper is to question the
‘given’ order of police that seems to be the ‘natural’ order of things to question
its partitioned spaces and to verify the equality of any speaking being (Rancière
1999). Therefore, genuine political activities always involve forms of innovation
that tear bodies from their assigned places and prevent free speech and right of
expressionfrombeingreducedtomerefunctionality(RancièreandCorcoran2010).
ForQuintana(2020,p4),thisproduces‘arupturewithacorporeality,withawayof
experiencingthebody,whichbringsaboutatransformationofitsposition,thatis,an
inscriptioninanothersensibleuniverse,otherthantheoneassignedtoit’.Thinking
withwhatshecoineda‘politicsofbodies’emancipatorypotentialsandnewrealities
areemerging,manifestingin‘inothereconomiesofaffectiveforces,inotherforms
ofgesturalityandinpracticesofcorporealreflectivitythatalsoproduceanotherway
ofseeingtheworld,ofbeingaffectedbyit,andofjudgingit’(Quintana2020,p4).
ForQuintana,thisisreallythemanifestationofwhatRancièrecallsthepartagedu
sensibleas‘alterationofthewayinwhichbodiestakeonwhattheycan,intheway
theyexperiencetheircapacitiesandincapacities’(Quintana2020,p4).

3.3 PlazaBaquedano:Landmark,Contestation
andOppression
PlazaBaquedanoislocatedinthenorth-easterncornerofthecommuneofSantiago,
constitutinganurbanlandmarklocatedinoneofthenodesofthebeltwayproposedby
theformersuperintendent,BenjamínVicuñaMackennain1875,whoseoriginalname
wasPlazaLaSerenaandlaterPlazaColón(DeRamón2007).In1928,thesculpture
developed by Virgilio Arias was installed in the roundabout, commemorating the
militarychiefManuelBaquedano,herooftheWarofthePacific,whousedtotravel
the sector on horseback. Popularly, the sector is known as Plaza Italia, due to the
factthat,infrontofPlazaBaquedano,thereisamonumenttotheGeniusofLiberty
donated by the Italian Government for the centenary of Chile’s independence in
1910.PlazaItaliaasasector,PlazaBaquedanoasthespecificpointindisputesince
the social outbreak in Chile. With the inauguration of the Santiago Metro, a new
remodellingtookplaceintheearly1980saftertheinstallationoftheunderground
stationcalledBaquedano(Fig.3.1).Thisspacehasbeencataloguedastheneuralgic
point of the capital, for being the point that articulates the eastern sector along
theaxisofAvenidaProvidencia–Apoquindo,wherethemostaffluentsectorofthe
population lives; the southern sector along Avenida Vicuña Mackenna, where the
workersliveandthecommuneofSantiagoCentro,wherethemainservicesofthe
city are located (Fig. 3.2). A more recent proposal was presented in 2016, which
soughttoeliminatetheroundaboutandreplaceitwithawaterfountain(Boschetal.
2015); however, this proposal was later withdrawn by the authority for presenting
socio-economic profitability problems according to the evaluation method used in
Chile.Itisasymbolicspace,too,becauseoftheveryhistorythatthecity’sinhabitants
havebuiltinthisplace,beingthemeetingplaceforcelebrationsandlamentations,

forprotestandmerriment,itisanareaofthecitychargedwithmeanings,thestatue
ofGeneralManuelBaquedanobeingasymbolintension.
Orozco defines this square as an identitarian public space, characterised by the
agglutination of spontaneous as well as massive manifestations of citizen groups
in the face of certain events in national public life,which, lodged inthe collective
consciousness,constitutespartoftheimmaterialheritagewithaspecificspatialrepre-
sentation(Orozco2018),which,beyondthesquare,ismadeupofephemeralevents
thattakeplacethere.TheurbanspaceofSantiago,inthehandsoffinancialcapital
anditsspeculativetendencies(Vergara-PerucichandBoano2020b),iscontestedin
thiscentralspaceofthecity,withastrongsymbolicchargeinaprocessofcitizen
empowerment of reclamation, deliberative, organic, massive, chaotic and antago-
nistic.Urbanpracticesareconstitutedthatturnthespaceintoapoliticalresourcethat
instrumentalisesurbanlifetocallforademocratisationoftheprevailingdemocratic
system (Vergara-Perucich 2019). As Erik Swyngedouw (2011) argues, the dissent
that has disarticulated the link between citizenship and the democratic system is
rearticulatedinpublicspaces,whichmorefrequentlybecomescenesofthepower-
less, who violently and passionately transform the space as if they were a group
of angry and insurgent architects (Swyngedouw 2011). Plaza Baquedano with its
heterotopictransformationssituatesitselfasthenation’smostrelevantpoliticalsite,
and with it, its recent ephemeral alterations register the ups and downs of Chilean
politicsincrisis.

3 UrbanSocietyinTension:TheSantiago’sCommune 45
3.4 TheCommuneofSantiago
Social science experts had no doubts that a social explosion was coming in Chile;
there were many factors behind it, from the collapse of democratic institutions
(Fuentes2019)totheverycausesoftheinequalitygeneratedbytheneoliberalmodel
(Araujo2017).Ontheotherhand,however,theeventshavenotbeeneasytosituate
inaspecifictheoreticalframework.Sincethereisnoagreementontheorganicnature
oftheoutbreak,twoyearsafteritbegan,itisstillnotknownhowsomesurprising
eventshaveemerged,suchasthesimultaneousburningofthecity’sMetrostations,
theorganisationofthedefenceofthesquarebygroupsofpeople,theoverwhelming
massivenessofhundredsofthousandsofpeoplesupportingtheprocessofpopular
contestation and, later, the ratification of this indignation with the triumph of the
‘Apruebo’oftheplebiscitethataskedthecitizenswhethertheywantedanewconsti-
tution(78.28%saidyes)andwhowascommandedtodraftit(79%preferredagroup
ofconstituentselectedsolelyforthispurpose).Certainly,thechangetotheconsti-
tution is a desperate offer (Salazar 2020) from a political class without credit (the
congress only has an 8% popular approval rating), but beyond a new constitution,
itseemsthatthechangeswillnotonlyfocusontheprevailingconstitutionalframe-
workbutonanewcollectiveethos,thescopeofwhichisdifficulttoforesee.Forthe
moment,therepresentationofthechangescouldbefoundinthemodesofoccupa-
tionofspaceinPlazaBaquedanoorPlazadelaDignidad.Thishistoricallypopular
spacewithanimportantimmaterial,heterotopicandsymbolicheritage,haschanged
itsdynamicsofuseinanewurbanformandnewprocessesofspatialproduction.To
framethesechanges,wewilltalkaboutthecommuneofdignity,inclearreference
tothecommuneofParis.
TheParisCommunewasamomentofpopularempowermentinthecityofParis,
between 18 March and 28 May 1871, decreeing the self-management of factories,
thecreationofnurseries,theseparationofthechurchfromdecision-makingbodies,
theuse of places of worship for neighbourhood meetings, the remissionof unpaid
rentsandtheeliminationofinterestondebts,aswellasanorganisationthatsoughtto
solvetheproblemofpoverty.ThePariscommunewasdeliberativeandorganic,faced
strong repression and ultimately failed. From its political and spatial experience,
many lessons can be drawn so that other citizen empowerment organisations with
anarchic overtones do not fail again (Lefebvre 1972), which could be applied to
thePlazaBaquedanoinSantiago,perhapsthecommuneofdignity.Thedifference
betweenthecaseofParisandSantiagoisthatinthelatter,theexperiencewasless
violentandorganic,butalsoinSantiago,thechangemaybepermanentbecausethe
popularuprisingtriggeredthechangeofthepoliticalsystemviaanewconstitutional
acttobeenactedin2022.Thetensionintheurbanspacecouldbeaneffectivepolitical
instrumentforchange.
Strugglesandtensionsinthepublicspaceproducepower.Inthisanalysisforthe
Santiago’scommune,thevoiceofJacquesRancièremayshedsomelightonitspolit-
icalimplications.InRancière’sunderstandingofplanningdiscoursesasconflictive,

### Page 50

46 J.F.Vergara-PerucichandC.Boano
emancipatory,andrevolutionary,thequestionofhowanarchicpoweriskeyforunder-
standingtheChileanoutbreak(Pløger2004;GrangeandGunder2019;Purcell2009,
2014).Rancièreoffersanaccountofpoliticsinrelationtoadominantorderthatis
markedbyitsmembers’possessionofreasonedcapacityofspeech.Hehighlightsthe
problematic aspects in which such dominant frames ‘prevent us from considering
the unforeseeable and incalculable ways in which bodies can reinvent themselves
fromthepositions,roles,andpracticestheyaresubjectedto’(Quintana2020;p2).
In such situations analyses the ‘interruptive force of forms of existence that were
previously insensitive and that, via their demonstration of their reasoned speech,
transform’(Huzar2021;p1)whathecallsthe‘distributionofthesensible’(2010;p
36),makingthemselvesunderstoodasspeakingbeingswhentheypreviouslycould
notbeheardinthisway.Forhim,politicsconsistsintheprocessthattakesawayany
foundation—àrcheDiCesare(2020b)wouldhavesaid—fromanysocialorder.Since
anorderalwaystendstostabiliseitselfandrepresentitselfascomplete,rationaland
absolute, politics shows its conditional and fragile character. In fact, it takes place
in the interruption of an order, its presumed naturalness. As itself is intermittent,
politics is not always there; indeed, it never exists, it does not exist, but is carried
outhereandthereasthedisruptionofaneffectiveorder.Pattonarguesthat‘politics
is commonly thought in term of struggles over power institutions, public reasons
andpublicopinions.BycontrastforRancière,politicsisamatterofconflictsover
theveryexistenceofthisstageaswellasthemake-upstatusoftheperformerswho
are entitled or able to appear’ (2012, p 129). Without a terrain, a surface and an
objectwhetherintheformofthestate,thegovernment,orthelaw,politicsisalso
deprivedofasubjectwhetherintheformofpeople,nationorclasswhocanguide
anemancipatoryprocess.
Inthisinterpretationofpolitics,equalitygivesrisetopoliticsinauniquewayas
itbecomesacontinuouspolemicalexpressionofit,neverfixedandassumedevenif
presupposedinanysocialorder.ForRancière,properorderisalwaysbeinterrupted
byimproprietywhich,servedtosetthestageforhisprovocativeconceptionofpolitics
and his constant and insistent defence of democracy as dissensus, as scandalous
(Rancière 2010, 2017). For Rancière ‘political activity is whatever shifts a body
fromtheplaceassignedtoitorchangesaplace’sdestination’(1999,p30).Politics
disrupts‘waysofdoing,waysofbeing,andwaysofsaying(p29)thatareallocated
withinadominant,extantorder’(ibid).Politicsthenisneveranassignationofplaces,
noteventhemaintenanceorthemodificationoftheruleoflaw.Itisinstitutingan
order in which no one needs to speak anymore because the government is already
responding to the nature of man, and therefore, all that needs to be done has been
putinplace.ThisconditionsisoneofthemainoutcomesoftheChileancaseunder
study.
Genuine political activities always involve forms of innovation that tear bodies
fromtheirassignedplacesandpreventfreespeechandrightofexpressionfrombeing
reducedtomerefunctionality(RancièreandCorcoran2010).ForQuintana(2020;
p 4), this produces ‘a rupture with a corporeality, with a way of experiencing the
body, which brings about a transformation of its position, that is, an inscription in
anothersensibleuniverse,otherthantheoneassignedtoit’.Thinkingwithwhatshe

### Page 51

3 UrbanSocietyinTension:TheSantiago’sCommune 47
coineda‘politicsofbodies’emancipatorypotentialsandnewrealitiesareemerging,
manifestingin‘inothereconomiesofaffectiveforces,inotherformsofgesturality
andinpracticesofcorporealreflectivitythatalsoproduceanotherwayofseeingthe
world, of being affected by it, and of judging it’ (p 4). Dissensus then introduces
newsubjectsandheterogeneousobjectsintothefieldsofperception.Suchemergent
distributionsetsthedivisionsbetween whatisvisibleandinvisible,speakable and
unspeakable, in Rancière’s words—audible and inaudible. It determines what can
be thought, made or done (Porter 2007), that ‘define[s] the “modes of perception”
thatmakethatordervisibleandsayableinthefirstplace[…]isthecuttingupofthe
wordandofword’(Rancière2001;p20).YetwhatRancièreoffersultimatelyisa
powerofmoments,ratherthanapoliticsofmovements.
TheCommunadeSantiagostartedwiththepolitizationofbodies.Thousandsof
mutilatedandtortsarepartoftheconsequencesoftheconfrontationbetweentheold
orderdefendedbythepoliceandthedestituentpowerfrompushingforbigchanges.
Thelattertriumphedagainstoppressionanddissensusasapoliticalmanufacturing
processofnewordersalaRancièremaybematerialisedsooninSantiago.
3.5 TowardsaDestituentPower
Destituentpowerisnot,anti-political,orproto-politicalorevenpost-political,rather,
takesusoutsidethetraditionaltrajectoriesoftheconceptualizationofmodernpolitics
outsidethemodernconstructofthecoincidenceofpoliticsandpowerandtherefore
betweenpoliticsandstate.Destituentisapoliticsbeyondthequestionofpower,or
rather not founded by power on power. It is a politics that ‘it follows the opposite
path, moving from the edges, breaking barriers, escaping the police function, and
redeemsitsownname[…]itsharesthewrong,manifestsdissent,andshineslights
ontheinvisibleandtheunwelcomed.Italsotakespartofthosewhodonothaveparts,
deniesthedistribution,showsthecontingencyoforder,breaksthepolicehierarchy
of the archè, which wants the monopoly of the beginning, which claims to have
establishedthecommand’(DiCesare2020;p23).
Destituent was used for the first time by the Colectivo Situactiones in Buenos
Aires to describe the original features of the Argentine piqueteros movement of
2001 capable of bringing about a real change in Argentina by delegitimizing the
existingpoliticalforces(Laudani2016)veryattunedtotheRancière’spartage.More
recently, the concept has found in Giorgio Agamben a figure who expresses the
fullforceofitspoliticalmeaning.InhislastinstalmentoftheHomoSacerproject,
The Use of the Bodies (2016) where he suggests that a destituent power is one
that“deactivatesomethingandrenderitinoperative—apower[…]withoutsimply
destroyingitbutbyliberatingthepotentialsthathaveremainedinactiveinitinorder
toallowadifferentuseofthem”(p.273)andthat‘whileremainingheterogeneous
to the system, had the capacity to render decisions destitute and suspend them’
(p.274).Destituentpower isconfigured asawayofpractisingandthinkingabout
politicsthatradicallybreakswiththemodernlogicofsovereignty.Consequently,it

### Page 52

48 J.F.Vergara-PerucichandC.Boano
canbeseenasaradicalalternativetoconstituentpowerinatimethatisnotthatof
controlandsovereignty,butthatoftheimmanentpermanence,albeitinapotential
form, of multiple and plural instances of liberation that do not find a solution in
stateinstitutionsandsomehowremaininécartswithrespecttodominantforms.As
the Fig. 3.3 illustrates, the changes on Plaza Baquedano after almost two years of
contestation changed its social meaning, and now the name for the most people is
PlazaDignidad(Fig.3.3).
Thisdislocatespoliticsinaterritorywithoutfoundationsandservestoestablish
aninitialqualifyingpointofdestituentpower—onethat,asnotedearlier,doesnot
disregard the myriad subjectivities that reject political power. In other words, they
do not simply position themselves against power, but at a distance from it: they
dodgeit.Itisclearthatinacertaintypeofdemonstrationandstruggleoverthelast
twenty years, that is, from the Los Angeles riots to the ‘Arab spring’, which have
in common the desire to avoid any direct conflict with the established power, the
dynamicofrefusaliscrucial,ofabsence,ofthenon-negativeforceofthenegative,
of the affirmative dimension of destruction, as a critique of everything that exists,
according to that link between nihilism and politics. Destituent subjectivities are
linked to an idea of politics without arché, that is, which feeds a conception of
democracyasexcess:democracyisalwaysademocraticexcess;whateverydemo-
cratic regime today intends to suffocate in the name of democracy), are a people.
They are the (im) popular, unexpected, uncounted, event of the people. An unex-
pected,unforeseenpeople,withoutconscience(thereisnoproject),withoutheador
tail, which emerges unexpectedly in the struggle. Only a power that aggregates in
thiswaycanbeademocraticpowerthatescapesitsdemocraticcapture(democracy
isneveramatterofnumbers).Adestituentpoliticshasalimitedbutprecisetask:to
createtheconditions,thatis,thevacuum,sothatanotherpolitics,theonethattoday
seemsimpossible,canhappen.Itindicatesthefirstmovementtobemade:tounleash
apoliticsoftheevent,theeventofpoliticsnestsinasingulardesertionfromwhat
is,tobreakthenormalcourseofhistoryandproduceamultiple,ecstatic,plurality.
Destituentpoliticsconsists,rather,intheprocessthatremovesthefoundationfrom
a social order. And since an order always tends to stabilise itself and to represent
itself as complete, rational and absolute, politics shows its conditional and fragile
character. It takes place, in fact, in the interruption of an order, of its presumed
naturalness:intermittentitself,politicsisnotalwaysthere,indeeditneveris,itdoes
notexist,butittakesplacehereandthereasadisruptionofaneffectiveorder.
3.6 Conclusion
Plaza Baquedano was a symbol of the Chilean neoliberal swamp trapped in the
promise of development that only trickled to high-income households but n-ever
learnedhowtodistribute.ThisplazasawhowtheneoliberalethosofChileansociety,
characterisedbyastrongindividualism,turnedagainstneoliberalismitself,claiming
for its overcoming in an uncoordinated way in terms of demands but coordinated

Chapter 4
Latent Tensions and Urban Change
in Two Milan Neighbourhoods
CarolinaPacchi
Abstract Latent contentious issues, which significantly characterise urban neigh-
bourhoods, are frequently present and underway for many years in cities, even if
they donotemerge asopen orexplicit conflicts. They contribute toshaping urban
transformationatdifferentscalesandworkasmoreorlessvisiblelinesoftension,
movingbetweenspaceandsocialpractices.Usingtheurbantensionframeworkto
analyserecenttransformationsmeansbeingabletodetectsuchlinesofcontention
and possible fractures even when they do not emerge as visible conflicts, an ever
morecommonconditioninpost-politicalurbansettings,inwhichhorizontalgover-
nancepracticesoftencontributetoremoveconflictandcontentiousissuesfrompublic
discourse.
· · ·
Keywords Grassrootactivism Socialstigmatization Tacticalurbanism Social
housing
4.1 Introduction
Latent contentious issues, which significantly characterise urban neighbourhoods,
are frequently present and underway for many years in cities, even if they do not
emergeasopenorexplicitconflicts(Mössner,DelRomero2015).Theycontribute
toshapingurbantransformationatdifferentscalesandworkasmoreorlessvisible
linesoftension,movingbetweenspaceandsocialpractices.Usingtheurbantension
frameworktoanalyserecenttransformationsmeansbeingabletodetectsuchlinesof
contentionandpossiblefracturesevenwhentheydonotemergeasvisibleconflicts,
anevermorecommonconditioninpost-politicalurbansettings,inwhichhorizontal
governancepracticesoftencontributetoremoveconflictandcontentiousissuesfrom
publicdiscourse(Swyngedouw2018).

Thechapterconcernstheinterpretationoflatenturbantensionsintwocomplex
and multi-layered peripheral neighbourhoods in Milan, Corvetto and via Padova,
characterisedinonecasebylargesocialhousingcompounds,andinbothcasesby
changingpopulations,formsofmarginalityanddisadvantage,butalsobyverylively
and active local communities and forms of grassroots activism. Moreover, in the
lastfewyears,bothareas,evenifwithdifferentintensity,havebeentheobjectofa
numberofurbanregenerationpoliciesandinnovationprogrammespromotedbythe
MilanMunicipalityandotherlocalorcitylevelstakeholders.
Impingingonfieldresearchdevelopedalsointheframeworkofacourseonurban
conflicts,1 thepaperwillcriticallymentionthreeemergingdimensions,discussing
moreinparticularpracticestotacklehousingaccessibilityontheonehandandthe
roleoftacticalurbanismontheother.
The underlying research question revolves around the role of latent tensions in
shaping recent developments in the two areas, looking in particular at three rele-
vantdimensions,locatedatthecrossroadsbetweenphysicalinterventionandsocial
activism; the first issue concerns the process of social stigmatisation that charac-
terisesbothareas,andthewaysinwhichthisinfluencespublicdiscourseandpolicy
making, but also self-recognition on the part of local inhabitants; the second one
looks at informal ways to tackle underlying and deep-seated problems in housing
accessibility for large segments of the urban population, such as squatting prac-
tices,inparticularinsocialhousing,andformsofirregularorblackmarkethousing,
in private housing; the third one focuses on the role of tactical urbanism projects
implementedinlocalpublicspace,andtheimpacttheyhadontheuseofspaceby
inhabitants.
Exploringtheroleoflatenttensionsinshapingcontemporaryurbandevelopments
and transformations means first of all taking into account how underground and
scarcely visible urban phenomena may play crucial roles and exert a long-lasting
influence. To try and answer this question, we will explore the two neighbour-
hoods,takingintoaccountthethreementioneddimensions,locatedatthecrossroads
betweenphysicalinterventionandsocialactivism.Indoingso,thechapterwilltry
todecodetheunderlyingcontentionandurbantension,whilelookingatthepeculiar
nexus between the physical organisation of space and the role of social agency at
locallevel.
Theprocessofsocialstigmatisationisacomplexcollectiveprocessofdifferenti-
ationandrecognition,originallyidentifiedasaffectingmostlyindividualsorgroups
(Goffmann 1963; Link and Phelan 2001), which has been more recently analysed
also in the spatial perspective, as attached to the territory (Wacquant 2007). The
effects of cognitive processes leading to attaching specific stigmata and to stereo-
typingneighbourhoodscanhavesignificanteffectbothinternally(withinhabitants
detaching themselves from their neighbourhoods, retreating to private and family

onurbanconflicts,inaninternationalMasterinUrbanPlanningandPolicyDesign(Politecnico
diMilano).Internationalstudentswithdifferenteducationalbackgroundshavebeenworkingin
groupsonMilan,lookinginparticularatemergingtensionsandlatentconflictsintheCorvettoand

life,ortryingtoescapeassoonastheycan),andexternally(bynegativelyorienting
