Insuchneighbourhoods,latenttensionsbecomeintensifiedandsomewhatmagni-
fied, thus making other lines of tension, in turn, more problematic and difficult to
tackleviapublicpolicies(Hamdietal.2019).
Wewillnowdiscussmorein-depthtwoareasoftensions,oneconcerningdiffi-
cultiesandlocalisedformsofdisadvantagewithregardtohousingpractices,andone
exampleoftensionsrelatedtotheimplementationofatacticalurbanismproject.
ThehousingissuehasbeenforsomeyearsamajorthemeinMilan,whichhasnot
beenfullytakenonboardbymunicipalpolicies,alsoduetoitsundeniablecomplexity,
andwhichisstrictlylinkedtothegrowinginequalitiesthatcharacterisethecity,with
significantaccelerationsduetothe2008economiccrisis(CuccaandRanci2017),
the explosion of short-term lets and the shifting role of the city as a major tourist
destination (Amore et al. 2020), and has been made even more stringent with the
mostrecentCovid-19pandemiccrisis(Tralockdownesmartworking2021).
Since the topic is too complex and vast to be dealt with in a short paper, we
will focus exclusively on the urban tensions linked to the accessibility issue, and
emergingfromthetwocases.Aswewillsee,sinceneithermarket,norpublicpolicies
are fully able to deal with vast (and differentiated) areas of housing disadvantage,
manysocialgroupsresorttomakeshiftadjustments,workingingreyandblackareas
of the market, through informal and in some cases outright illegal arrangements
(PacchiandSartori,2015).WhiletheCorvettoneighbourhood,intheSouth-Eastof
thecity,ischaracterisedbyavastsupplyofpublichousing,insomecasesdatingback
to the pre-war period, in other cases to the 1960s, that amounts overall to several
thousand units, in the via Padova area, in the North-East of the city, the housing
stockispredominantlyprivate,withsomesmallexceptions,thatdonotsignificantly
influencetheoverallstructureofpopulationdistribution.
Ontheotherhand,tacticalurbanismexperiences,althoughoriginallydeveloped
byalternativedesignerscollectivesandorganisations(Catalanotti2020)havegained
prominenceinrecentyearsinEuropeancities,asameansforLocalAuthoritiesto
quicklyandcheaplytestinnovationsandpublicspacetransformations,andforactive
citizenstochannelideasforurbantransformationsthrough(Silva2016).Inthecase
we’llbelookingat,though,we’llseethatatacticalurbanisminterventionimposed
top-down from the city council in a disadvantaged public housing neighbourhood
hascausedformsoflocaloppositionandtension.
4.2 EmergingTensionsandGrassrootsActivism
inCorvettoandViaPadova
IntheCorvettocase,thereisalonghistoryofoccupationsandsquattinginvacant
unitsinthedifferentsocialhousingcompounds(Alvarezetal.2019).Ingeneral,it
ispossibletoidentifydifferentmodalities,fromsheerneed-drivenoccupations(by

### Page 60

56 C.Pacchi
families,migrants,etc.)tooccupationsorganisedandmanagedbyracketsandforms
of organised crime (structured locally or at other scales), and, finally, politically
oriented forms of squatting. These last ones are usually twofold experiences: they
aim at the same time to provide a shelter for people in need, and to raise a voice,
usingthisillegalmeansofaccessingthehousingstocktohighlightandgiveasignal
tothecitygovernmentthattheproblemisserious,andlargelyunresolved.
InthecaseofviaPadova,thesametensionemergessomewhatdifferently,inthat
thelargeprevalenceofprivatehousingentailsadifferentrangeofphenomenainthe
areasofinformalandillegalhousing,namelyasgreyorblackhousingmarkets:inthis
neighbourhood,asamatteroffact,manyinhabitantsdonothaveanyotherchoice
but to resort to informality, which in turn ma means quite different arrangements
(Azunre et al. 2020). Thus, black market can in general be defined as “unofficial,
informalcontracts,whichviolatelegalregulationsandthereforeremaininanextra-
legal, unprotected sphere, typically to the detriment of the tenant” (Bargelli and
Bianchi 2017: 1–2), and it manifests itself with a range of diverse situations and
arrangements.
It is possible to highlight three recurring situations: one in which the housing
conditions are comparable to the usual ones, but the contract is not registered, to
avoidtaxationforthelandlord;thisisahighlycommonanddiffusedarrangement
(it concerns around 900.000 housing contracts in Italy every year), and it is not
necessarilyconcentratedinmoredisadvantagedneighbourhoods(Bargelli,Bianchi,
cit).
Inthesecondtypicalsituation,thereisnoformalcontract,theapartmentisleftto
atenant,whichallowsinalargegroupofpeople,resultinginovercrowdedspaces.
Finally,thethirdtypicalsituationisthatofapersonrentingaflat(withorwithout
a formal contract) and then subletting it to a high number of people illegally, in
some cases even in shifts(different people use the same beds indifferent times of
the day). Such somewhat extreme housing situations merge with a high variety of
intermediatesituations,whichconjureacomplexgreyandblackmarketinthewhole
viaPadovaarea.Suchhousingprecariousnessandinstability,initsvariedcasesin
point, concerns a significant amount of the population of the area, and it concerns
disproportionatelymigrantindividualsandfamilies,addinguptoalreadycomplex
lifecourses.
Suchsituationsseldomemergeintheopendebate,alsoduetotheextremeprecar-
iousnessandinstabilityofthelifeconditionsofthoseconcerned,tornbetweenillegal
presence in the country (or extreme difficulties in obtaining permanent resident
status),anuncertainjobmarket,andtheharshhousingconditions.Thiscontributesto
createundergroundtensions,thatusuallynevercometothesurface,butaddsignif-
icantly to the complexity of life in such areas, and add some ambiguity to public
discoursesandpolicies.
The second line of tension that it is possible to identify concerns the use of
public space and its transformation though the experimentation of non-permanent
andreversibletransformativesolutions,suchasthoseinthetacticalurbanismdomain
(Mould 2014; Lydon and Garcia 2015); in both neighbourhoods there have been

### Page 61

4 LatentTensionsandUrbanChangeinTwoMilanNeighbourhoods 57
tacticaltransformationsofpublicopenspacewithminimalandreversibleinterven-
tions,suchastheoneinPiazzaAngilbertoIIinCorvetto(Chavezetal.2019),and
the ones concerning via Giacosa and via Spoleto in Nolo, near via Padova. The
one in Piazza Angilberto II (Fig. 4.1), originally implemented at the end of 2018,
andamongthefirstinterventionsofthisnatureinMilan,hasbeenmetwithrefusal
andoppositionbylocalinhabitants,thatrequestedmorestructuralandrobustpublic

4.3 TensionsandUrbanTransformation
In order to identify and start to disentangle forces shaping urban transformation,
somepotentiallypromisinglinesofreasoningemergefromtheempiricalresearchin
thetwoneighbourhoods.Takingintoaccountallthesignificantlimitationsduetothe
factthatpartoftheempiricalresearchhasbeenconductedthroughstudentexercise,
itappearsneverthelessthatsomerecurringelementsshouldmorepromptlybetaken
intoaccount.
Thefirstemergingissueisthecontinuousinteractionbetweenmicro,hyper-local
issues and supra-local ones, which has proved to be an important dimension in
manyresearchesaboutlocalcontention,participationandcivicengagementinurban
policies(Silveretal.2010;Pacchi2020).Asthedifferentcasesshow,localtensions
arefrequentlythe(moreorlessvisible)manifestationoffaultlines,whicharemore
generalandmorestructuralthantheindividualcontentiouscaseitself.
Thisappearsasextremelyclearinthehousingcase,sincehousingitselfisamajor
issueinMilan,thathasseldomfoundformsofpoliticalrepresentationinrecentyears
(differentlyfromwhathappenedinthepastdecades)andtheindividualepisodesof
disadvantageandprecarioushousingconditions,aswellasthedifficulties,obstacles
anddailychallengesthatthousandsofinhabitantsfacedaily,haveseriousdifficulties
inbecomingacollectivevoice(PacchiandSartori2015).
In the case of the opposition to tactical urbanism, the elements that emerge are
somewhatnuancedandambiguous:whileinadisadvantaged,socialhousingneigh-
bourhoodatop-downtacticalurbanisminterventionismetwithsuspicionandscep-
ticism by disappointed and disheartened local inhabitants, in a more mixed neigh-
bourhoodtacticalurbanismisseen,onthecontrary,asanopportunityandapotential
resourceinthehandsofcitizenstopushtheiragendathrough.
Secondly,allcasesshowtheimportanceofreadingspatialandsocialagencyissues
atthesametime.Itwouldnotbepossibletofullygrasptensionsonstigmatisation,
informalandillegalhousingarrangementsorontheuseofpublicspace,withoutfully
takingintoaccounttheactualspatialandphysicalconditionsinwhichtheyemerge.
Lookingatthecases,thediffusedpresenceofdegradedpublichousingstockinthe
onecase,thediversedailylifepracticesofurbanpopulationsnegotiatingtheiruseof
space,thedynamicsofhousingneedandsquatting,theformsoflocalprotestsand
grassroots organisations are strictly connected in complex socio-spatial settings in
whichunderlyingtensionsacquiremeaning.

### Page 64

60 C.Pacchi
Finally,whatclearlyemergesfromthecasesisthatinthefaceoffaultlinesand
structuraldifficulties,suchasthemanifestationofsocialandspatialinequalities,it
is extremely ineffective to experiment design and policy shortcuts to temporarily
mendissuesandquestionsthatexpresscomplexityandpoliticalsignificancemore
deeply than the policy or design answer itself. The difficulties in accepting top-
downandnon-permanentinterventionsinneighbourhoods,particularlywhensuch
interventionisnottheoutcomeofextensiveandlocallyrootedactivismandengage-
ment, lies in the fear that such an approach may end up simplifying (and, to a
certain extent, trivialising) the deep-seated and manifold problems characterising
eachneighbourhood.

Chapter 5
Fear and Segregation: Anxiety Beyond
Gated Communities. The Costa Rican
Case
KarlaBarrantesChaves
Abstract Spatial segregation is one of the main consequences of building gated
communities.Theyaretypicallywalledorfenced,withprivatesecurityandsurveil-
lancedevices.GatedcommunitieshavebeenspreadingrapidlyinLatinAmericaas
theyareseenas‘shelters’againstcrime;paradoxically,theymightbecatalysingthe
fear of crime towards the neighbouring public spaces, fuelling a vicious circle of
fear.Thewaytheyarefortifiedrepresentsmorethanaphysicalbarrier;itcontributes
torisingtensionsbetweenthoseondifferentsidesofthewall.Thisessayexplores
the perception of non-gated residents from eight case studies with different levels
ofpoverty withintheGreater Metropolitan Areaof CostaRica.Thoseviews were
gatheredmainlythroughwalkstrackedwithaGPSandfocusgroups;thequalitative
datafromthewalkswerecodifiedandvisualisedthrough‘Talk’strackmaps’.The
essayaddresseshowgatedcommunities’edgesexacerbatetheexclusionfeelingsin
adjacentneighbourhoodsandhowthosereactionsarelinkedtofearofcrime.
· · ·
Keywords Fear-crime Gatedcommunities Residentialsegregation CostaRica
5.1 Introduction
Spatialsegregationisoneofthemainconsequencesofbuildinggatedcommunities
(BlakelyandSnyder1997;Caldeira2000;Low2004;Roitman2010).However,their
physicalfeaturesmightalsocontributetorisingtensionsbetweenthoseondifferent
sides of the wall. Caldeira (2000) states that the closeness between gated commu-
nities and marginalised groups in Latin America has provoked ‘fortified enclaves’
promotingtheseparatenessinsteadofintegration.Thisessayexploreshowelements
of gated communities’ edges might exacerbate the exclusion feelings already in
placebyresidentialsegregation,andhowthosereactionsarelinkedtofearofcrime
inneighbouringcommunities.
B
K.BarrantesChaves( )
UniversityCollegeLondon(UCL),London,UK
e-mail:karla.chaves.16@ucl.ac.uk
©TheAuthor(s),underexclusivelicensetoSpringerNatureSwitzerlandAG2021 63
I.Vassalloetal.(eds.),SpatialTensionsinUrbanDesign,TheUrbanBookSeries,
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-030-84083-9_5

### Page 67

64 K.BarrantesChaves
Gated communities as residential developments arose in the USA in the early
1980s(BlakelyandSnyder1997)andLatinAmericancitiesinthelate1990s(Castells
1999;Coy2006).TheemergenceofgatedcommunitiesinLatinAmericaresultsfrom
several factors; Coy (2006) attributes both external and internal driving forces. As
externalreasons,Coymentionsstructuralconditionssuchasneoliberalism,globali-
sationandprivatisation,whileinternalforcesmightbestatus,lifestyleandsecurity.
In this regard, fear of crime might be one of the most important reasons for the
tremendoussuccessofgatedhousingareas.
SafetyisanessentialconcerninmostofLatinAmericanpeople.Thisregionholds
thehighestratesofhomicidesworldwide(Chioda2016).Thefeardiscoursehasalso
beenpartofthepoliticalrhetoricfromthe1990s,fuellingpeopleanxietyevenmore
(Huhn2017).Inthiscontext,gatedcommunitieshavebeenseenas‘shelters’against
criminality; Caldeira (2000) holds that those walled enclaves and the increase of
privatesecurityarelegitimisedbyfeardiscoursesthatperpetuatestereotypesabout
undesirablepeople.Tensionsbasedondistrusttowardstheimmediateenvironment
might provoke the same feeling in turn, this time coming from the communities
outsidethewall.
CentralAmericaisoneofthemostviolentareasintheworld;however,itisnot
homogeneousintheirfigures.CountriessuchasCostaRica,PanamáandNicaragua
havelowerratesofhomicidesthantherest.Althoughtheoverallvictimisationrate
inCostaRicahasdiminishedfrom2008(INEC2015),gatedcommunitieshavebeen
proliferating. This trend has started to fragment the city, physically and socially,
creating isolated places into the urban fabric. This essay’s argumentation is based
on data collected through eight case studies within the Greater Metropolitan Area
of Costa Rica. The findings suggest a deep residential segregation and exclusion
feelings in most open neighbourhoods beside gated communities. The outcomes
show pre-existing fears fuelled by isolated landscapes and uncertainty about who
livesbehindthosewalls.
5.2 FearofCrimeandBuiltEnvironment
Fearofcrimehasprofoundimpactsonqualityoflife;itaffectssocialcohesionand
theexperienceofthecity.ThisfeelingisdefinedbyFerraroandLaGrange(1987:72)
as a ‘negative emotional reaction to crime or the symbols associated with crime’.
Fearofcrimeisacomplexphenomenon,whichinvolvesdifferentdimensionsand
factors such as incomes level, gender, education, ethnicity, age and attachment to
theneighbourhood(Grayetal.2011).Someofthosefactorsarealsoaffectedbythe
builtenvironmentandurbandesigndecisions.
Someauthorshavesuggestedthemanipulationofphysicalspacetopreventcrime.
Newman(1973)throughhisconcept‘DefensibleSpaces’,pointsoutthatbuildings’
configuration and urban design can help to surveillance. In his view, the commu-
nity becomes a guardian encouraging territoriality feelings. Besides, the Situation
PreventionThesis(Clarke1983;Crawford1998)supportstheideaofreducingthe

### Page 68

5 FearandSegregation:AnxietyBeyondGatedCommunities… 65
opportunitiesforcrimethroughinformalcommunityvigilance,theintroductionof
barriers,anduseofClosed-circuittelevision(CCTV)andphysicalalterations.Inboth
cases,thepresenceofstrangersisavoided,andneighbourhoodsareundercommunity
control.
However, those guardianship’s principles affect the permeability of the city.
Minton(2009)holdsthatthe‘DefensibleSpaces’arethereasonwhygatedcommu-
nitiesandculdesachavebeenspreadacrosstheUSAandBritainsince1970.She
arguesthatthoseviewsencouragetheideaofstrangersasasourceofdanger;there-
fore, the tension between the absence or presence of unfamiliar people affects the
dynamismofthecity.Jacobs(1961)highlightstheimportanceofbringingdifferent
typesofpeoplealtogether.Shepointsoutthatasuccessfulcityiswherepeoplefeel
safeamongstrangers.Sennett(2018:126)holdsthatpreconceptionsaboutstrangers
just because ‘they are incomprehensibly strange, degrades the ethical character of
thecity’.Hehighlightsthatlivingwithinadiversegrouphasthepowerofreducing
the feeling of insecurity and frustration because there is no clear image of who is
theenemy.Sennettsuggeststhatturningwallsinmembranesallowstheinterchange
inside-outside,stimulatingthecasualmixamongresidentsandmakingcontactless
confrontational.
5.3 WallsandSegregation
ThegrowthofgatedcommunitiesinLatinAmericamightbeexplainedasananswer
tosocialconflictandviolenceincities;however,thesedevelopmentsalsoreflectnew
lifestylesemergingundertheglobalisationprocess(CoyandPöhler2002).Underthis
perspective,developersareseenasprovidersofbothsafetyandfamiliarity(Blakely
andSnyder1997).
InLatinAmerica,safetypoliciesinresidentialareashaveincreaseddifferentia-
tion barriers; paradoxically, rising the segregation and creating exclusion’s spaces
(Carrion2008).Thissituationisalsopresentinthesuburbs,wherepoorandrichare
nexttoeachotherbutseparatedbyawall(RoitmanandPhelps2011).CostaRicahas
hadasignificantincreaseinthenumberofgatedcommunities.Mostofthemhave
beencreatedunderthecondominiumscheme,anownershipsystemwithindividual
dwellingswithinasharedlandwithcommonareas.From1990to2017,theriseof
thebuiltareaunderthiscategoryincreasedfrom5.2to25.2%(ProgramaEstadode
laNación2018).Althoughthisfiguredoesnotmeanthatallthosedevelopmentsare
walled, it represents the current trend. A report from the United Nations Develop-
mentProgramme(UNDP)highlightsthatthewidespreadofunsafetyfeelingshave
alteredpeoplebehaviourinCostaRica‘Theyliveinacondominiumratherthanopen
spaces,putrazorwirearoundwhatwasonceanopengarden’.(PNUD2005:4).
In Costa Rica, gated communities are mostly concentrated in the urban region;
Pujoletal.(2011)suggestthatwithinGreaterMetropolitanArea,socialsegregation
hasclimbedsteadilyinplaceswheregatedcommunitiesarelocated.Theplanning
systemhasdifferentrulesforopenresidentialdevelopmentsandgatedcommunities.

### Page 69

66 K.BarrantesChaves
Openneighbourhoodssupplylandforpublicparksandstreets,whilegatedcommu-
nitiescankeeptheentirepropertyforthemselveswithoutprovidinganypublicspace.
Althoughlocalgovernmentscouldincluderulesintheirmunicipalplanningcodes
torevertthatsituation,theirresponsehasbeenslow;furthermore,thenationalplan-
ningsystemhasmanydrawbackstoadaptchangesquickly(Barrantes-Chaves2019).
Thissituationreducesthenumberofopenspacesperinhabitantandareasforsharing
withinthecity.
Many traditional open neighbourhoods have started to be surrounded by gated
communities. Those neighbourhoods are very diverse; they may be either low or
highincomes.Manyauthorshavesuggestedapositiveassociationbetweenfearof
crime and income inequalities (Franklin et al. 2008; Wilkinson and Pickett 2009;
Vieno et al. 2013). The case studies selected for this research combine different
typesofpovertylevels.
5.4 RecordingtheSpatialTensions
Theurbantensionsarenotalwaysevident,makingchallengingtheirresearch.Fear
ofcrimeisamulti-factorialphenomenonandspatialtensionsmightexacerbatethat
feeling,butnotnecessarilyinthesameintensitypercommunity.Thestudyisbased
onneighbourhoodsfromdifferentpoverty’srangeswithintheGreaterMetropolitan
AreaofCostaRica;theeightcasestudiesareneighbourhoodsnexttogatedcommu-
nities.Theywereselectedrandomlyaccordingtotheindicatoroftheunfulfilledneeds
(NBI).1 TheNBIisapovertymeasurefromtheCostaRicacensus.Itencompasses
fourelements:housing,educationallevel,healthandaccesstogoodsandservices;
when a dwelling holds 4 NBI it means the highest level of poverty, while 0 NBI
representsnopoverty.
Thedatacollectionwascarriedoutthroughwalkinginterviews,focusgroups,in-
depthinterviewsandobservations.Mobileresearchmethods,wheretheresearcher
walks along with participants in their familiar environments have been increasing
lately(EvansandJones2011);Carpiano(2009)calledthistypeofmethodas‘go-
along’.InCostaRica,UN-Habitatintroducedthe‘Safetyexploratorywalks’(ONU-
Habitat2013)toexplorepeople’sperceptionsaboutfearofcrime.Theydefinedthem
asshorturbanroutes,previouslydefinedandcarriedoutbythecommunity,inwhich
participantsexpresstheirthoughtsabouttheircommunity’ssafety.
Evans and Jones (2011) suggest using Geographic Information Systems (GIS)
totrackandvisualisethewalkinginterviews.Inthisstudy,thewalksweretracked
withaGlobalPositioningSystem(GPS)andsynchronisedwithanaudiorecording.
Besides,eachparticipantwasprovidedwithindividualsmapswithstickerstoexplore
theintensityoffear.
1TheunfulfilledneedsknownasNBIbyitsacronyminSpanish[NecesidadesBásicasInsatisfechas]
is a Costa Ricanpoverty indicator elaborated by the National Institute of Statisticsand Census

5.5 TheOutside:TensionsBeyondtheGate
5.5.1 ResidentialSegregation
Residential segregation was found in seven of the eight cases. People from non-
gatedneighbourhoodsdonothavecommunitytieswithgatedresidentsnearby.They
believethatgatedresidentsdonotcareabouttheirwelfareorcommunity,exceptfor
thewealthiestneighbourhood(0NBI).Inthiscase,peoplefromthegatedcommu-
nitiesnearbywerepartofthecommunityorganisationandcontributedactivelyand
economicallytotheneighbourhood’saffairs.Thiswastheonlycasewithapermeable
barrierbetweengatedandnon-gatedcommunities.
Teenagersfromcommunitieswithinthehighestandmiddlerangesofpovertywere
reached through focus groups. It was found that they reproduced the same spatial
segregationpatternsthanadults.Despitehavinganextensivenetworkoffriendships
beyond their own neighbourhood, teenagers manifested not having friends within
thegatedcommunitiesnearby.
The edges between gated communities and open neighbourhoods were mostly
walls.Asmentionedabove,theonlyexceptionwasthewealthiestneighbourhood;
inthatcase,theperimeterwaspermeable(railings)butkeepingaccessrestrictions.
Duringthewalkinginterviews,thegatededges(mostlywalls)wereassociatedwith
feelingsoffear.Theseweredescribedasisolatedanddarkplaces;facilitatingcrimes

### Page 72

5 FearandSegregation:AnxietyBeyondGatedCommunities… 69
such as muggings, drugs’ traffic, abductions or sexual assaults. Neighbours high-
lightedthelargesizeofthosegateddevelopments,manyofthemweremoreexten-
sivethanninehectares–sixoftheeightcases.Thissituationforcedpeopletowalk
longdistancesalongtheedgesfortheirdailyroutinessuchastakingthebusortaking
theirchildrentothelocalschool.Infact,manybusstopswerelocatedjustnexttothe
wall;thosebusstopswereconsidereddangerousbytheparticipantsduetoisolation
andlackofnaturalsurveillance,especiallyduringthenight.
5.5.2 WallsandPreconceptions
As a result of residential segregation, non-gated residents have created their own
notion about who lives in gated communities. They express preconceptions about
gated residents; for instance, participants believe gated neighbours do not identify
themselveswiththecommunityneedsbecausetheymaycomefromanotherplaceout
ofthetown.Theincomelevelofthosepeoplealsocausesconcernamongneighbours;
for instance, many participants think gated residents are wealthy people, which is
an issue for their neighbourhood because gated communities might be a target for
criminality.Someparticipantsbelievethatitmightbedifficultforoffenderstoenter
intothegateddevelopmentduetosophisticatesafetysystems;therefore,criminals
couldchoosetocommitcrimesintheirneighbourhoodinstead.
People mainly from the middle and the highest poverty ranges mentioned that
closedcondominiumresidentscouldbeinvolvedinillegalbusinesses,puttingtheir
neighbourhoodsunderthreat.Thissituationwashighlyworryinginneighboursfrom
thecommunityinthehighestpoverty,whofeltanxiousaboutthesecriminalnetworks
astheybelievetheiryoungpeoplearevulnerableandeasytargetstoberecruitedby
organised crime. On the contrary, the communities with higher incomes had less
negative preconceptions about the gated residents. Some of those participants had
metpeoplefromthegatedcommunity,althoughtherewasnotnecessarilyafriendship
tie.Theopinionaboutthosegatedresidentswasnotlinkedtoorganisedcrime;non-
gated neighbours described them as working people from middle to upper-middle
class.Beyondthephysicalbarriersamongneighbourhoodsandgatedcommunities,
prejudgmentsresultfromuncertaintyaboutwholivesbehindthewalls;increasing
pre-existingfearsandrisingthetensionsbetweentheinsideandoutsidemainlyin
communitiesfrommiddleandlowincomes.

politicalissuesthatlaterfacilitateddrugtraffic,usingtheCentralAmericanisthmus
asabridgefromSouthAmericatoNorthAmerica.Currently,duringthistransfer,

somequantitiesofdrugsremaininCostaRicaasapaymenttodealersorforlocal

5.5.3 OverwhelmingGrowth,InvoluntaryEnclosure
As noted earlier, gated communities in Costa Rica are expanding quickly. Within
the Greater Metropolitan Area, they are embedded into the urban fabric, next to
established neighbourhoods. The residents from those places in rapid expansion
expressed their discontent about the situation. For instance, those neighbourhoods
surroundedbyatleastfourgatedcommunitieslargerthanonehectaresaidtheyfelt

### Page 74

5 FearandSegregation:AnxietyBeyondGatedCommunities… 71
‘wrapped’bythosedevelopments.Thissortofinvoluntaryenclosureseemstorisethe
exclusionfeelingsandfuelresentmentsamongnon-gatedresidents;theyexpressed
a feeling that their community has been taken by strangers who live in their own
fortifications.Intheabsenceofspacesforsharingandmixing,theanxietytowards
thosestrangersisevenmoresignificant.
Acommoncomplaintwasthelackofcontributionfromgatedcommunitiestothe
neighbouringfacilities,especiallyregardingthesupplyofpublicspacessuchasparks
andstreets.Besides,theypointedouthowthesignificantsurfacecoveredbythose
developments makes communication among neighbourhoods difficult. Pujol et al.
(2011) stand that gated communities have risen the drawbacks in the Costa Rican
roadsystem,whichalsohasaffectedtrafficcongestion.Inthisregard,thehightraffic
volumewascitedasanimportantissueinmostofthecases.Consideringthatbusstops
weremainlylocatednexttogatedcommunities’walls,theheavytrafficworksasa
‘secondwall’increasingfearsduringwaitingtimes.Ingeneral,inthoseneighbour-
hoodswheretheamountofgatedcommunitiesisgrowingfaster,thereisadiscontent
towardslocalgovernment’scontrolsandregulations.Non-gatedresidentsthinkthe
planning rules are not strong enough to control the size, the connections within
the urban fabric and the supply of facilities for neighbouring communities. In this
respect, the participants mentioned that gated communities cover extensive areas
withoutsupplyinganystreet,obstructingcontactwithotherneighbourhoods.
A general complaint, especially in the 2NBI and 3NBI (middle and high
poverty)groupswasthediscomfortduringtheconstructionprocessofgatedcommu-
nities.They emphasisedhowthoseoperations damaged theneighbourhood infras-
tructure,whichwasbuiltwithcommunityfunds,inmanycases.Thosetensionsraised
whenthegroupsrealisedthelackofinterestfromthegatedcommunity’sadminis-
trationtomakereparations,whichwerefinallymadeafterthecommunityleaders’
pressure.
5.5.4 Distribution ofFearofCrime
Thegeneraldistributionoffearofcrimeinneighbourhoodshasvariationsaccording
tothepovertylevels.However,inmostcases,theintensityoffearincreasedwhen
thewalkwasapproachingthegatedcommunity’swall,mainlyduringthenight.As
mentioned,fearofcrimeisacomplexphenomenon;therearepsychological,demo-
graphicandenvironmentalfactorsinvolved;therefore,eachcasestudyhasitsown
nuances. For instance, those cases without community parks showed higher levels
offearinsidetheneighbourhoodthanthosewithrecreationalareas.Communityties
andspontaneousappropriationofpublicspacesinfluencedalsosafetyperceptions.
Despitethedifferencesamongcases,itcanbeconcludedthatthephysicalstructure
ofgatedcommunitiesproducesanemotionalresponseinneighbouringcommunities,
andthisresponsecanbeexacerbatedbyresidentialsegregation.

### Page 75

72 K.BarrantesChaves
5.5.5 APerspectiveontheTensionsinGatedCommunities
ofCostaRica
The tensions from open neighbourhoods towards gated communities are fuelled
by feelings of exclusion and distrust. The residential segregation has provoked
fears towards the unknown; people from gated communities are seen as strangers
whobelong somewhere else.Theexception tothiswasthewealthiestcommunity,
wherethegatedcommunity’sedgesaresoft,andpeoplefromthereareinvolvedin
communalissues.Inthiscase,thelevelsoftrustworkinbothdirections,unlikethe
rest.
LocalgovernmentsinCostaRicahavenotreactedquicklytocontrolfeaturesof
gated developments, such as size, edges and configuration. As a result, there are
‘islands’ of dwellings embedded into the city, without any permeability towards
their neighbours. This slow response is noticed by non-gated residents, who see
themselves as victims of the local government inefficiency with a small room for
actingtopreventtheissue.
Although gated communities have been advertised as ‘shelters’ against crime;
theireffectsonperceivedsafetyhavebeenoverlooked,mainlytheconsequencesfor
thoselivingoutsidethegate.Theexternalitiesoverthegatedperipheriesarerising
fearsandtensions,whichareintensifiedbyincomeinequality.

Chapter 6
Design as a Tool to Ground Rights
in the Case of Cavallerizza Reale in Turin
DanieleCampobenedetto
Abstract In the Italian reformist tradition, cities are considered spaces shared
by a diverse community. Containing this diversity—of cultures, social conditions,
incomes,andinterests—withinlimitedboundariescanresultinabuild-upofpres-
sure, or tensions, over space use and access. In this essay, some aspects of these
tensionsareexploredusingthecaseoftheCavallerizzaRealeinTurin,highlighting
howurbandesigncanbeconsideredavaluabletoolforestablishingcommons.The
firsttensionisbetweenpropertyandrightsofuse;thesecondtensionarisesduetothe
urgencytoutilizeascommonsurbanareasthathavebeenabandonedorareunder-
used;thethirdtensionisbasedonthetoolsusedtotacklethetransitionfromunused
orunderusedspacestourbancommons.TheCavallerizzaRealecasereveals,inthe
light of these three tensions, how urban design tools can play a role in grounding
rightsofuse,overcomingthesuperpositionofpublicpropertyandpublicuse.What
is more, it unveils how processes that bring agency to the transformation can play
aninstitutionalizationrole,eventuallyspeedinguptheelaborationofurbandesign
toolsbyofficials.
· · ·
Keywords Urbancommons Urbanrules Urbandesign Turin
6.1 Common(s)Tensions
IntheItalianreformisttradition,citiescontinuetobeconsideredspacessharedby
adiversecommunity(Olivetti1960).Containingthisdiversity—ofcultures,social
conditions,incomes,andinterests—withinlimitedboundariescanresultinabuild-
upofpressure,ortensions(Huron2015),overspaceuseandaccess.Inthisessay,
some aspects of these tensions are explored using the case of the Cavallerizza
Reale in Turin, highlighting how urban design can be considered a valuable tool
forestablishingcommons.

DAD—DepartmentofArchitectureandDesign,PolitecnicoDiTorino,Torino,Italy

76 D.Campobenedetto
ArelevantstartingpointforthisexplorationisVitale’sreframingoftwoseminal
worksofcommonsliterature(2013:14).HearguedthatbyopposingHardin’s(1968)
and Ostrom’s (1990) studies on commons, we are losing other understandings of
these two works, such as the importance of actual space (Ostrom argued that her
researchfocusedoncommonsofcontrolleddimensions)andtheimportanceofrules
(Hardindescribedcoercionandtherecognitionofnecessityascrucialpointsinhis
argument).Harvey(2012:83)continuedonthesametrackbyquestioningthestate’s
regulatory role in the spatial field: “we are left in the dark as to how such higher-
orderrulesmightbeconstituted,bywhom,andhowtheymightbeopentodemocratic
control.”
Vitale’sandHarvey’sunderstandings,whichfocusedonspatialdistributionand
therulesbywhichitcanbeusedasacommon,unveiledthreespatialtensionsthat
canberecognizedinawiderangeofcases.
The first tension is between property and rights of use. In several contexts, it
seems that the ascription of urban space as public property could be the only way
toguaranteethenon-rivalryofthisgoodandthusitspublicuse.1 Theoverlapping
of public property and common use has been questioned by several scholars, who
havearguedthatthisoverlapcannotbeconsideredasgranted.Ward(2002),among
others, explored the private use of common lands through the squatting practices
observedincommonsinEngland,andKayden(2000)offeredacounter-perspective
bylookingatthepublicuseofprivatelyownedspacesincontemporaryNewYork
City.Inotherwords,thereareseveralexamplesofbuildingsandurbanspacesthat
canbedefinedas—toparaphraseKayden—publiclyownedprivatespaces,suchas
barracks,policestations,prisons,andgovernmentoffices,aswellasprivatelyowned
spacesthatcanbeconsidered,asamatteroffact,commons.
Thesecondtensionarisesduetotheurgencytoutilizeascommonsurbanareas
thathavebeenabandonedorareunderused.AsBrenner(2019)andHarvey(2012)
stated, the contradictory interplay between fixity and motion has underpinned the
continuousreshapingofthecity.Thechangesinuseoccursignificantlyfasterthan
thereshapingoftheurbanforms.Experiencesoftemporaryusesofabandonedurban
spaces,suchassitesforartisticperformances,andtacticalurbanism,areincreasingly
consideredpracticesthatcanhelptoaddressdifferentandevencontradictoryissues.
On one hand, this spatial agency (Awan et al. 2011) maintains the social mix and
addresses the poor quality and usage of public spaces (Lydon et al. 2015). On the
otherhand,thesepractices,whicharesometimesindependentfromtheintentionsof
thepromoters,playaroleinaddressingthedevaluationofrealestate(Landry2006)
andchangingtheperceptionsofdistricts(Oswaltetal.2013).
Thus,spacesintransition,especiallyifpubliclyowned,areoftenatthecenterof
bothmedium-termtop-downprocessesthataimtogovernthetransitionandbottom-
upshort-termtacticsthataimtoreturnthosespacestocollectiveuse.
1ThisperspectivewasconfirmedinItalyontwooccasions.First,in2011,duringtheabrogative
referendumonwaterprivatisation,whenthepublicwaterfactionclaimedanoverpoweringvictory
of.Second,in2014,whentheRegulationofGreenSpaceswasadoptedbytheCityCouncilof
Rome, giving some responsibilities to private individuals (in this case citizens) for maintaining
publicproperty.

### Page 80

6 DesignasaTooltoGroundRights… 77
Thethirdtensionisbasedonthetoolsusedtotacklethetransitionfromunusedor
underusedspacestourbancommons.Therighttothecityisoftenpursuedthrough
specialtools,suchasmasterplansordetailedplansthataresite-specific.Incontrast,
institutions and citizens struggle to apply an ordinary right (Olmo 2019), stated
throughregulationsthatapplytothewholecity,regardlessofthepropertyregime.
Adeeperanalysisofasinglecasestudymayoffersomeinsightsintothesethree
tensionsandthekeyrolethattherelationshipbetweenrulesandactualspacesetby
urbandesigntoolsplaysinthiscontext.
6.2 TheCavallerizzaReale:AnUrbanRiddle
The Cavallerizza Reale is a complex of buildings and open spaces that were once
devotedtotheroyalmilitaryacademy,horsestables,andridingactivities.Itislinked
to a larger system of royal palaces within the historic city center of Turin, called
ZonadiComando(Fig.6.1).
Thiscomplexiscomposedofavarietyofbuildingswithdifferentscalesandshapes
thatwerebuiltoveralmostthreehundredyears(from1675toabout1955).Monu-
mentalbuildings,mostlyunfinished,areintertwinedwithminorstructuresresulting
from normative and architectural adjustments, patching, and adaptations. Open
Fig. 6.1 CavallerizzaReale complex (black line) and the other buildings of Zona di Comando
listedasUNESCOworldheritagesites

### Page 81

78 D.Campobenedetto
spaces reflect the situation of the built structures and include monumental courts,
little alleys, and small squares. Thus, this layered built environment has resulted
from a slow growth process in which buildings have been incrementally added or
slightlymodified,sometimesfollowingandsometimesbetrayingacomprehensive
design.
Theoverallvisionforthiscomplexhasvariedwidelyovertime.IntheTheatrum
Sabaudiae, a recueil of projects that foreshadowed the desired aspect of several
citiesintheDuchyofSavoy,thecomplexwaspartofaconsistentdesignplannedto
impresstheEuropeancourts.Thisgrandeurwascompletelyabandonedinthemid-
twentiethcentury,whentherewereplanstomodernizethecitycenteranddemolish
the area.2 Nevertheless, not the first nor the second vision was accomplished. The
CavallerizzaRealecomplexremainspartofthehistoricalcitycenter,anduntilthe
endofthetwentiethcentury,theinfrastructurewasusedforavarietyofpurposes,
mostlyconnectedtoitsformermilitaryfunction.Thischangedduringthe1990s.In
1997,theCavallerizzaRealewasincludedinthesystemofroyalpalacesthatobtained
recognitionasaUNESCOWorldHeritageSite.Amasterplanfortransformingthe
complexintoamuseumwasdraftedsoonafter.Thischangeopenedthepublicdebate
aroundthefutureofthecomplex,startingaprocessthat,forthefollowingdecade,
wascharacterizedbytensionsandconflicts(Fig.6.2).
Nearly10yearslater,in2006,immediatelyafterTurinhostedtheWinterOlympic
Games, the municipality acquired the eastern sector of the complex from the state
propertyadministrationinthehopeofrealizingthemuseumplans.However,in2010,
itwasforcedtoselltheproperty.3
Meanwhile, the Cavallerizza Reale complex, now with the vast majority of its
buildingsfreefromotheractivities,underwentsometransformation,particularlyon
thegroundfloors.From2005to2013,themainridingbuilding(ManeggioAlfieriano)
wastransformedintoatemporarytheaterhall,continuingonfromthefirstexperiment
made in 1997, when the Young Artists Biennale was held within the complex. In
2008,asthefirstphaseofthemasterplan,therenovationofanotherridingbuilding
(Maneggio Chiablese) into the main lecture hall of the University of Turin was
launched.
Theyear2014canbeconsideredaturningpoint:theDepositsandLoansFund
purchasedthewesternsector,completingtheprivatizationofthesite.Inthesameyear,

6.3 PublicPropertyandCommonUse
ThethreemasterplansfortheCavallerizzaRealeprovideanaccountofthetensions
produced between property regimes and the right of use in the institution of a
common.
Thefirstattempttoreturnthesemi-abandonedcomplextopublicuse,backin1999,
consideredtheoverlapofpublicpropertyandpublicuse.Thismasterplaninvolved
moving on from an agreement between the state property administration and the
municipality of Turin that kept the whole complex in a regime of public property.
This, together with the consideration of the Cavallerizza Reale as a monumental
complex to be established with a comprehensive design, was a key element of the
regeneration hypothesis. The vast majority of the complex was to be a museum,
exhibition rooms, and a university library. These proposed functions affected both
thegroundfloor,withitsbighalls,andthepartitionedupperfloors,whiletheopen
spaceswereconsideredpublicsincetheygrantedaccesstoallthepublicbuildings.
We can argue that, in this case, tensions around the transformation of the area
were grounded more in the setting of rights (of public access and use) tout-court
ratherthanthedistributionofrightswithintheactualconfigurationofspaces.
Thisfirstattempthadmaterialeffectsthatimpactedthesiteforthenextdecade,
onthegroundsoftheCavallerizzaRealethatpertainedtothepublicdomain.4Itwas
soon clear, however, that the realization of the museum and the library would not
be feasible.5 The following ten years were characterized by the long-term effects
ofthefirstmasterplanandtheoccupationoftheCavallerizzaReale.Anotherfactor
alsohadanimpact—perhaps,themostinterestingforourdiscussion—thechangein
ownershipthatoccurredbetween2007and2010andtheconsequentintroductionof
public–privateactors.
Themasterplanpromotedin2015bythesenewactorsconsideredthemultifaceted
nature of the historic buildings and abandoned the perspective of the need for a
comprehensive function; thus, it offered a different understanding of the tools for
rights distribution. By looking at the Cavallerizza Reale as a multi-layered system
of buildings owned by different parties, the masterplan proposed a design-based
approach: the reorganization of the right of use to set a specific urban space as a
common.Whatresultedwasaclearpartitionbetweenpublicuseofthegroundfloor
andprivateuseoftheupperfloors(Fig.6.3).
TheMunicipalCouncilchange in2016resultedinanothermasterplanin2019,
whichseemedtopartiallyalignwiththepreviousmasterplan.Thisprojectassumed
thechangedpropertyregimesandsetapublicrightofuseforthegroundfloor.This
was clearly set for the open spaces, while the rights of use for the interior spaces
seemedtobeimpliedbytheenvisionedfunctions.
4ExamplesaretheuseoftheManeggioAlfierianobytheStabiletheatreandtheprojectforthe
mainhalloftheuniversityinManeggioChiablese.
5Thefirstoftheseprojectswasthe2004feasibilitystudy.Whilekeepingthemuseum,thisproject

Thisshorttalebringsusbacktothetensionbetweenownershipandaccessand
howithaschangedthroughouttherecentseriesofmasterplansfortheCavallerizza
Reale. The first episode proposed the unity of public function and public property
with a comprehensive project that nonetheless established enclosures (as recalled
in the paraphrasing of Kayden). The second offered an approach that considered
the distribution of the architectural space to set rights of use that (private) owners
mustaccepttoplaytherenovationgame,whilethelastseemstohaveconcentrated
onsettingrightsforopenspaces.Inthiscontext,theconsiderationofactualspatial
distributioncanplayaroleinkeepingthebalanceinwhatSamuelStain(2019:30)
called“thepropertycontradiction,”thatis,thetensionsbetweenthe(private)capital
desire to use planning to speculate and the aspiration to contrast restriction to the
propertyrightsthatplanningcanset.
6.4 AsSoonasPossible
OnMay23,2014,theAssembleaCavallerizza,6agroupofyoungartistsandstudents,
reopenedthegatesafteraperiodofpartialabandonmentofthecomplex.Theyoccu-
pied some of the halls on the ground floor, the rooms on the upper floors, and the
6Seehttps://cavallerizzairreale.org/.

### Page 85

82 D.Campobenedetto
open spaces. The occupiers claimed the urgency for the common use of the estate
after the Maneggio Alfieriano was no longer being used as a theater hall and the
urgency to save the buildings from being sold to private actors for speculation.
The Assemblea members soon started a program of concerts, workshops, theater
performances, and working groups of participatory planning. They were active in
contrasting the aforementioned municipality actions with protests. This bold and
vitalactivitystartedafteryearsofthecitizensseeingfewsignsofastructuralregen-
eration,despitethemunicipalitydeclaringthecomplexascrucial.Thisappropriation
(BragagliaandKrähmer2018;Vianello2015;DiFeliciantonio2017),intheabsence
ofothersubjects’agency,emphasizesthetensionbetweenthemunicipality’sattempts
torunarenovationprojectandtheurgencytousethespaceasurbancommons.In
thisregard,theCavallerizzaRealecaseprovidesquiteadifferentscenariotoother
well-knownexperiences,suchasthefirstwaveofurbansquattinginBerlinduringthe
1980s(Awanetal.2011:80)orinLondonduringthe1970s(Martini2016;Anning
1980).Thefirstdifferentiatingfactoristheabundanceofinstitutionalprojectsthat
occurredontheestatebeforetheoccupation.Theoccupationseemsnottobejustan
exampleofareactiontounusedspacebutratherareactiontotheincapabilityofthe
variousdevelopers(municipality,atfirst)todeliverapublicuseestate.Masterplans
andprojectsseemtobevoidboxeswhencomparedtothevitalitythattheoccupiers
cancatalyzeinmorethanfiveyearsofactivity(Fig.6.4).
Marcel Roncayolo (2002) provided an understanding of such a situation in his
workontherelationshipbetweeninstitutionalizationandtransformationprocesses
Fig. 6.4 Central alley of the Cavalleria Reale that, until 2019, was the heart of Assemblea

attheendofthefirstFrenchEmpire.Inthatcontext,heregisteredashiftbetween

thatwereputinplacebytheEmpiretodealwiththecontrolofbothurbanformand
use(inparticular,thealignmentplans).Inthatcase,achangeinbuildingtechniques

WecanrecognizesometraitsofasimilarmechanisminthecaseoftheCavallerizza
Reale;themasterplanofthelate1990sclashedwiththeglobalfinancialcrisisand
withthelackofpublicandprivateinvestments,andthemunicipalityandotherprivate

tobeineffective.TheAssembleaoccupiersfitthisnarrative,apparentlysucceeding
ininterpretingthischangebyusingthepotentialoftheactualspatialdistribution(big
hallsonthegroundfloor,smallroomsontheupperfloors)andthe(central)position
oftheCavallerizzaReale.
Thesecondratheruniqueelementistheroleoftheoccupationintheinstitutional-
izationoftheCavallerizzaRealeissue.Inothercases,theinstitutionalizationprocess
waspossiblebecauseofariseinrealestatevalues.Forexample,intheoccupation
ofwarehousesandworkshopsinNewYorkCityduringthe1960s,thesideeffectof
risingrealestateendedupinachangeintheregulationofthecitythatlegalizedthe
lofts(Shkuda,2016).TheCavallerizzaRealeoccupationdidnothavethesideeffect
ofraisingthelandvalue.Nevertheless,theoccupiers’refusaltonegotiatewiththe
municipality without the approval of a comprehensive regulation regarding urban
commons played a crucial role in the approval of the Commons Regulation of the
city. Thus, the non-institutional tactical experience, born as a reaction to the lack
of use of the historical complex, seems to have produced the effect of the public
authoritiesacceleratingtheinstitutionalizationofurbancommonsinthecity.
6.5 OrdinaryandSite-SpecificRights
Thisbringsustothethirdtension:thatwhichoccursbetweenordinaryrights,which
apply regardless of the character of the site, and rights that take into considera-
tion the configuration of the built space. Cremaschi (2019) underlined how urban
designdraftssubstantialrightsonthegroundduetothearrangementofurbanspace.
Kratzwald (2015), in her critique of Orstom’s principle for long-lasting commons
institutions, recognized boundaries as a key feature for the establishment of urban
commons. Combining these two understandings of the issue, we can recognize
as boundaries not only the perimeters traced on the ground but also the spatial
arrangementofthebuiltenvironment.
As seen in the Cavallerizza Reale case, masterplans can sometimes recognize
thesearchitecturalboundaries,whichcanbeusedtoestablishlong-lastingrightsof
use rather than just functions. Thus, tools dependent on the actual organization of
spacecanbeconsideredessentialfortheestablishmentofcommons.Nevertheless,

### Page 87

84 D.Campobenedetto
thesetoolshavealimit:Theyareparticularinscriptionsofrightslinkedtoaspecific
proprietorialsituation.
Harvey (2012) described how citizens’ political action (not public property) is
necessaryfortheconstructionofcommons.However, whenthispoliticalactionis
beyond a regulatory system, such as that of the Assemblea, it is less likely to last.
Thus,whatseemstobeneeded,inadditiontomasterplans,isanordinaryright(Olmo
2019)abletoregulatetheuseofspaceindependentlyfromthepropertyregimeorthe
positionalandarchitecturalcharactersofthosespaces.UrbanCommonsRegulations
released in the last year in Turin, Bologna, and 245 more public institutions7 are
effectively working on this track, with one major drawback: The vast majority of
theseregulationsdescribeaprocedurefortheestablishmentofurbancommonsthat
fails to state a pre-agreed order. Thus, they lose their ordinariness to fit the case-
specificconditions.Thisentailsanegotiationbetweentheactorsinvolvedtocreate
asetofrulesthatcanbeappliedinthedomaininwhichthemasterplanoperates.
Therefore, in the Cavallerizza Reale case, tension exists between the two kinds
of tools. Still, it may be possible to dissolve this tension with a double-layered
organizationofrights,basedonbothordinaryandsite-specificrules,designednot
toquestionthepropertyregimebutrathertoofferaclearandsharedunderstanding
ofrightstothepartiesinvolvedinthetransformation.
6.6 Conclusions:UrbanDesignasaTooltoGroundRights
This examination of Turin’s Cavallerizza Reale reveals shades and nuances that
offer some information for the ongoing debate on urban commons. In particular,
it highlights how urban design tools can play a role in grounding rights of use,
overcoming the superposition of public property and public use. The Cavallerizza
Realecasesuggeststhatthisispossibleundertwoconditions:thattheconfiguration,
distribution,andtransformationpotentialofaspaceplaysaroleinthedefinitionof
enjoyment of the rights; and that through an ordinary right, all actors can take the
initiativefortheestablishmentofcommons.
What’smore,thiscaseunveilshowprocessesthatbringagencytothetransfor-
mation process, particularly by releasing the potential of use of underused urban
areas,canplayaninstitutionalizationroleinthetransformationprocess,eventually
speedinguptheelaborationofurbandesigntoolsbyofficials.
ThetransformationoftheCavallerizzaRealeisongoing.Thus,theseconclusions
canbeconsideredasworkinghypotheses.Nevertheless,theyprovidehintsonhow
physicalspacecantransformthroughdesigntools,fromanelementthatgenerates
urbantensiontoapartofthesolution.

Chapter 7
From Tensions to Design: Building
Inclusion with Roma Communities
in Milan
GiovannaCavalli,BeatriceGalimberti,andSilviaNessi
Abstract In the Milanese territory, Roma camps differ in permanence and legal
recognition,intheirinhabitants’originsandunwrittenrules.Nevertheless,thereare
somerecurrentfeatures,suchasinformalityandsocio-spatialisolation.Indeed,the
mostinaccessibleurbanandperi-urbanareasusuallyhostthecamps,makingthem
invisible to the rest of the city. This invisibility is two-folded. On the one hand,
it provides a protective context to develop mutual aid processes, activating slow
paths to better living conditions. On the other hand, it worsens isolation, leaving
room for the law of the strongest and thus inhibiting the possibilities of improve-
ment and social integration. This chapter draws on these arguments to recount the
experienceofERSILIAlab,theASFItalia’surbanlaboratory,which,between2015
and2019,workedwithtwoRomacommunitieslivingintheMolise-Calvairate-Ponti
neighbourhoodinMilanandwiththeschoolsandassociationsofthesameneighbour-
hood.Withoutdenyingthedifficulties,ERSILIAlabpromotedmutualunderstanding
betweenRomacommunitiesandtherestoftheneighbourhood,beyondthestigma,
throughaparticipatorydesignprocessthatledtothebuildingoftheERSILIAlab’s
carriage. The carriage hosted an exhibition on Romani culture illustrated by the
projectparticipantsaroundthecity’spublicspaces.

7.1 Introduction
IntheMilaneseterritory,Romacampsdifferinpermanenceandlegalrecognition,
as well as in their inhabitants’ origins and unwritten rules. Nevertheless, there are
somerecurrentfeatures,suchasinformalityandsocio-spatialisolation.Indeed,the
mostinaccessibleurbanandperi-urbanareasusuallyhostthecamps,makingthem
invisible to the rest of the city. This invisibility is two-folded. On the one hand, it
providesaprotectivecontexttodevelopmutualaidprocesses,activatingslowpaths
to better living conditions. On the other hand, it worsens isolation, leaving room
forthelawofthestrongestandthusinhibitingthepossibilitiesofimprovementand
socialintegration.
Informality,invisibility,andisolationseemtobeinextricable,beingatthesame
timethecauseandtheeffectofprofoundsocio-spatialtensionswiththerestofthecity.
However, the work by the non-profit organisation Architetti Senza Frontiere Italia
onlus—ASFItalia1—seekstochangeperspective.Indeed,itsuggeststhatifwelook
at informal spaces as places of continuous transformation and possible inclusion,
wecandevelopstrategiesforapproachingthemwithoutdenyingtheirnon-regulated
nature,promotingtheirpotentialitiesandusingthemtocounterisolationissues.
ThiscontributiondrawsontheseargumentstorecounttheexperienceofERSILIA
lab,theASFItalia’surbanlaboratory,which,between2015and2019,workedwith
two Roma communities living in the Molise-Calvairate-Ponti neighbourhood in
Milan and with the schools and associations of the same neighbourhood. Without
denyingthedifficulties,ERSILIAlabpromotedmutualunderstandingbetweenRoma
communitiesandtherestoftheneighbourhood,beyondthestigma,throughapartic-
ipatory design process that led to the building of the ERSILIA lab’s carriage. The
carriagehostedanexhibitiononRomanicultureillustratedbytheprojectparticipants
aroundthecity’spublicspaces.
To structure this account, the chapter begins by giving an insight into Roma’s
conditions in Milan, among difficulties and possibilities. Then it focuses on the
context of the project. In the following paragraphs, it intertwines the process of
the project, the reflections developed during ERSILIA lab, and some considera-
tions on the Municipality of Milan’s governance regarding the inclusion of Roma
communities.
7.2 RomaCamps,AnItalianPhenomenon.WhatAbout
Roma’sLivingConditionsinMilan?
Italy is the only country in Europe that, since the late 1980s, has consolidated a
parallelhousingsystemforaspecificethnicgroupthroughregionallaws:theRoma

2020).

exclusionaryone,astherulessetconditionsforaccesstotheliststhatthoseliving
inaninformalsettlementcannotmeet.4

On the other hand, however, it is precisely invisibility that fosters the rising of
some positive urban alternatives (Fisker et al. 2019). In this sense, in our experi-
enceofworkingasASFItaliaintheformermunicipaltemporarycentre—CAT—of
via Sacile, we have witnessed the flourishing of some alternatives, protected by
invisibility.Thisisthecase,forexample,ofmutualaidrelationshipsbasedonrecip-
rocaltrust:singlewomen,ifnecessary,couldentrusttheiryoungestchildrentoother
womenwhoremainedinthecentre.Moreover,intheabsenceofregularemployment
pathways,beggingandillegalactivitiesarenottheonlyoptions.IntheCAT,thelife
ofmostoftheinhabitantswasbuiltaroundthesecond-handmarketeventsinPiazzale
Cuoco.Duringtheweek,rubbishfromthecitywascollected,sorted,cataloguedand
repairedwithskilfulcraftsmanship,becomingagainitemsforsaleonSundaysinthe
market.
Thesearesmallexamplesofhowinformalityconditionsalsohaveapositiveside
thatcanleadtotheconstructionofinnovativeprocesses.
In Milan—and elsewhere—the third sector plays a fundamental role in inter-
veningintheseprocesses,workingwithininvisibilitycontexts,reachingoutwhere
themunicipalityfails.Thisworkisslowandfullofobstacles,butitpointstotrans-
formingtheobjectiveofsocialinclusionoftheRomafromawickedproblemtoa
possiblepath.
For these reasons, we take ERSILIA lab as an example, the work that, as ASF
Italia, we are carrying out with two Roma communities in Milan. To do this, we
startbywritingaboutwhotheyare,wheretheylive,andtheproblemsfacedbythe
protagonistsofourparticipatoryactions.
7.4 ERSILIALab’sPlaces:TheCamp,TheCAT,andThe
Neighbourhood
The camp of via Bonfadini is one of Milan’s regular camps. It is located in the
south-easternpartofthecityandhasbeenestablishedbythemunicipalityin1987
with parking lots for the trailers of a Roma community from Abruzzi—which are
Italiancitizens.Overtheyears,thecommunityreplacedthetrailerswithshacksand
tiny houses. Today there are about 40 families. The camp is an example of spatial
segregation, being a triangular buffer zone between three railway lines. The only
access route is a two-lane subway that floods with rain. Close to a now partially
disusedmanufacturingarea,thecampisfarfromtheresidencesandservicesofthe
MoliseCalvairatePontineighbourhood,ofwhichitisofficiallypartandwherethe
childrengotoschool.
Therearenocollectivespacesinthecamp,yetchildrenandwomenspendmostof
theirtimeinsideofit.Thewomenarealwaysbusywithchildcareorhousework.Some
ofthemwork,andsomehavetosupportthefamilyalone—havingtheirhusbandsin
prison.Thirdsectoroperatorstakechildrentoschool,withup-and-downresults.The
fewrelationshipsthechildrenestablishoutsidethecamparelimitedtoschooltime

### Page 95

92 G.Cavallietal.
andspace,andtheyarepowerlesstobreakthroughthemistrusttheneighbourhood
feelsforthecamp,whichisconsideredano-goarea.Theisolationmakesthecamp’s
surroundings a breeding ground for acts of petty crime by some of its inhabitants
andoutsiders.
In2016,thecamp’ssegregatedandstigmatisedstatewasfurtherworsenedbythe
openingofatemporaryreceptioncentre—CAT—atthecornerbetweenviaBonfa-
dini and via Sacile, a few dozen metres away. The CAT, managed by Fondazione
Casa della Carità, mainly accommodates Roma families of various ethnicities—
around 130 people—who have been evicted from irregular camps and have estab-
lished a social integration pact with the municipality, which is the start of a long
journeytowardssocialhousing(Fig.7.2).
TheCATisanenclosedspacewithtwelvelivingcontainers,eachabout90square
metres in size and housing five families promiscuously; each family sub-unit is
separatedfromtheothersbyacurtain.Othersmallcontainershousesharedbathrooms
and kitchens. In spring and summer, the treeless, earthen and rubbish-filled open
spacefillswiththelifeofthistemporarycommunity.
The CAT inhabitants reciprocally mistrust the Roma from the Bonfadini camp
and,exceptfortheirchildren’sschooling,theyhavenonetworkofrelationswiththe
MoliseCalvairatePontineighbourhood,whichisdividedfromthecampandCAT
by a network of infrastructures and the Alessandrini park. The Molise Calvairate
Ponti neighbourhood is predominantly working-class and multi-ethnic and hosts
manyolderpeopleandfamiliesineconomicallyfragileconditions.Thus,thelevel
ofconflictbetweentheneighbourhood,theBonfadinicamp,andtheCATinhabitants
ishigh,andthereismutualfear.

7.5 ERSILIALab’sProcess
InJanuary2015,asASFItalia,wegotintouchwiththirdsectororganisationsalready
workingwiththeBonfadinicampinhabitants,suchasFondazioneSomaschi.
Our first steps were all about listening to the community’s voices, so as to
explore—with our gaze anchored to space—their point of view on the camp and
the neighbourhood, coming to talk about their desires and expectations regarding
futurehousingandworkingconditions.
Many women and young people from the camp were very active in the debate,
despitethelegitimatemistrust—asmanythirdsectorassociationsstartprojectssoon
abandoned.Whatemergedfromtheirspeecheswasadifferentperspective,adesire
fortransformationthatcanbethebasisforcommunityempowerment.
During2016,theworkwiththeBonfadinicampinhabitantsevolvedintoaspatial
project called ERSILIA lab, the relational library to create an intermediate place
to foster relationships between children and teens from the camp and the neigh-
bourhood. In the meantime, the Sacile CAT has been set up, and its community
progressivelyinvolvedintheproject.
Women,youngpeople,andchildren—Romaandnot—werealwaysattheheart
oftheproject,whereco-designsessionsalternatedwithaseriesofworkshopsand
festiveevents.
Aseriesofobstaclesemergedduringtheproject,leadingtocontinuousreshaping
of the process. In its first version, the relational library was about co-designing a
temporarystructuretoco-buildinAlessandrinipark,asortoftradingzonephysically
placedbetweenthecamp,theCAT,andtheneighbourhood.Localassociationsand
localbusinesseswouldhavesupportedthebuildingphase.DespitetheMunicipality
political figures’ support, we had to overcome the Municipality technical figures’
obstacles due to what they called the exceptionality of our project. Nevertheless,
once they were overcome, we were ready to build the library, a future symbol of
dialogue.
In the spring of 2017, just a few weeks before the start of implementation, the
localauthoritiesofMunicipio4begantostronglyopposetheproject,ridingthewave
ofthecontingenthostileclimatetowardsmigrantsandoutcastssuchasRoma.From
alegalperspective,wecouldhavestillcarriedouttheproject.However,whatwas
thepointofdoingsoifthelibraryanditsattendersweretobecomeaneasytarget
forhostilities?
Wedecidedtoputasidetheconstructionofthetemporarylibrarytoprotectboththe
peopleinvolvedandtheaimofbridgingRomaandtheneighbourhood.Wedecided
totakeadifferentpath,togetherwiththeheterogeneousandwillinggroupgathered
around our activities. The new idea was about a project that would foster cultural
exchangeandmutualunderstandingwithoutrunningtheriskofbeingvulnerableto
hostilities.TheRomanìcultureitselfsuggestedtheidea:anomadicandmobileobject,
a small openable carriage, a relational library without foundations for travelling
aroundtheneighbourhoodandthecity.

94 G.Cavallietal.

knoweachother.
Meanwhile, we opened il tavolo di ERSILIA lab, the first neighbourhood table

ERSILIA lab’s table envisioned a future of collaboration and sharing of places,
services, and activities in the neighbourhood, while maintaining the richness and
specificityoftheRomacultureandalltheotherswholivethere.

fordebatesopentothepublic,aimedatintroducingtheRomanìculturetothecity

and promoting dialogue on the current living conditions in camps and temporary

Conditionsmaychangeinthecontext,thesingleprojectsmayend,butwhether
eachparticipant’spotentialisenhanced,thejourneycontinues.
7.6 AContradictoryGovernance
In the last decade, the Municipality of Milan has taken some steps towards urban
inclusion of Roma communities, implementing the Strategia Nazionale di Inclu-
sioneRom,Sinti,Caminanti2012–2020(UNAR2012)7throughthedocumentLinee
GuidaRomSintiCaminanti(ComunediMilano,2012).Theimplementationofthese
guidelineshasbeenpartialandcontradictory,oftenduetopoliticaltensionsbetween
municipalbodies,aswitnessedbytheexperiencedescribedinthischapter.
Since 13 December 2019, the Municipality of Milan, like others in Italy, has
openedaworkingtablefordraftingaPianoLocalediAzione—localactionplan—to
make the work begun with the 2012 guidelines more effective. At the table, there
areseveraldepartmentsoftheMunicipality,institutions,tradeunions,associations,
foundations,andschools,allrealitiesthatalreadyknowandworkpositivelywiththe
Romaworld.
However,rightfromitsstart,thisnewinitiativehasasignificantflaw:thereare
no Roma community representatives in Milan at the table. The new path does not
7TheStrategyplannedbyUNAR—theItaliannationalanti-racialdiscriminationoffice—isinspired
bythe10CommonBasicPrinciplesonRomaInclusionbytheEuropeanCommission(2009).

### Page 99

96 G.Cavallietal.
start positively as “systematic exclusion from the institutional processes of repre-
sentation and decision making […] in a ‘slum redevelopment scheme’, it is active
discrimination”(ArchitectureSansFrontièresInternational,2012).
7.7 FinalRemarks
Extrememarginality,whichcharacterisesinformalsituationsandhousingemergen-
cies,islikeflowingwater:evenifonetriestodivertitorstopit,everytimewater
findsitscourseagainthroughnewpaths.Forthisreason,workingwithmarginality
requiresatransformationeffortthatneedstostartinsidethecontext.
Workneedstostartfromtheresourcesavailable.Findingthemandmakingthe
mostofthemisoftennoteasy;onehastolisten,observe,andexperiment.Whatis
certain is that some resources are always there. When a designer has to deal with
complex spatial and social tension, the work is long and experimental. To quote
Haraway(2016),thedesignershouldlearntoactby“stayingwith”thetensions.
Defusingsocialandspatialtensionsisnotafastoperationbutalong-termgoal.In
thisperspective,itisusefultokeepthefocusnotonthesingleartefactorthesingle
transformedspacebutonthecreationofanarticulatedpathwaythat,initsdevelop-
ment, leads to opportunities for knowledge, dialogue, valorisation, self-promotion
andtrust-building.Workingonthespaceinaparticipatorywayisalwaysthemeans.
Itisnevertheaim.

andgenericspacesofthemoderncityofthe1960s,encounteringlobbiesfurnished
with uncomfortable decorative chairs, infinite stretches of office cubicles, mosaics
of unshattered windows glowing at night with a multitude of life scenes taking
place. Beyond its caricatural aim, the movie highlights how the modern space for
collectivitywasoftenprioritizingtherepresentationofmodernityitself,lackingthe
supportforhumanrelations,asthetimelesspresenceofMonsieurHulotexposedthe
complicationsandparadoxesofthemodernhabitat.Inthecontemporarycity,many
of the devices and machines of the 1960s have gone with the digitalization. If in
the modern Paris of Playtime face-to-face relations were unavoidable—generating
muchofthefilm’shumour—contemporarytechnologiesallowtocoexistinthesame
placeswithoutnecessarilyinteracting.

8.1 Public–PrivateGradient
Jacque Tati’s Playtime is one of the classical representations of the clash between
modernity and everyday life. In the movie, Monsieur Hulot enters the typical and
generic spaces of the modern city of the 1960s, encountering lobbies furnished
with uncomfortable decorative chairs, infinite stretches of office cubicles, mosaics
of unshattered windows glowing at night with a multitude of life scenes taking
place. Beyond its caricatural aim, the movie highlights how the modern space for
collectivitywasoftenprioritizingtherepresentationofmodernityitself,lackingthe
supportforhumanrelations,asthetimelesspresenceofMonsieurHulotexposedthe
complicationsandparadoxesofthemodernhabitat.Inthecontemporarycity,many
of the devices and machines of the 1960s have gone with the digitalization. If in
the modern Paris of Playtime face-to-face relations were unavoidable—generating

Thepandemicstimulatedalengthydiscussiononthefutureofurbanspaceand
thespaceofsociability.However,whileattentionisbeingpaidtoopenspacesand
indoorspacereorganization,thegrowingnumberofindividualhouseholdsandthe
shrinkingsizeoftheirdwellingsremaintheconstantvariablesoftheurbanquestion
(OgdenandSchnoebelen2005).
AccordingtoKlinenberg,theindividualhouseholdisdestinedtorepresentmost
oftheurbanpopulationinthecontemporarycity(Klinenberg2012).Thecausesare
multiple and span from the labour market structure to gender emancipation. Plus,
digitalized forms of labour and consumption facilitate the alone-together ethos of
contemporaryurbansociety.
Inparallel,thebranchesofthedigitaleconomyascollaborativeconsumptionand
platformeconomyrelyconsistentlyontheconceptofsharing,fromtheimmaterial
sharingofcontentonsocialmediatoasharedtripwithacar-poolingapp(Botsman
andRogers2011;Srnicek2016).
Ifweleavesociologistsandanthropologiststhetasktounpacktheconsequences
ofthecurrentsocio-economicshifts,itislegitimatetoaskourselveshowthemantra
ofsharingimpactsurbanspaceandcollectivespaceinparticular(Fig.8.1).
In the early 2010s, the real estate market was mature enough to embrace the
principlesofthedigitaleconomy,releasingonthemarketnewhousingformatsfor
theurbanmiddle-classes,revealingsomehintsonthemodificationstakingplacein
thedesignofcollectivespace.Inthiscontext,co-livingwasdevelopedasarealestate
productemphasizingspacesfor“sharing”and“buildingcommunities”.Thefirstbuilt
examples like The Share in Tokyo and The Collective in London, contain already
intheirnameanexplicitreferencetocommunallivingandsharing,differentlyfrom
thehotellabellingtraditionofrecallingexclusivityandleisure—palacehotel,suites,
inn.
Co-living is a hybrid concept between commercial hospitality and a traditional
apartmentbuildingdevelopedasa“convenient”alternativetoflatsharing.1Currently
builtprimarilyinmetropoliseslikeLondonandNewYork,co-livingschemesoffer
roomsassmallasninesquaremeterscombinedwithvariousmaterialandimmaterial
servicesconsideredessentialfor“generationrent”(McKee2012).
Thelivingunitsofco-livingprojectsresemblethegenericityandstandardsofthe
roomsofahotel,whilethecollectivespacesarecharacterisedbylayoutssuggesting
flexibilityandmultipleuses.
Asalong-stayhospitalityformat,co-livingincorporatescommercialbusinesses
likegyms,bars,andco-workingspacescombinedwithasetofamenitiesaslaundries

The apical point of this tendency was reached in 2012 when Mark Zuckerberg
commissionedFrankGehrytodesignFacebook’sheadquartersasasingleopen-plan
officespaceofforty-thousandsquaremeters.2
In co-living projects, a similar inclusion process of multiple activities in large
collectivespacesaimstoblendprinciplesofoptimizationborrowedfromthehospi-
tality sector with the marketing goal of uniqueness. The results are various and
context-dependent but reveal a trajectory of design that could be generalized and
appliedinotherresidentialandnon-residentialprojects.
The research for adequate dimensioning of collective space has a long history
tracingbacktotheearlytwentieth-centuryexperiments.InRussia,theSovietrevo-
lution prompted the search for universal and standardized collective spaces at the
toplistoftheState’spoliticalagenda,initiatinganintenseresearchprocessamong
architectsandtheoriststhatwillleadtovariousbuiltexperimentsandfailures.
8.2 SovietOversizedCommunalSpace
IninterwarEurope,thesearchfortheperfecthousingfloorplanfortheexistenzmin-
imum was one of the primary brain puzzles for the architects of the International
Congresses of Modern Architecture. Confident in scientific research methods, the
participants of the third CIAM presented hundreds of projects challenging the
minimumdimensionsofashiporatraincabin(InternationaleKongressefürNeues
Bauen und Städtisches 1930; Teige 2002). The Taylorism ethos of the machine á
habiter of Le Corbusier oriented the discussion principally on the micro-scale of
the dwelling plan as the extreme modernized reduction of the nineteenth-century
bourgeoisehome.
As noted by Robin Middleton: “Yet, surprisingly, the single-cell living unit is
notincludedamongstthehundred-odddesignsinthereportofthattitle,introduced
by the CIAM congress of 1928. All the architects involved considered the minimal
existencetobeafamilyaffair”(Middleton1983,60).
Atthetime,tofindresearchonthesingle-celllivingunitpraisedbyMiddleton—
andtheconnectedcollectivespaces—oneshouldlookatpost-revolutionaryRussia.
While interwar European social democracies pivoted worker’s housing around the
social subject of the family, the Soviets had the ambition to provide independent
spacestotheworkersasindividuals.
Startingfromthemid-1920s,theministryofeconomyassignedMoiseiGinzburg
andtheOSAgrouptodesignastandardizeddwellingsystemtoapplytolarge-scale
projectsofcollectivehouses.Inordertoaccomplishthistask,Ginzburgrunsdetailed
researchonthecorrectandconvenientsizingofresidentialprojects,realizingsome
experimental transitional projects like the iconic Narkomfin building in Moscow
(FernándezPerMozasandOllero2013).
2https://www.archdaily.com/267366/facebook-frank-gehry.Accessed6May2021.

### Page 105

8 “TheBeachisBoring”.TheCollectiveSpaceofCo-living 103
Havingthepossibilitytocollectfirst-handinformationonthebehavioursofthe
inhabitants of the Narkomfin, Ginzburg raised various concerns on the efficacy of
totalcollectivization.
TheNarkomfinisdividedintoaslabofstandardizedunitsofvariousdimensions
and a connected pavilion for services. Ginzburg noted how the communal kitchen
andcanteenoftheannexbuildingwereactuallyusedmoretocollectmealstobringto
theprivateapartmentsthanformealsharing(Ginzburg2017).Thiskindofepisode
made him more cautious in proposing radical choices as the abolition of private
kitchensandtheminimizationofspaceforprivacy,andsoonadesigncompetition
willofferhimmoreargumentsfortheseassumptions.
Inparalleltotheresearchonstandardizationtosatisfytheurgenthousingdemand,
the OSA also focused on developing an experimental communal house (Dom-
kommuna), launching a competition between the Organization in 1927 that will
seeaswinningtheproposalofBarshchandVinogradov.
TheDom-kommunaproposalwascomposedbyaschemeoftwocrossingslabsof
two-hundred-and-fiftymeters,onededicatedtosixsquaremeterindividuallodgings
and one to communal services. Ginzburg notes how this clear separation between
theresidentialandthecommunalrequiredauniversalandstandardizedorganization
ofdailylife(Ginzburg2017,142).Inhisopinion,thisprojectandallitssubsequent
iterations “suffer from a lack of understanding of the importance ofpersonality in
thesocialistcollective”(Ginzburg2017,138).
Theextremereductionofindividuallivingspaceledtoaparadox:thecommunal
spaces as industrial kitchens and canteens sitting at least a thousand people were
contradictingthesocialistpurposebringingto“astronomicaldimensionsthemolec-
ularelementsofwayoflifeoftheoldfamily”(Ginzburg2017,142).Togiveascale
comparisonbetweentheNarkomfinandtheDom-KommunabyBarshchandVino-
gradov,thetotalcirculationareaofthelatteramountedatfifteentimesmoretheone
intheformer–thatisconsideredthesocialcondenserparexcellence.
Thiscommunalspacehypertrophywillleadtoprojectsliketherealizedstudent
hostel by Nikolaev (1930), where the sleeping cabins were windowless and with
sharedbathroomsanytworooms,orMelnikov’sSleepPavilionforZelenyGorod,
“where sleep is declared to be ‘socialist’, i.e. where people sleep all together in
enormousroomsandwherespecialorchestrasandreflectorsmufflethe‘socialized’
snoringinaccordancewithalltherulesofmodernscienceandart”(Ginzburg2017,
142).
Atthebeginningofthe1930s,thecentralgovernmentwillreorientitseconomic
planningmoreontheindustrializationofbuildingtechniquesthanonthearchitecture
ofmasshousing,interruptingthedebateonthedomkommuna.Theresultwillbethe
prefab concrete landscape stillpopulating mostof theEastern Bloccities (Meuser
etal.2016).
ThefailedattemptoftheOSAtofullystandardizethecollectivespaceofSoviet
housing fell apart because of the difficulties of not turning domestic space into
commercialspace.Ironically,theconcernsofGinzburgonthehypertropiaofcollec-
tivespacewillreturnasadesignissueinthemostmaturerepresentationofcapitalism
asthetechcompanyofficesofSiliconValleyandco-livingspaces.

Whatco-livingcompaniesdefineas“convenient”istheall-inclusivenessofthis
model.Thefactthatthemonthlyrentcomprisesworkspace,gymsubscription,and

The recent research from the French Bond Society shows how in a survey of
twenty-five co-living projects worldwide, the number of services offered and the
amountofdedicatedspaceareindependentofthenumberofresidents(Gautreauand
Bond Society 2018). This finding highlights the fundamental difference between
co-livingandtraditionalcommercialhospitalityprojects.Thenumberofcollective
spacesandamenitiesisstrictlyregulatedbyformulasandstandardsoftheindustry,
which qualify different rankings between hotels. In co-living, the collective space
quantityisnotconceivedonlyforitsresidentsbutforitspotentialtoattractthewider
urbanpopulation.
Beyond the quantitative data, this approach explains the diffuse attitude of co-
living companies to overcharge the symbolic space over the actual space. From
observingseveralrealizedprojects,itispossibletoextractsomespecificqualitiesof
thecollectivespaceofco-livinganditscriticalaspects.
Inoneofthefirstbuiltexamplesofco-living,TheShareinTokyo,NiklasMaak

betweenaclub,arestaurant,andanartificialforestwithhammocks—aswellasa
largekitchen,aspacefortheatreperformancesandfilmscreeningsorlectures,anda
library”(Maak2015,143).Eveninthereducedspaceofa1960sapartmentbuilding
reconversion, the sheer number of services contained is all aimed to stimulate an
interiorlandscapeoftheoverlap.Evenifthetheatreroomcouldneverbeusedasa
theatre,itsvaluestandsinthedifferencefromatraditionalresidentialprojectandin
thepotentialforunexpecteduses.Theoverlappingapproachdescribestheattempt

Chapter 9
The Two Faces of Matera: Diachronic
Narratives of Changing Perspectives
on Heritage
NicoleDeTogni
Abstract In the early 1950s, the Italian city of Matera gains international
prominence with the label of “national shame” attributed to the case of the Sassi,
inhabitedcavesexcavatedinthelocalravine.Inadecade,itwastransformedintothe
manifestoofPost-WorldWarIIdevelopment,inrelationtotheresearchandbuilding
programsoftheNationalInstituteofUrbanPlanningandtheUnitedNationsRelief
andRehabilitationAdministration,promotingnewneighborhoodsasamodernalter-
nativetorelocateSassi’sresidentsassuringbetterlivingconditions.Theinclusionof
theSassiintheUNESCOWorldHeritageListin1993,andtheprocessandevents
relatedtotheEuropeanCapitalofCulture2019,haveformalizedthelong-standing
recognitionoftheSassiasaheritagesiteandshowsthecomplexityoftherelation-
ship between cultural events and heritage. On the other hand, the post-war neigh-
borhoods disappeared from the public and disciplinary narrative, while facing the
typicalproblemsofhalf-a-century-oldmodernbuildingstockandsolicitingadisci-
plinaryreflectionintheframeofthetwentieth-centuryheritage.Thepapertracesthe
genesis of the urban tensions opposing different and shifting understanding of the
veryconceptofheritage,architecturalvalue,andplanningdiscipline.
· · ·
Keywords Megaevent Narrative Fragility Matera
9.1 Introduction
In the early 1950s, the Italian city of Matera gains international prominence with
the label of “national shame” attributed to the case of the Sassi, inhabited caves
excavatedinthelocalravine.Inadecade,itwastransformedintothemanifestoof
Post-World War II development, in relation to the research and building programs
oftheNationalInstituteofUrbanPlanningandtheUnitedNationsReliefandReha-
bilitation Administration, promoting new neighborhood as a modern alternative to
relocateSassi’sresidentsassuringbetterlivingconditions(Pontrandolfi2002).
