110 N.DeTogni
TheinclusionoftheSassiintheUNESCOWorldHeritageListin1993,andthe
processandeventsrelatedtotheEuropeanCapitalofCulture2019,haveformalized
thelong-standingrecognitionoftheSassiasaheritagesiteandshowsthecomplexity
oftherelationshipbetweenculturaleventsandheritage(Ponzinietal.2020).Onthe
otherhand,thepost-warneighborhoodsdisappearedfromthepublicanddisciplinary
narrative,whilefacingthetypicalproblemsofhalf-a-century-oldmodernbuilding
stock and soliciting a disciplinary reflection in the frame of the twentieth-century
heritage(Lenza2019).
Theresearchapproachisrootedinthecriticalassumptionthatthedisciplinaryand
publicnarratives—collectiveandindividual—relatedtourbanheritagecouldinflu-
enceitsperceptionandrepresentationbothforageneralandspecializedpublic,with
impactonitspatrimonializationprocessandprotection.Observedandinvestigated
onadiachroniclevelintheirgenesisandrelationshipwiththephysicalenvironment,
thesediscoursesconveythepolitical,disciplinary,andsocialclimateofprecisehistor-
icalmoments,aswellastheinterests,aspirations,andimaginariesoftheactorswho
originatethem.
Thegreatculturaleventsthatactontheunderstanding,institutionalization,orcele-
brationofdifferenttypesofheritagearegenerallyinterestingmomentsofrethinking
or consolidation of political, strategic, urban, and architectural readings related to
it. In this context, they can represent a potential element of heritage recognition,
boosterism,orfragility,especiallyinconnectiontopublichistoryandprocessesof
patrimonializationofan“ordinary”residentiallandscape(CaramellinoandDePieri
2015)thatisrarelyrecognizedasinstitutionalizedheritage.
InthecaseofMatera,thiscriticalperspectiveisusedtoinvestigatethenarratives
andthechangingfortunesoftheSassiandthepost-warneighborhoods,tracingthe
genesis of the urban tensions opposing different and shifting understanding of the
veryconceptofheritage,architecturalvalue,andplanningdiscipline.
9.2 MateraandtheSouthernQuestioninthePost-War
Period:TheSassiandtheImpactonthePublic
andDisciplinaryDebate
In1945CarloLevi’sdenunciationstoryCristosièfermatoaEboli(Levi1945)was
published,andthesituationoftheSassiofMaterawasbroughttopublicandpolitical
attentioninItaly,becomingthesymboloftheSouthernquestion.Levispeaksabout
hisconfinementbythefascistregimeintheMateraregionandtellsofthediscovery
of the civilization of the peasants of Southern Italy, dedicating about ten pages to
theSassi,wherealmost70%oftheinhabitantsofMaterawerelivinginintolerable
hygienic and sanitary conditions. Levi describes the misery of what he defines as
“thecapitalofruralcivilization”andhighlightsthepresenceoftwoworlds—thatof
the peasants and that of the low middle class—which ran parallel as two separate
universes.Heemphasizestheroleofsolidaritybetweenpeasants’families,speaking

### Page 113

9 TheTwoFacesofMatera:DiachronicNarrativesofChanging… 111
about a “sorrowful beauty”. The novel had a strong impact on the Italian public
opinion and was widely translated, taking the case to a long-lasting national and
international attention (Sacco 1969; Tafuri 1974; Toxey 2011) and fostering the
interestoftheAmericanpublic(DiLena2007).
In 1948, Palmiro Togliatti, head of the Communist Party, visited the Sassi as a
stepofhiselectoralcampaignanddefinedthema“nationalshame,”givingrisetoa
long-lastingstigma.Thefollowingyear,theeconomistNalloMazzocchiAlemanni
presentedtotheU.S.EconomicCooperationAdministrationinItalyareportforthe
solutionoftheproblemoftheSassithroughtheconstructionofresidentialvillages,in
theframeworkofthecurrentlandreclamationandresettlementpolicies(Mazzocchi
Alemanni 1951,1955).In1950,thePrimeMinisterAlcideDeGasperivisitedthe
Sassi of Matera and, in the following months, he charged the Lucanian Minister
EmilioColombowiththestudyofalawtopromotetherehabilitationandthesolu-
tion of the problem of the Sassi.1 The legislative process for their rehabilitation
began,resultingintheSpecialLawfortheevacuationoftheSassiwhichimposed
ontwo-thirdsofthecity’sinhabitants,aboutseventeenthousandpeople,toabandon
theirhomesandmovetothenewdistricts:“TheStateassumestheexpenseforthe
rehabilitationofthedistrictsSassoCaveosoandSassoBarisanoofthetownofMatera
and for the construction of houses particularly suitable for peasants, workers, and
artisans, to replace those currently existing in these districts that will be declared
uninhabitableanddemolished.”2
Starting with a literary denunciation, the case was brought to public attention
andtakenbydifferentpoliticalforcesasanopportunitytowinthefavorofspecific
socialstrataandgroups,inthefirstyearsofRepublicanItaly.Atthesametime,it
wasacrucialcasestudyintheemergingpost-warintellectualandtechnicaldebate
redefiningscopeandtoolsoftheplanningdiscipline,openingtoamorecomprehen-
siveapproachintermsofdesignscale,administrativeprinciples,andsocialconcerns
rooted in the experiences of regional planning, land reclamation and resettlements
(Olmo1992;Tenzon2018;DeDominicis2019;MonicaandBergamaschi2019).

9.4 The“SassiRedevelopment”Neighborhoods:
ArchitectureandPlanningDialoguingwithSocial
Instances
WhileLaMartellawasinprogress,in1953,aDetailedPlanforthecitywasdesigned
bytheplannerLuigiPiccinato,8enchargedbytheMinistryofPublicWorkstoimple-
menttheprovisionsofLaw619/1952.Itincludesthelineamentsoftheso-calledSassi
redevelopmentneighborhoods,whosedesignwasentrustedwithaNationalArchi-
tecturecompetitionwonbyagroupheadedbyCarloAymonino.Thefourneighbor-
hoodsofBorgoVenusio,SpineBianche,SerraVenerdì,andLanerawererealizedin
fewyearsandintendedfordifferentinhabitantsifcomparedtothefirstrealization
ofLaMartella,imaginingaprogressivesocio-economicdevelopmentfrompeasants
tothelow-middleclass,thankstolandreformpolicies.
IntheirdesignemergesthestrongtensionthatpervadedItalianarchitectureand
planning—inparticulartheRomanschool—intheperiodofreconstruction,rooted
intheideathatanewwayofconceivingarchitectureandplanningcouldberealized
starting from the socio-economic necessities of the local communities up to the
definition of neighborhoods that would respond to those needs. A new city for a
new community, redeemed from the label of national shame, was intended as an
unrepeatableopportunityforanewwayofdoingarchitecture.Intheprojects,thisis
expressedbothintheurbanorganizationandinthearchitecturaltypologies,evenif
inavarietyofsolutions:thethemeoftheneighborhoodiscomposedwithservices,
greenery, and the road network. The expressive means are renewed, balancing the
Scandinavian approaches ofEuropeanrationalismwiththelocalinstancesandthe
moderncultureoftheItalianschool(Fig.9.3).
Fortheentiredecadeofthe1950s,inMateraconvergetherelevanceofasocial
problem,significantagrarian,andlandtransformations,andthecontributionofvarie-
gatedavant-gardeculturalexperiencesinthefieldofarchitecture,planning,andsocial
studies, refounding the disciplinary perspectives while integrating and hybridizing
international contributions. The city was defined as the workshop of Italian urban
planning, a celebrated (Gorio 1954; Piccinato 1954; Casabella Continuità 1959)
opportunity for architectural and planning culture to experiment in an interdisci-
plinary perspective oriented to promote a modern approach to housing and urban
design.
At the same time, the Sassi became empty and abandoned, offering a perfect
locationformajorItalianandinternationalfilmproductionsinsearchofhistorical,

TimurBekmambetov’sBenHur in2016wereamongthemasterpiecesthatstirred
thepublicimaginationinthisregard.
9.5 ReconsideringtheSassi:Fromthe1970stoNowadays
In the 1970s, a period of reconsideration of pre-war Matera and the Sassi begins.
StartingwiththeReportonMaterabythegroupIlPolitecnicoin1971(Musacchio
et al. 1971) and the seminal essay by Fabbri (Fabbri 1971), this is “the moment”
(Abbruzzeseetal.1978)fortheSassi:differentactorsworkonhypothesesofrecovery
andrehabilitation,pursuingpathsofanalysisandrethinkingoftheurbanheritagethat
shareaperspectivefromunderdevelopmenttonewideasofthecity,intheframework
ofarenewedinterestforthehistoriccentersintheItaliandebateonarchitectureand
urbanplanningandpolicies.
TheprocessreachedaturningpointwiththeenactmentoftheSpecialLaw771
of 1986,9 allowing the citizens to return to the districts of the Sassi. In 1993, the
Sassi entered the UNESCO World Heritage List as the first site characterized as
culturallandscape,10andthesubsequentManagementPlanurgedtherehabilitation,
redevelopment, and enhancement of the Sassi activating a process of urban and
socio-economicregeneration.
This process culminated in the successful candidacy of Matera as European
Capital of Culture 2019, a long path that began in 2008 involving many local
actorsandsolicitingpublicparticipation.TheECOCplanningandeventspromoted
improvementsintermsofaccessibilityandpublicspacesandtheprogramhasbeen
wideandvaried.Eveniftheheritageofthecitywasnotintegratedintotheinterven-
tionsandnotfullyconsideredtoframetheevents;nonetheless,itabsorbedvisitors’
interest, bringing the Sassi to the attention of the national and international press
(Ponzini et al. 2020) with considerations of value that were completely different
fromtheonesofthe1940s.
9.6 DivergentIdeasoftheCityandtheHeritage
This renewed interest in Matera and its heritage has left in the shade what the

10.2 Ahmedabad:APolitically EmblematicCity
The city of Ahmedabad appears emblematic for the cyclical tensions that have
been generated by the opposition of the Muslim and Hindu communities. From
its origins, this capital attracted communities of different backgrounds, fascinated
by the commercial importance of Ahmedabad within important merchant routes.
Tensions which arose from the contrasts of different cultures were mitigated by a
federal governmental structure on an urban scale (Doshi 2003) within which the
differentgroupscoulddefinetheirownrules.2
From1817,underBritishrule,Ahmedabaddevelopedathrivingindustrialproduc-
tionfocusedoncottonprocessing.Ifinmajorcenters,suchasDelhiandMumbai,this

economy was promoted by the Britishrule, in Ahmedabad the industry developed

independencefromtheEnglishdomainpromotedbylocalentrepreneursdefineda

With the death of Nehru (1964), many of the secular and socialist ideals of the
Congress came into crisis by reaffirming the political role of religious divisions,
whichwerefurtherhighlightedbythefailureoftheindustries.Thereturntocommu-

Thissocio-economiccontextledtothedeclineofsecularNehru’sthought,opening

tobeguiltyoftheeconomicandsocialcrisis.ThisledtothefacingofMuslimand
Hinducommunitiesintheriotsof1969,1985,1992andthemostviolentonein2002
(Spodek2011).
Duringtheseconflicts,Ahmedabadwascharacterizedbyacompletecrystalliza-
tionofthesocialpanoramawithagradualdisappearanceofareasofstrongcultural
mix, and an increase in monocultural quarters. If in the years following indepen-

theMuslimcity,whichconsistsoffoursmallghettotowns(Juhapura,ShahAlam,
RamolandVatva),andtherestofthecityinhabitedbyHindumajority(Mahadevia
etal.2014).

10.3 TypesofBorderintheUrbanFabricofAhmedabad
Inthisfragmentedsocialcontext,thepresentresearchfocusedonthenatureofurban
forms and aimed at understanding whether they reflected the “divided identities”
ofAhmedabad.WithintheurbantissueofAhmedabad,threeborderareasbetween
Muslim and Hindu communities were chosen to represent emblematic conditions.
Notably,aborderisnotalwaysdeterminedinaphysicalway,butratheritisdefined
by a mental construction of the inhabitants. Borders are points of passage which
markastateofdanger,andthusareoftenabandoned.Itisimportanttounderstand
thenatureoftheboundaryanditsidentity,basednotonlyonthephysicalstructureof
theseremarkablepoints,butalsoontheusethattheinhabitantsmakeoftheseplaces
andtheirsocialcomposition(JaffrelotandGayer2012).Thethreeareaschosenfor
thepresentresearcharerepresentativeofdifferentcharacters:oneisplacedwithin
thenewexpansionareasonthewestofAhmedabad;thesecondislocatedwithinthe
industrialareas;thethirdisplacedwithinthehistoricalwallednucleus(theso-called
OldCity).
Amongthenewlyexpandingareas,ShrinandNagarRoadwaschosen,aroadalong
whichthereisaclearpassagefromtheMuslimghetto,thedistrictofJuhapura,toa
district—Vejalpur—with predominant Hindu dominance. Juhapura accommodates
about240,000Muslims,representingaboutthreequartersofthetotalpopulationof
IslamicoriginofAhmedabad,becauseofthissocialconformationthisneighborhood
hasgrownasanautonomouscityitself(Desai2008).Theboundariesofthesetwo
quarters mark a stark division between religious groups, and even more between
groups with different economic status. This contrast is clearly manifested in the
differentresidentialtypologies:theareawitharicherHindumajorityhashighwalls
ofenclosuretoprotectthegatedcommunities,whiletheMuslimquarterhasamore
informal structure and recovers traditional forms by placing commercial activities
onthefronttosafeguardtheresidentialareasbehindthem.
The second focus ison theindustrialarea easttotherailwaylinethathas been
thesceneofthebloodiestriotsbetweenthetwocommunities,asitwasinhabitedby
aheterogeneousworkingpopulationwhohadbeenseverelyaffectedbythefailure
ofthecottonmills(Dasetal.2003).Thisareawaschosentostudyaneighborhood
betweenSaragpurBridgeandAnilSarchRoadpresentingaHinduenclavewithina
Muslimquarter.TheHinducommunitystronglyshowstheneedtoprotectitselffrom
theotherthroughahighenclosurewallandrelatestotherestofthecitythrougha
singleentrance.Thethirdstudycasewillbedescribedinthenextparagraphs,which
focusontheanalysisofthehistoricalcentralareaofAhmedabad.

10.4 TheHistorical NucleusofAhmedabad:ASedimented
SystemofCulturalCohesion
In order to understand the reasons of separation and conflict, it is fundamental to
considerthehistoryoftheheartoftheOldCityofAhmedabad,theareawithinthe
fortifiedhistoricalnucleus.ThedistrictofDariyapurwaschosenasthekeyplaceof
thisstudyanddesignexperimentationbecauseitwasoneoftheareasmostlyaffected
by the riots, housing both Hindu and Muslim communities employed in cotton
Mills (Varshney 2002). Additionally, Ahmedabad’s historical nucleus is unique as
it combines urban forms and settlement strategies that are useful for two different
communitiestocoexist.Thehighdensity,withinthehistoricalnucleusonereachesa
populationdensityof800inhabitantspersquarehectare(MinistryofForeignAffairs
2001),andheterogeneouspopulationhaveledtothedefinitionofacomplexsettle-
ment organization that shows different urban layouts, also present in more recent
urbanextensions.
Theoriginalstructureofthewallednucleusofthecitywasorganizedaccording
toahierarchicalradialroadsystempassingfromwidestreetstoincreasinglynarrow
roads. This simple criterion has been instrumental in giving privacy to different
communities.Thedistributionofthecommercialandreligiousactivitiesofthetwo
communities within the district of Dariyapur defines a clear separation of the two
groupsanddifferentmodalitiesofaggregationofthecorrespondingpublicspaces.
If the collective areas in the Muslim quarter orbit around the mosque placed in
themiddleoftheward,theHinducommunities clearlydivide theintercommunity
meeting places (the markets) from those belonging to the individual communities.
Inside,themosquehostsmorecomplexfunctions,suchasKoranicschools,andplays
animportantroleattheurbanscale.Unlikethemosque,theHindutempleisclosely
linkedtotheinhabitantsofthesingleenclave(Jain1975),notconstitutinganinstitu-
tionalcollectivecenter.TheenclavesoftheindividualMuslimcommunities,unlike
the Hindu settlements, do not have a mosque inside and are distributed along the
ward’saxeswithoutdefiningacleardistinctionbetweenresidentialandcommercial
areas.4Theanalysisofindividualneighborhoods(HinduandMuslim)infactshows
howplacesofworshipareabletobuildtheurbanformofthesettlement(Fig.10.1).
The structure of the road network also reports a different organization of the
twoneighborhoods:thewardswithaHindudominanceusuallyhavethemainroute
placed along the perimeter of the settlement, while areas with Muslim majority
gravitate around a center connected with radial axes to the outer perimeter. This
organization is further underlined by the different positioning of the commercial
areas. If the perimeter of Hindu settlements was further marked by the presence
of commercial activities, Muslim quarters have shops in the central area and near

the entrances. Hindu settlements then concentrate the public activities along the

visibledifferencesaremotivatedbythehistorical-politicaldevelopmentofthecity.
Muslimwardsoriginallyformedaself-standingareabelongingtoprominentperson-
alities of which the sultan was surrounded. These areas, called Puras, constituted
independent settlements, presenting an orthogonal urban organization according
to two central axes, at which intersections were placed institutional buildings as
mosquesandnoblepalaces.TheHinducommunitysettledwithinthewallednucleus
of Ahmedabad, only during the Mughal domination. Due to the constant tensions
with the Islamic community (Theodore 1866), Hindu neighborhoods were struc-
turedaccording tomoreintroverted systemstryingtoprotecttheresidentialareas.
Enclaves belonging to individual communities, called Pol in Gujarati, constitute
thebasicelementoftheurbanstructure.Theorganizationoftheseresidentialunits
appears common in both the Muslim and Hindu communities showing the same
joints.ThePolhasadenseperimeterdefinedbyblindfacadesorcommercialactiv-
ities. Within it, the road network follows a hierarchical order by gradually giving
access to more intimate places. Through a sequence of interior spaces to the pole,
it is possible to access the temple and the common square, called Chowk, around
whichthecommunitylifeorbits.

10 DesignExperimentationinaContextofCulturalDivision… 127
10.5 TheStructureoftheBorder:TheExample
ofDariyapurRoad
AtthecrossingpointofDariyapurRoad,activitiesarerepeatedonbothroadfronts;
thepointsforrickshaw’sdriversareatashortdistanceonbothsidesoftheborder
and drivers follow specific routes to avoid crossing areas belonging to the other
religiouscommunity.AninterestingfeatureoftheborderofDariyapurRoad isthe
specialization of the two communities in separate activities. In Ahmedabad, as in
otherIndiancontexts,itisinfactcommontofinddifferentwaysinwhichthesame
goods are produced and sold (Davis 2006). The Hindu community of Dariyapur
ownsmostoftheshopsandlaboratoriesspecializinginthesaleandproductionof
drums such as Dhol and Tabla, percussion instruments used in both Muslim and
Hindureligiousprocessions.Ontheotherhand,intheMuslimwardthereisastrong
specialization in weaving and sewing of fabrics and clothes. This specialization
allows the definition of a channel through which the two communities manage to
interact.Infact,theCommunitydivisionoflaborallowsHindugroupstofrequent
the Muslim quarter to buy colorful fabrics and prints, crafts in which the Islamic
communities are specialized. In turn, the Muslim communities go to the Hindu
quarterstobuydrumstobeusedduringthereligiousanniversaries(Jain2011).In
additiontotheserelationshipsofcommercialinterdependence,thegrowingprocess
ofurbanizationofIndianmetropolisescontributestothecreationofnewplacesin
whichcommunitiesofdifferentoriginsandreligionsinteract.Publictransportstops
andinfrastructures,likedrinkingwaterdeliverypoints,becomemeetingpointsfor
communities,constitutingfundamentalurbannodes(Krishnaetal.2014).

10.7 FunctionalFlexibility andTransformation
oftheArchitecturalStructure
The continuous change of activity within the pavilions corresponds to a transfor-
mationoftheplanimetricorganization,madepossiblebytheuseofmobilepanels.
Theflexibilityofpublic(andprivate)spacehasbeenrevivedbytraditionalmerchant
houses, called haveli, which have an internal organization capable of adapting to
different functions. The traditional houses are articulated around a central patio,
calledChowk(whichcanbetracedbacktotheideaofacommonpatio),whichover-
look“functional”spacessuchasthekitchen,thevotivechapelandthewaterroom.
Dependingontheneeds,themaindomesticactivitiescanexpandwithinthechowk,
whichcanbecomeaplaceofprayer,adiningroomorareceptionspace(Ray2008).
Theprojectofthefivepavilionsincorporatesthissolution,articulatingtheground
floorinacentralpatioaroundwhichareoverlookingthemainspacesthatcanexpand
theiractivitiesinthesamechowkorinthestreetspace,virtualextensionofdomestic
Indianpublicspace(Fig.10.3).
Thepavilion’sextensionontheroadwidenstheconceptofflexibilitypresented
in the traditional house: the traditional central patio in fact divides the house into
two blocks presenting a more private and a more public area adjacent to the road,
consisting of a portico called otla. The adjoining domestic spaces to this portico
areplacesofrepresentation,filterspacestobetterprotecttheinnermostpartsofthe
house. As emerged in the description of the structure of the city, the urban fabric,
tobetter accommodate populations of different origins,determines net transitions,
defining areas clearly belonging to the individual communities. The structure of
thepavilionsincorporatestherelationshipbetweentheatriumandthepublicspace,
presentingathreshold-diaphragmadjacenttotheroadabletocompletelytransform

Chapter 11
Resisting Gentrification by Reclaiming
a Work–Life Balance. An Exercise
of Research by Design in Brussels
VerenaLennaandLuisAntonioMartinSanchez
Abstract The contents of this chapter are based on the exercise of design devel-
opedintheframeworkofthemasterclassDesigningBrusselsEcosystems,organised
by Metrolab Brussels. The masterclass explored the concept of urban ecosystem,
by looking at the Brussels Capital Region (BCR) through four lenses: agriculture,
density, work and circularity. Together with other participants, we focused on the
topicofworkbyexploringtheurbanecosystemsofthreedifferentcasesstudiesin
descriptive and designerly terms. In the three cases, former vacant and manufac-
turingspaceshavebeenrenovatedanddevelopedbydifferentactors:Recyclart,an
organisation that combines cultural and creative activities with work-based inser-
tion programmes; Smart LaVallée, a cooperative aimed at supporting small scale
entrepreneurial activities, free-lance professionals and other precarious workers;
and Zinneke, a centre for artistic production that operates by involving available
spacesandactorsacrossthewholeBrusselsRegion.

11.1 Introduction
The contents of this chapter are based on the exercise of design developed in the
frameworkofthemasterclassDesigningBrusselsEcosystems,organisedbyMetrolab
Brussels.1 Themasterclassexploredtheconceptofurbanecosystem,bylookingat
the Brussels Capital Region (BCR) through four lenses: agriculture, density, work
and circularity. Together with other participants, we focused on the topic of work
by exploring the urban ecosystems of three different cases studies in descriptive
and designerly terms. In the three cases, former vacant and manufacturing spaces
have been renovated and developed by different actors: Recyclart, an organisation
combining cultural and creative activities with work-based insertion programmes;
SmartLaVallée,acooperativeaimedatsupportingsmallscaleentrepreneurialactiv-
ities, free-lance professionals and other precarious workers; and Zinneke, a centre
forartisticproductionthatoperatesbyinvolvingavailablespacesandactorsacross
thewholeBrusselsCapitalRegion.
Situated in different spots along the post-industrial corridor of Brussels, where
poverty,unemployment,immigrantsandtheyoungestindividualsconcentrate,these
cases have the potential to engender gentrification processes. On the other hand,
the kind of urban ecologies possibly generated by these initiatives could in fact
contributetoresistgentrification,underspecificconditionsthatthedesignexercise
allowedustoexploreinprojectiveterms.Howcouldaprojectofarchitecturalrenova-
tionimprovethelivingconditionsofaneighbourhood,whileresistinggentrification
and the collateral expulsion and privatisation processes? This chapter will present
the reflections fuelled by the design exercise we did during the masterclass, as a
researchandenvisioningpractice.
Wewillfirstdescribethethreecases.Foreachofthemweidentifiedacoreconcept
that seemed to coherently be at work both in spatial and in organisational terms.
Movingfromthearchitecturaltotheurbanscale,inallthethreecasestheleading
actors are aware of the spatial and urban impact of the activities they organise.
In particular, Zinneke has an explicitly ecologic and ecosystemic approach to the
organisation of their environment and their activities, and their understanding of
governance aims to involve the whole neighbourhood and surrounding actors. By
referringtothiscase,theexerciseofdesignallowedtoreflectonthetensionbetween
potential expulsion processes and the authentic will of the observed artistic and
creativepracticestoreclaimtheemancipatoryvalueofawork-lifebalanceandthe
relatedurbanecosystem.Wearguethatthewayoutofthedilemmaisbyredesigning
thegovernanceoftheconcernedneighbourhoods.Conceivedfromtheperspectiveof
anecosystemicapproach,urbanregenerationandarchitecturalrenovationprocesses
could play a crucial role, by triggering the appropriation of local resources by the
concernedcommunitieswhilemakingpossibleandsubstantiatingtherecognitionof
1MetrolabBrusselsisatransdisciplinaryandinter-universitylaboratoryforappliedandcritical

Thegraphicsandpicturesinthischapterwererealisedandcollectedforandwithintheframework

11.2 AMagnet,ABubble,AProjectofDoors
The three initiatives described in the following paragraphs are the expression of
anemancipatoryapproachtowork,workconditionsandlife,experimentingonthe
expression of the self and new forms of social protection. The spaces they shape
reflectandmakepossibletheirgoalsandattitudes.
Recyclartisamulti-purposecentreforculturalandartisticproduction,anatelier
for the creation of wood and metal artefacts called Fabrik along with a restaurant.
The cultural centre was established in 1997, by occupying one of the secondary,
rarely used railway stations in Brussels, La Chappelle. For twenty years, the site
hostedavarietyofculturalprogrammesandartisticprojects,implementedincollab-
orationwiththeRegion.Asaresult,thisneglectedpartofthecityenjoyedavibrant
atmospherethatcontributedtothepreventionofcrime,whileofferingsomeformof
supporttolocalhomelessandmarginalisedindividualswhoreliedonthesolidarity
and sympathy of the artists and organisers. Today the whole area surrounding the
stationisaffectedbyagentrificationprocessandlandvaluehasrisen.Notfarfrom
La Chappelle Station, Bar Recyclart is a restaurant where precarious and fragile
individuals—becauseoftheirincome,lifepath,educationorhealthissueshavethe
chancetotakeparttoprogrammesofwork-basedinsertion,thatarefundedbythe
Region.Attheircore,workisconsideredtobethefirststeptowardsemancipation.
Similarly,atFabrikpeoplecanlearnhowtocraftmetalandwood.Atthebeginning
of2018,aftermorethantwentyyearsofsuccessfulartisticandculturalproduction,
SNCBandInfrabel,thecompaniesinchargeofthefunctioningandthemanagement
ofthestation,askedRecyclarttoquittheplace,forsafetyissuesrelatedtothereno-
vationworkstheywereintendingtostart.Followingthemobilisationofcivilsociety
andtheinterventionofafewpoliticians,anewsiteforRecyclartwasidentifiedinthe
municipalityofMolenbeek:aformertypographyinRuedeManchester,comprised
of a few buildings and an interior courtyard. As a magnet, attracting people from
any area of the city, Recyclart will operate as a centre of artistic production and
emancipationinamunicipalityseverelyaffectedbytheendoftheindustrialeraand
stigmatisedasdangerousandpoor(Fig.11.1).
TheestablishmentofLaValléeasaco-workingspacewasmadepossiblethanks
totheinitiativeofSmart,oneofthebiggestcooperativesinEuropethatprovidesnot
only co-working spaces, but also training programmes and juridical and financial
assistance to precarious workers and entrepreneurs in the creative industry. While
maintainingfriendlyrelationshipswiththeneighbourhood,LaValléeisanintrovert
place,abubbleofprotection,offeringalternativespacesforencounterandexchange

11.3 Individuals,BeyondIndividualism:SpatialandUrban
DimensionsofanEmancipatoryApproachtoWork
Thoughatthismomentthethreeobservedinitiativesmayhaveamarginalimpact,
they indeed represent an innovative approach to work, which resonates with what
waspresentedanddiscussedintheframeworkofthedesignbiennaleofSaintEtienne,
in2017,WorkingPromesse—LesMutationsdutravail.3Whatwasshowcasedthere,
was the possibility for individuals to reclaim themselves through their work and
creativepractices,thusmovingfromaconditionofdispossessionoftheself(Castel
andHaroche2001)andhyper-fragmentationoftheworkcapacities—oratomisation,
in the words of (Berardi 2004, 2009)—to a condition in which they could reclaim
asenseofmeaning,authorshipandcapability(Gorz1988).Intheframeworkofsuch
anemancipatoryapproach,workisnotopposedtolife:workislife.

11.4 AProjectofDoors
FocusingonZinneke’sprojectofdoors,weimaginedwhatkindofecosystemcould
begeneratedbyadifferentapproachtowork,manufacturingand—inparticular,in
thecaseofZinneke—theuseofneighbourhood-basedresourcesandspatialpotential.
Theexerciseshowedhowdesigncouldplayacrucialrole—amongotherthings—in
establishinganempoweringgovernancefortheconcernedcommunities.
Aspreviouslymentioned,theprojectofdoorswasdefinedassuchbythemembers
ofZinneketostresstherelevanceofdoorsasdeviceswhichallowthecoexistence
withinthesamebuildingandatthesametimeofactivitiesimplyingverydifferent
needs, for example in terms of safety, logistics, scale and externalities. A door is
what deals with and allows the compatibilities and incompatibilities of activities:
it merges or divides spaces, regulates accessibility while taking care of safety or
privacyissuesandtransformsspacesbyinterveningintheirporosityandflexibility,
accordingtochangingneedsanddesires.Aprojectofdoorswouldallowdifferent
activities to not simply coexist, but also to establish collaborations. More ambi-
tiously,itshouldalsomakepossibletheappropriationofthebuildingbyneighbours
and non-local actors, who could enjoy the use of the building without interfering
5Data can be verified at http://monitoringdesquartiers.brussels/. Many scholars and researchers

while mentioning the seminal socio-geographic investigations of Christian Kesteloot, since the

withtheneedsofthepermanentactivities.TheaspirationofZinnekehowever,goes
beyond use and accessibility: an effective project of doors should enable a shared
governance, which means not only a right to decide, but also shared responsibil-
ities and shared care. Therefore, appropriation and accessibility would imply the
responsibilisationandtheengagementoftheconcernedusers,whoshouldacquire
the capacities to properly maintain their resources. A project of doors would thus
provideanoccasiontoincreasetheawarenessandexpertiseofusersconcerningthe
governanceoftheirassets,thusempoweringthemandideallycreatingtheconditions
necessary to recognise their contributions to the liveability and quality of a place.
Andallegedly,theirpowertodecideaboutit.Whiletheprojectofdoorsdescribedby

thewholeneighbourhood.Whatifthatconceptcouldapplytothewholeneighbour-
hood,asacontinuationofthespatialarticulationscreatedwithinthebuildingwalls?
What if the idea of a shared governance concerned other buildings, vacant plots
and assets in the neighbourhood? In such a scenario, local actors could gradually
appropriatenotonlytheformerimprimerie,butinfactthewholeneighbourhood.By
increasingtheirexpertiseandcapacitiestogoverntheirlocalresources,theycould

avoiceintheirgovernance.AsHenryGeorge(George1883)andEbenezerHoward
(Howard1902)pointedoutinthepast,theproblemwithprivatisationandspeculation
istheappropriationbyveryfewindividualsofcollectivelygeneratedplusvalueand
resources.

The design exercise allowed the imagining of the kind of urban ecosystem
thatcouldbegeneratedbyacooperativeapproachtowork,basedonsharedresponsi-
bilitiesaswellassharedrights,centredonrebalancingworkandlife,individuallyand
collectively,asembryonicallyexperimentedwithinthewallsoftheformerprinting
house. We explored how existing actors could support the emergence of such an
ecosystem. We reshaped and reprogrammed vacant plots and buildings in order to
makepossiblethecoexistenceandhybridisationofdifferentactivitiesandasustain-
able governance of resources, the latter meant to be the socio-spatial assets of the
wholeneighbourhood.
WithreferencetothemodusoperandiandphilosophyofZinneke,weidentified
three actions that we deployed as narratives for specific parts of the neighbour-
hood,basedontheirsocio-spatialpotentials:tonegotiate,toperformandtoinvade.
We explored the occasions for negotiation along Parc de la Senne, imagining how
an alternative market could be organised by involving local ateliers and commu-
nitykitchengardens,notonlytoselltheirproducts,butalsototriggertheengage-
mentandtheinterestoftheinhabitantsinlocal,community-basedactivitiesandthe
concernedspacesandresources.WeinterpretedtheactofperforminginPlaceMasui
bysuggestinghowartisticeventscouldbringnewlifetothesquare,workingbeyond
thewallsoftheimprimerie,redefiningthetransitionfromonespacetotheother—
the private-collective, the semi-public and the public-allowing local inhabitants to
appropriatethem.Forthethirdprogramme,weimaginedinvadingtheCanal’sbanks
withunusualactivitiessuchaspromenadesandfestivals,thusguidingandencour-
agingthelocalcommunitytoknowandtoappropriatethispartofthecity,pavingthe
wayforcollaborationsandworkinsertionprogrammes.Forexamples,therecycling
ofmetalwaste,thecoremissionofoneoftheexistingcompanies,couldinterweave
withtheactivitiesofZinneke,whilereactivatinglocalvacantspacesforanyphase
oftheprocessandforrelatedcreativeapplications.
Through these narratives, the urban ecosystem of a whole neighbourhood is
reshaped, with a different system of values concerning work and the environment
that could support it at its core, centred on the empowerment of the individual as
partofacommunityendeavour.Individualsandcollectiveswouldbeencouragedto
experimentbecauseimmersedinacooperatingecosystemthatcouldprovidemate-
rialsupport,expertiseandinspiration.Suchanapproachtoworkwouldrequireand
engenderasharedgovernanceofthespacesandtheresourcesservingtheconcerned
communities.Asthisisrequiredfortheveryexistenceandthesustainabilityofthat
ecosysteminthelongrun,theprotectionofthoseassetswouldhavetobeapriority
(Fig.11.4).
From an operational point of view, those programmes could be progressively
realised through a combination of permanent and temporary activities (Fig. 11.5).

(Lenna2019).Meantasaframeworkforco-creation,infactdesignwouldbefunda-
mental for the identification of the concerned communities and the emergence of
the socio-spatial synergies at the core of the emancipatory ecosystems of work-
as-life described above. On a strategic level, it would allow the involved actors to
voicetheirneeds,butalsotobecomeawareofthelocallyandnon-locallyavailable
resources and to discuss the strategies required for their optimal maintenance. On
a spatial level, design is what would allow the envisioning of the morphological
conditionsfosteringexchangeandcollaboration,thecoexistenceofcompatibleand
incompatibleactivitiesandtheestablishmentofahorizontalformofgovernance.
11.5 Conclusion
Designed around the narrative of an emancipatory approach to work as life, over-
coming fragmentation and alienation, the ecosystem described above intends to
suggests that improving the living conditions of a neighbourhood does not neces-
sarilyhavetoengendergentrificationandexpulsions,evenwhenbasedonartisticand
culturalpractices.Theimplementationofahorizontalapproachtogovernancewould
allow not only the retention of the collectively produced value, but also the redis-
tributionofit,whileempoweringandrecognisingthecontributionoftheconcerned
communities: local and non-local, occasional users and inhabitants, community-
basedorganisationsandadministrativeactors.ThemodeloftheCLTproposedhereis
justoneamongmanyotherpossibleoptionswhichenabletheachievementofsimilar
results.Onaspatiallevel,suchaformofgovernancerequiresanarchitecturaland
urbanprojectaddressingtheporosityofspace,theregulationofaccessibilityandthe
distributionofresponsibilities:aprojectofdoors,makingpossiblethecoexistence
ofdiverse,compatibleandconflictingactivitiesandtheprotectionoftheconcerned
resourcesinthelongrun.Asaresult,neighbourhoodslivingunderthethreatoftwo
opposites,eitherneglectorgentrification,couldbereappropriated,underthepretext
offuellingandsupportingcommunity-basedeconomies.

Chapter 12
Reconfiguring the Neoliberal City: Three
Stories of Urban Pioneers in Berlin
NataliaKvitkovaandLorenzaManfredi
Abstract The availability of urban voids that have characterized Berlin’s urban
landscapesincetheSecondWorldWaruntiltheearly2000shasemergedasatradition
of spontaneous appropriation of space. This shift in discourse, promoted by the
city senate and the economic growth, has been the focus of Berlin’s more recent
urban development. It created completely different conditions, where competition
foravailablelandhasbecomemoreandmoreacute.Thenewpressureontheland
hasmadeitincreasinglydifficultforurbanentrepreneurs,who,inordertomaintain
projectsstartedinthe1990sand2000sortobeginnewones,havehadtoelaborate
strategiesandreconfigurethemselvesaccordingtothecompetitivepositioningand
maximization of value imposed by neoliberalism logics. Our observation of the
threecasestudiesofHolzmarkt,RAWGeländeandHausderStatistik analysesthe
transformationsandstrategiesthaturbanpioneershadtodevelopinordertosurvive
withtheirprojectsinrelativelycentralareasofthecityofBerlin.
· · ·
Keywords Berlin Neoliberalcities Autonomousprojects Reconfiguring
temporariness
12.1 TheNewPressureonLandHinderstheTraditional
SpontaneityofBerlin’sAppropriationInitiatives
SincetheSecondWorldWar,morphologicalvacancyandemptinesshavebeenamong
thedefiningfactorsofBerlin’surbanspace,capturingtheinterestsandimagination
ofobservers.
TheexperienceoftheBerlinervoids(Huyssen1997)hasstimulatedtheemergence
of numerous narratives of a city that is constantly in flux, undefined and ready to
accommodatethevaryingneedsofitscitizens.Thesheerabundanceoflargetracts
ofvacantwastelandwereuniqueforaEuropeancapital;theresultsoftheinterplay
ofdiversephenomenaspecifictotheBerlincontext.

ofspaceavailableforeventualappropriation,togetherwiththenegligenceofacity

in its development according to their own personal contribution and taste. Around
thebeginningofthe2000s,someindicatorspointedtoeconomicgrowthinthecity.
From2002to2015,tourismswelledandthenumberofovernightstaysdoubled.Real
estate purchase prices increased by 120% between 2004 and 2019, making Berlin
thecitywiththehighestgrowthrateofrealestatepricesintheworldin2017,which
recordeda+20.5%thatyearalone.
The establishment of a neoliberal logic was facilitated by—and, in turn, facili-
tated—processesofgentrificationandprivatisationofmunicipalrealestate,creating
an ever greater strain on the housing market and on the socio-spatial fabric of the
city. The new pressure on the land has made it increasingly difficult for urban
entrepreneurs,who,inordertomaintaintheprojectsstartedinthe1990sand2000s
or to begin new ones, have had to elaborate strategies and reconfigure themselves
according to the competitive positioning and maximization of value imposed by
neoliberalismlogics.OurobservationofthethreecasestudiesofHolzmarkt,RAW

River,takingoveranabandonedfactoryunderthesametemporaryuseterms.The
group was evicted from this second location in 2014, again due to redevelopment

Whentheassociationrealisedthatthelandonwhichtheyhadinitiallystartedthe
Bar25 had been put up for sale by the city administration, they decided to devise
a concept to deliver to the district as an alternative approach to redevelopment,
revitalisingtheiroriginalpremisesintoanewquartier.
Their aim was to “build a new city within the city and to make it open to the
publicasfaraspossible”.6Itwouldpromotetemporaryusestructuresfortenantsin
a bid to support emerging businesses while also ensuring a diversity of users and,
mostimportantly,keepaccesstotheriverbanksopentothepublic.Theyproposed
aleaseholdforuseoftheirformerspacetothedistrict,butthecitycouncildecided
not to favour the proposal, as it would have been possible if they had understood

decided to sell it to the highest bidder. The Bar25 initiators began to look for a

helpoftheSwisspensionfundAbendrot.Theirintereststemmedfromtheproposed
social benefit of the project which also met their criteria of a sustainable-oriented
investment.Abendrotboughtthe18,000squaremetresoflandin2012for10million
euro.

anddevelopers.ThereisalsotheMörchenparkassociationwhoinitiallyorganized

12.5 StrategiesofReconfiguration
Manyoftheactorsoriginallyinvolvedinthetemporaryusesceneattheendofthe
90s and beginning of the 2000s have been a key in defining use and development
ofurbanprojectsinsomeofthemostattractiveareaswithinthecentralcityrailway
ring. However, this required the professionalisation of those actors, as the result
of accumulated experience. Their communications with politicians and investors
haveoftenbeenunclearandconflicted,requiringahighlevelofknowledgeoftheir
ownrightsandahighdoseofperseveranceandcunning:manyoftheenergiesand
thevaluesproducedbytheZwischennutzer havebeenexploitedforcitymarketing
and profit making interests by policy-makers and real estate developers, resulting
in the threat of the very meaning of their existence or in their physical eviction
(Amongothers:Colomb2012a,b;Peck2005).Withstandingthesechallengesand
the increasing competition on available land required the development of survival
strategiesandeventuallysetthemuptonegotiatethepossibilityofcooperation.In
somesuccessfulcases,thishasevenledtotheelaborationofnewideasforpossible
setsofdialogicpartnerships,asintheongoingexperimentofHausderStatistik.
In the case of RAW Gelände, the actors who initiated the temporary use of the
derelictareaoperatedunderademocraticideal,leavingcooperationuptoindivid-
uals.Thisresultedinalackoftransparencyandmistrustamongtheusersoftheland.
They acted without a long-term strategy which enormously impacted their agency
onthesite.Nevertheless,whentheconfrontationwiththeownerssoftenedslightly
thankstotheadventoftheKurthGroup,theuserswereabletoasserttheirproposals
for the buildings of the soziokulturelle L. The persistence of these buildings dedi-
catedtoculturalandsocialuseprovidesahighqualityofferinoneoftheareasof
Friedrichshain-Kreuzbergmostaffectedanddisfiguredbythearrivalofaggressive
tourismandbytheprocessesofgentrification.Themediationandsupportofinsti-
tutionsatalocallevel,whichinturnhavefollowedthedevelopmentoftheproject
overtheyears,provedcrucialintheirsuccesslinkingupwiththeGSE,whichshould
fillthemainorganisationalgapsoftheassociationandprovideamoretransparent
controlfunctionoftheusesofcommongood.
For the actors involved in Holzmarkt, we can recognize how strategies of
networking and mutual aid allowed the creation of the base capital and the skills
necessarytobecompetitiveaccordingtothelogicofthemarket.“Weneverbrought
inknowledge,neverboughtexperts,butwehadM.H.whowasoneoftherepresen-

but he got out of this because he didn’t want to work in such a capitalist environ-

Chapter 13
Segregating Infrastructure:
The Nazareth Border-Road
GabrielSchwake
Abstract Walls,fencesandbarriersareessentialcomponentsofethnicallydivided
cities, forming an integral part of contested areas and their landscape. Whether in
Berlin,theUS–Mexicoborder,Ceuta,PalestineorKorea,separationinfrastructure
constitutes a physical materialisation of political and ethnic conflicts, reproducing
them through built space. In the case of Israel, one might immediately think of
the West-Bank Separation Barrier, the Gaza Strip land obstacle, or the fences and
entrancegatesthatdecoratemostoftheIsraelisettlements.Nevertheless,alongside
these usual martial techniques, roads have been functioning as a complementary
mechanism,whichcontinues,enhancesandservestheovertterritoriallanddevelop-
ment policy. However, while the use of segregated bypass roads in the West-Bank
creates parallel and ethnically segregated networks, inside official Israeli territory
theyfunctionastheborderitself,preventingintegrationwhilehighlightingthespatial
stratificationwhichthelocalsocietyisbasedupon.
· · ·
Keywords Border-road Israel/Palestine Spatialsegregation
Counter-integration
13.1 Introduction
Walls, fences and barriers are essential components of ethnically divided cities,
forminganintegralpartofcontestedareasandtheirlandscape.WhetherinBerlin,the
US-Mexicoborder,Ceuta,PalestineorKorea,separationinfrastructureconstitutesa
physicalmaterialisationofpoliticalandethnicconflicts,reproducingthemthrough
built space. In the case of Israel, one might immediately think of the West-Bank
Separation Barrier, the Gaza Strip land obstacle, or the fences and entrance gates
that decorate most of the Israeli settlements. Nevertheless, alongside these usual
martial techniques, roads have been functioning as a complementary mechanism,
whichcontinues,enhancesandservestheovertterritoriallanddevelopmentpolicy

(Lein2004).However,whiletheuseofsegregatedbypassroadsintheWest-Bank
creates parallel and ethnically segregated networks, inside official Israeli territory,
theyfunctionastheborderitself,preventingintegrationwhilehighlightingthespatial
stratificationwhichthelocalsocietyisbasedupon.
Thischapterfocusesonroute75,namedtheZionismroad,theroadthatsimul-
taneously divides and connects the Upper [Jewish] and Lower [Arab] Nazareth.
Designed as a multi-lane freeway, it forms a local bypass road that redistributes
traffic inside the Nazarene metropolitan area, serving drivers entering and exiting
both the thousands-years-old Arab city and the new Jewish settlement of Upper
Nazareth,whichwasbuiltin1954.1 Laidalongthemunicipalborderbetweenboth
towns,itconstitutesaspatialbarrier,highlightingtheethnic-basedspacestratification
thatcharacterisesthelocalbuiltenvironment;enforcingvelocityanddiscontinuity
whileemphasisingsegregationandexclusion.WhiletheliteratureonIsraelibypass
roadsintheoccupiedWest-Bankhasalreadydiscussedtheuseofcivilinfrastructure
as territorial tools (Weizman 2007), route 75 is located inside Israel proper, high-
lighting the spatial order between the two main groups of citizens, Jews and their
Arabcompatriots—thePalestinianCitizensofIsrael(PCI).Therefore,thischapter
arguesthatroute75followssimilarpatternsofethnic-basedsegregation,yetnotas
partofaparallelspatialnetwork,butratherasaterritorialbarrierintendedtoprevent
integrationandtoreproducetheexistingsocialandpoliticalorder.Similarly,Pullan
etal.havealreadyshownhowthatRoad1functionsasaborderinsidethegreater
Jerusalemmetropolitan area thatdivides the cityintoPalestinian and Jewishareas
(Pullanetal.2007).Thischapter,ontheotherhand,showshowtheroadfunctionsas
anextraterritorialentitywhichreliesonatrinityofseparation,controlandencounter,
creatinganexemptedspatialunitbetweenbothethnicgroups.Therefore,analysing
route75asanaxisofcontrol,separationandencounter,thischapterillustratesthe
manner in which it forms a consecutive extraterritorial land barrier, turning into a
border-road.Thus,thischapterexplainshowoneofthemostcrucialaspectsofurban
life,transportationandmobility,isusedtoenhancespatialdominanceandtoprevent
thecreationofanintegratedJewish–Arabeverydaylife.
13.1.1 IsraeliSpaceStratification
Asasiteofaterritorialturfbattle,thedevelopmentofthebuiltenvironmentinIsrael-
Palestine correlates with the desire to create a national-spatial unity. It is usually
commontoidentifythebeginning ofthisterritorialapproach withthefirstZionist
settlements in Palestine during the late nineteenth century, which were part of the
Jewish national renaissance, and formed the initial steps towards an independent
Jewishstate(Schwake2021).Gradually,theideaofsecuringspatialcontrolthrough
settlementturnedintothemostpracticaltoolintendedtopromoteafuturesovereign
1In2019themunicipalitychangedthenametoNofHaGalil.Inthischapterwewillbeusingthe
nameUpperNazarethinstead.

### Page 162

13 SegregatingInfrastructure:TheNazarethBorder-Road 163
political entity. This approach continued to develop after the establishment of the
State of Israel in 1948, as the young government launched a new strategic plan
intended to disperse the local Jewish population into a hierarchal setting of rural
settlementsanddevelopmenttowns,meanttosecurethenewlyformedbordersand
the state’s control over depopulated Palestinian towns and villages (Sharon 1951).
Therationalebehindallocatingnewsettlementsitesreliedonthreemainaspects—
“Hesech”, which is the “scarcity of Jewish settlement [in the area]’, “Havira”, the
possibilityofcreating‘interconnectionsbetweenJewishsettlements”and“Hayetz”,
thepotentialofpromoting“separationbetweenArabarea”(Benvenisti1984).The
ideawasthereforetocreateaconsecutive Jewish-Israeliterritorialsequencewhile
dismantling all possible Arab-Palestinian connections. This approach was applied
inthepre-statehoodyears,inthefirstdecadesfollowingtheestablishmentofIsrael
andintheWest-Bankfollowingitsoccupationin1967.
ThelocallogicofspacestratificationisapplieddifferentlyintheWest-Bankand
in Israel proper. The literature on Israeli settlements in the West-Bank is vast and
multifaceted,andtheabilityoftheIsraeliregimetocreateasegregatedspatialsystem
that simultaneously excludes and isolates the local Palestinians, who are deprived
of political rights, has been thoroughly studied (Weizman 2006, 2007). However,
whileintheWest-BankthePalestiniansocietyisleftoutsideofthepoliticalsphere,
enablingIsraeltoexpand“politicalpowerwithoutthefoundationofbodypolitics”,
(Arendt2017)insidethepre-1967thesituationisquitedifferent.UnlikethePales-
tinianslivingintheoccupiedWest-BankandtheGazastrip,thosewhoresideinside
officialIsraeliterritoryareconsideredasfullIsraelicitizens.Remaininginsidethe
newJewishstatein1948,theyareusuallyreferredtoasIsraeliArabsor48-Arabs,
or Palestinian Citizens of Israel [PCI]. They comprise around 20% of the Israeli
population and are formally entitled to the same rights as Jewish citizens. There-
fore,officially,theyareanintegralpartoftheIsraelibodypolitics,yetpractically,
they are continuously weakened, politically, economically, culturally and spatially
(Said1979).Accordingly,thedevelopmentofnewsettlementsinsidethepre-1967
borderwasintendedtoservetheJewishsector,whileexcludingthePCI(Yiftachel
2006). At the same time, the mid-1980s have witnessed a gradual immigration of
PCImiddle-classfamiliestoJewishtownsandsettlements,whereoneenjoysbetter
living standards. Consequently, Jewish localities began enacting selective criteria,
such as admission committees (Schwake 2020a, b, c, d), while highlighting their
nationalZionistcharacterandemphasisingspatialseparation(Schwake2020a,b,c,
d).Therefore,whiletheIsraelisettlementpolicyinthepre-1967resembledtheone
intheOccupiedTerritories,itcouldnotusecivilinfrastructureasameanstocreate
aseparatedspatialJewishnetworkbutrathertopreventintegrationbydevelopingan
isolatedextraterritorialborder-roadbetweenPCIandJewishbuiltspace.

### Page 163

164 G.Schwake
13.1.2 TheSuperiorandInferiorNazareth
Upper and Lower Nazareth are two adjacent localities in northern Israel which
demonstratethelocalethnic-basedmodelofspacestratification.Historically,thecity
ofNazarethisknownforitsreligiousconnotationandnotduetoitspoliticalimpor-
tance.BeingtherenownedhometownofJesus,until1948ithousedasmall,mainly
Christian,Arabcommunity,withseveralmonasteriesandotherchurchinstitutions,
and it was probably the Christian affiliation that spared Nazareth from a similar
destiny like other Arab localities nearby. With the main Palestinian cities inside
Israelbeingdepopulatedfromtheirinhabitantsduringthe1948War(Khalidi1992),
NazarethturnedintothelargestArablocalityinsidetheyoungJewishstate.Gradu-
ally,itwitnessedasharppopulationgrowth,becomingpredominantlyMuslim,while
turningintothepoliticalandculturalcentreofthePCIcommunity (Morris2004).
TocontrolArabNazareth,theyoungIsraeligovernmentpromotedtheconstruction
ofaJewishtowninitsoutskirts,intendedtoenhancethestate’sspatialcontroland
to dominate this predominantly Arab area. Named Upper Nazareth (Natzrat Illit),
it was located on the hills surrounding Lower Arab Nazareth. Accordingly, it was
designedto“demonstratestatesovereigntytotheArabpopulation”(Forman2006),
andasclaimedbyPrimeMinisterBenGurion,withthe“Arabcommunitylocatedin
thevalley”andtheJewishsettlementbeingplaced“onthemountains”,thestatewill
enjoy “an enormous military advantage” (Yozgof-Orbach and Soffer 2016). Thus,
formingaphysicalmanifestationofIsraelipowerrelationsandthespatiallogicof
dominationtheyrelyon.
Route 75 is the contact point between both Nazareths. Its first signs appeared
in the early 1940s, as part of the new road system for the city, yet its outline was
acontinuationoftheinternalstreetnetworkandnotabypassroad(Kendal1941).
ThisdidnotchangealsointhefirstplansforUpperNazareth,astheaccessroadto
thenewsettlementpassedthroughthehistoricalArabtown.Nevertheless,withthe
reorganisationoftheregionalinfrastructureintheareaduringtheearly1970s,the
road was diverged, creating an easier east–west connection from Tiberias to Haifa
through the Nazarene metropolitan area, yet without having to pass through town.
Consequently,UpperNazarethenjoyedadirectaccessroadwhichbypassedLower
Nazareth (Israel Planning Administration 1976), physically separating both towns
whilehighlightingthedivisionbetweenthem(Fig.13.1).Correspondingly,thispart
ofroute75wasnamedtheZionismroad,emphasisingtothosewhomighthavenot
yetunderstoodtheobjectivebehinditsconstructionandtherulingorderthatdictated
itsoutline.
13.1.3 AxisofSeparation
As a border, the first objective of route 75 is to create a division between Lower
[Arab]andUpper[Jewish]Nazareth.Itslocationanddesignarethereforeanintegral

clear, this slowly began to change during the late 1970s, when PCI families from

between both localities (Fig. 13.2). Moreover, while drivers from Lower Nazareth

The two-dimensional separation became three dimensional as theheight differ-
encesbetweenbothtownswereintegratedintothespatialsegregationsystemalong

thephysicalseparationdidnotpreventadditionalPCIfamiliesfrommovingtoUpper

ateachofthetown’sentrances.This,asexplainedbyMayorGapso,wasintendedto
makeclearto“anyonewhocomesfromthe[Arab]villages(…)thatheisenteringa

JewishfastingdayofYomKippurwhenamongotherthisoneissupposedtorefrain
from driving. To prevent Arab drivers from entering Upper Nazareth during this
day,thelocalmunicipalityandIsraeliPolicedeploylarge-scaleforcesandaseries
ofcheck-postsandroadblocks,completelysealingtheJewishNazarethofffromits
Arabsurroundings.Separatingtwoentitiesanddecoratedwithanationalflaginall
intersections, route 75 forms a clear border, including several characteristics that
correspondwithinternationalcrossingpoints.Yettofullyoperateasaborder,route
75neededotherfunctions,suchascontrolandencounter.

13 SegregatingInfrastructure:TheNazarethBorder-Road 167
13.2 AxisofControl
Besidesitsroleinmaintainingseparation,route75retainsthepowerrelationsofa
superiorandinferiorNazareth.Followingthetopographicallines,theroadproceeds
with the three-dimensional aspects of simultaneous separation and dominance by
preservingthegeographicalsupremacyofUpperNazareth.Furthermore,asanarea
dividingbetweenthosewhocontrolandthosewhoarecontrolled,theroadincludes
severalfunctionsthatcorrespondwiththisroleofsupervision.Accordingly,already
intheearlierschemes,architectsIdelsonandSharonlocatedthegovernmentalbuild-
ingsalongtheroad,betweenbothlocalitiesandonahilltopthatoverlooksanddomi-
nates Lower Nazareth (Sharon and Idelson 1955, 1956). Over the years, this area,
referredtoasKiryatHaMemshala(meaningthegovernmentalcompound),included
thebuildingsofthemainstateinstitutionsthatareinchargeoflaw,landsandcivil
registration (Eran Mabel Architects 2002). These institutions are the Israel Land
Administration, the Ministry of Interior Affairs and the District Courts. The Israel
Land Administration (ILA) was established by the State of Israel in 1960 for the
purposeofmanagingallstate-ownedlands,themajorityofwhichwereconfiscated
from their previous Arab owners. With its role in promoting the establishment of
new[Jewish]settlementsattheexpenseofArablands,theILAistheembodiment
of the Israeli land policy and one of the main facilitators of the national territo-
rial project.2 The Ministry of Interior is the governmental body in charge of civil
registration,citizenshipandresidencyanduntiltheearly2000s,alsostate-ledplan-
ning,whiletheDistrictCourtisinchargeofdefendingandapplyingIsraelilawon
the area. Therefore, combining land, people and law, the governmental compound
represents the aspects of civilian control and the establishment’s ability to enforce
itssovereigntyoverspacelocatedonahilltopalongthedividinglinebetweenboth
towns,thegovernmentalcompoundenhancestheroleofroute75asaborder-road,
turningitintoanaxisofcontrol.
The topographical dominance and control functions were enhanced by other
complementaryusesandlaterevenarchitecturaldesign.Besidesthegovernmental
compound, the route includes also a site for the Israel Automotive Industries, a
factoryinchargeofmanufacturingcommandvehiclesfortheIsraeliArmy,theIsrael
DefenceForces(Davar1967)(Fig.13.3).Therefore,whilethefirstfunctioncorre-
sponds with civilian mechanisms, the latter corresponds with military dominance,
adding an additional layer to the route as an axis of control. Planned as isolated
exclaves,bothcompoundswereformedasbastions,fortifyingtheborder-roadandits
lineofdefence.Whilethismightseemlikeanexaggeratedanalysisofurbanlines,it
receivessupportfromthearchitecturaldesignofthenewDistrictCourtbuildingfrom
theearly2000s.Aspartofthegovernmentalcompound,itislocateddirectlyontopof
LowerNazareth.Designedasacitadel,withtowers,edgyroofsandnarrowwindows
resembling firing slits (Fig. 13.4), it forms a fortress on the hill, a garrison and a
stronghold—supervising,dominating,controllingandrulingoverLowerNazareth.

75, emphasising its role as a border-road. Nevertheless, to complete this role, the

13.2.1 AxisofEncounter
Functioningasaborderarea,route75isalsoanaxisofencounter,basedmainlyon
tradeandexchanges.Asacommonfeatureofborders,commercialfunctionshave
historically been an integral part of crossing areas, whether due to the substantial
amountofpeoplepassingthrough,orduetothespecialcircumstancesthatusually
characterise international passages, such as an exemption of taxes and other priv-
ileges. Accordingly, it was quite natural that the area between Upper and Lower
Nazareth,andespeciallyroute75,wouldbecomeacommercialcentre.Consisting
oflargeleft-overspacesonbothsides,thissortofno-man’slandthatsurroundsroute
75turnedintothesiteofconstantlyevolvingtradefunctions.First,itwasthesemi-
improvisedweeklymarketplacewhichtookplaceonavacatedparcelinfrontofthe
automotiveindustriescompoundandlaterturnedintotheofficialtownmarkethall.
Duringthe1990s,route75witnessedamuchmoresubstantialcommercialcentrein
theformofthefirstshoppingmallintheNazarenemetropolitanarea.Lev-Hairmall,
literarily meaning the heart of the city, was not in the heart of any city, but rather
ontheborderareabetweentwocities,attractingaJewishaswellanArabclientele.
Subsequently, the junction near the mall turned into the second direct car access
betweenUpperandLowerNazareth,forminganadditionalcrossingpointalongthe
border-road.Lessthantenyearslater,theautomotiveindustriesbecameawareofthe
advantageous location they were lactated on, and thus initiated the transformation
ofitscompoundfromanindustrialcomplexintoacommercialsite,contributingan
additionalshoppingarea.Largeinfrastructureworksduringtheearly2000s,created
adirectconnectionbetweenroute75andthelargernationalroadnetwork,enabling
residentsofUpperNazarethtoreachtheirhousewithminimalinteractionwithLower
Nazareth (Ashkenazi 2008). At the same time, this new divergence was accompa-
niedbyanadditionalshoppingmall,largerthanthepreviousones,whichwaslocated
preciselyonthespotofthenewjunction.Therefore,alongaroadofmerefourkilo-
metres,onecanfindfourdifferentshoppingcentresthatemphasiseitsroleasanaxis
ofencounter.
Atthesametime,encountersalongbordersarenotonlyplacesofcommerceand
exchanges,butalsositesforviolentclashes.Whileroute75slowlybecamealinear
shoppingdistrictitalsofunctionedasaplaceofconflict.Wehavealreadymentioned

### Page 169

170 G.Schwake
the Israeli flags in each junction and the attempts to close Upper Nazareth during
YomKippur.These,however,wereacontinuationofthetensionsalongtheroadthat
areperhapsbestrepresentedbytheOctober2000events,whichincludedlarge-scale
demonstrationsinPCIlocalitiesprotestingIsraeliactionsintheoccupiedPalestinian
West-BankduringtheearlystagesoftheSecondIntifada.Correspondingly,onthe
eveofYomKippur2000,JewishresidentsofUpperNazarethbeganattackingArab
carsandhousesalongroute75.Thisledtoafull-scaleriotevolvingArabsfromLower
NazarethandJewishIsraelisfromUpperNazareththatendedwithtwoPCI’sbeing
shotdeadbytheIsraelipolice(RabinowitzandAbu-Baker2005).Whiletherehave
beenvariousclashesbetweenresidentsofbothtownsbefore,YomKippurof2000
highlightedtheconnectionbetweenlocaltensionsandthegreaterIsraeli-Palestinian
questionwhileemphasisingtheroleoftheareabetweenbothlocalitiesasanethnic
battleground. Following these events, residents of Lower Nazareth promoted the
constructionofamemorialcommemoratingallthirteenPCI’skilledduringOctober
2000 (Adalah 2006) which was placed on route 75, just across the Lev Hair mall.
Surroundedbyshoppingcompoundsandvisiblesignsofprevioustraumaticevents,
route75formsabizarreamalgamofmarket-orientedapproaches andnationalistic
agendas.Therefore,itdemonstratesacombinationofcommerceandchauvinismthat
isanintegralpartofcrossingareasandacrucialaspectinitstransformationintoa
border-road.
13.3 Conclusions:IntegrationandCounter-Integration
Theroleofroute75asaborder-roadwasintendedtopreservetheexistingsystem
of spatial segregation and power relations. Accordingly, it forms a linear barrier
betweenbothtowns,surroundedbyasystemofheterotopicenclavesandexclaves
thatsuittheroad’sstatusasaborderarea.Overtheyears,UpperNazarethcontinued
to attract PCI families, and by the early 2000s, it became more than 20% Arab,
promoting integration that challenges the town’s national territorial role and the
localethnic-basedmodelofspacestratification.Accordingly,withtheefficiencyof
route 75 as a border-road being continuously challenged, its role as a separating
entityonlyincreased,whetherintermsofdesignorsurroundingfunctions,forming
acounter-integrationeffortmeanttosustaintheexistingorderofspatialsegregation
and dominance. In 2019, the municipality of Upper Nazareth chose to change the
town’snametoNofHaGalil,addingaphoneticaldisconnectionwiththehopethat
this would aid in the efforts for spatial exclusion. Dividing between Nazareth and
thelocalitywiththeabstractnameofNofHaGalil,route75isnowsupposedtoturn
fromaroadbetweenadjacentmunicipalitieswithsimilarnamesintoaborderbetween
parallellyexistingentities.Therefore,forminganextraterritorialsequenceroute75
strengthensethnic-basedstratification,becomingtheinfrastructureofsegregation.

secondandthethirdsectorandleavesthepublicinstitutionoutofthedevelopment
discoursebydefiningnewactors,newcompetencies,andnewimaginaries.Moreover,
sincethisstrengtheningofthefabricofsocietyarisesfromthetensionbetweenterri-
tories,itisspecificallyvisibleinmedialterritoriesandisakeyfeatureofplanetary
urbanization.Farawayfromlaissez-faire,theterritorialtensionpromotesaredefi-
nitionoftheconceptofgovernancebasedontheprincipleofsubsidiaritywherethe
mainriskispreservingthegeneralinterestoverprivateandindividualinterests.After

BIellezza,andthe“Demopraxy”program,theexampleofBonprix.

14.1 TensionMakesAlternativeGovernanceModels
Whilespatialconflictsconcernthedisruptionoftherelationsbetweenstakeholders,
take place in the public space as if the urban would be a stage, need mediation to
generateatransformativeeffectonthecity(Gualini2015),spatialtensionsinstead
self-regulate,generatealliancesthroughcooperationandcompetition,producespace
themselves(CerrutiButetal.2017).Thecasesdescribedinthesecondpartofthe
book contribute to clarifying such a distinction by considering not only tensions
generatedbythespaceanditsdispositifs,suchasinthecasesofAhmedabadorthe
road75inIsraelbutalsotensionsproducedbyprocesses,suchasinBrusselsorBerlin,
whichtransformthespaceitself.Bothifstartingfromprocesses eitherdispositifs,
spatialtensionsmaintaintheirundoubtedlyunexpectedpoweroftransformationas

long as they succeed in self-regulating. The concept of self-regulation of systems
has been widely explored by Karl Polanyi within his acclaimed book “The Great
Transformation”(Polanyi1944).AccordingtoPolanyi,self-regulationisdescribed
asa“starkutopia”(ivi:3)thatisimpossibletoachieve.Nevertheless,inhisanalysis,
Polanyidefinestheconceptof“DoubleMovement”betweenmarketizationandsocial
protectionism: while laissez-faire reformers aim at disembedding economy from
society, institutional counter-movements try instead to react by generating laws,
protection, norms, and regulated prices. Such a reflection shows not only how the
economy isembedded insociety,butalsothatasortoftensionarisesbetweenthe
oppositeforcesofeconomyandsociety.Althoughtheself-regulationofthemarket
itselfisautopia,theembeddedsystemofmarketandsocietyisdevelopingbyself-
regulatingthroughthedoublemovementor,inourwords,bymaintainingatension.
Thischapterpreciselyfocusesonthetransformativepotentialoftensionsinspace
production, by underlying two hypotheses. The main hypothesis is that tensions
definebothnewactors,newcompetencies,andnewimaginarieswithinthefieldof
spaceproductionandcity-making.Regardingactors,tensionsdevelopindeedaset
offormalandinformalalliancesbetweenthesecondandthethirdsectorsthataims
attransformingorgoverning theterritorybutalsomany timesexcludes thepublic
andtheinstitutionsfromtheseprocesses.Concerningcompetencies,suchaspecific
formofallianceevolvesnewrolesfortheactorsthemselves:farawayfromthewell-
knownsecond-welfareexperiences,firms,organizations,andindividualscooperate
onshapingthespaceitselfandgoverningitstransformations,thoughnothavingthis
kindofknowledgeorexpertise.Asforimaginaries,thiskindofallianceproducesa
changeinterritorialimagesanddesignstrategiesenhancingalternativegovernance
methods.
The second hypothesis is that the described phenomenon is specifically visible
in “medial territories” (Cerruti But 2020). By this expression, I intend the diffuse
European“intermediateterritories”(Coppolaetal.2021).“Medial”refersnotonly
to their “dependency on Technics mediation” but comes from the fact that “this
conditionrepresentsthemajorityofEuropeanterritories”andthat“suchanindeter-
minatespaceliesbetweenthecrowdedmetropolitancentersandperipheralinternal
areas.Apartfromthemaincities,Europeisprimarilycomposedofmediatedurbanity
growingfromtherootsofdiffuseurbanization”(CerrutiBut2020).Theprovincial
Italian areas like the case of Biella, the peripheral areas such as the hinterland of
Paris,theoutskirtsofBarcelona,andthediffuseCatalonianareaarethreeexamples
ofthisspatialcondition.
Summarizingthetwohypotheses,theessayobserveshowthetensiongenerated
by the competition between territories is a key factor of contemporary planetary
urbanization.Thetensionbetweenmetropolitanandmedialorevenmarginalterri-
toriesgeneratesthestrengtheningofthefabricofsocietybyreinforcingthealliances
betweencompaniesandthethirdsectorandleavingthepublicinstitutionoutofthe
developmentdiscourse.Moreover,suchalliancestriggerprojectsandgovernanceof
theterritory.Far away fromlaissez-faire,theterritorialtensionpromotes instead a
redefinitionoftheconceptofgovernancebasedontheprincipleofsubsidiaritywhere
themainriskispreservingthegeneralinterestoverprivateandindividualinterests.

14.2 Biella,AnOperationalLandscapeinTension
ThefollowingparagraphdelvesintotheBiellacasestudy.Itexploreshowtheterrito-
rialtensionandcompetition(CheshireandGordon1995;Gordon2010)betweenthe
largedensemetropolitanareasofMilanandTurinandthehighoperationalmedial
territories (Brenner and Katsikis 2020) generates the strengthening of alliances
between local stakeholders from the second and third sector, embodying a trans-
formative power in spatial production. It also tries to understand how such a form
ofself-organizedgovernancemodelworksandproducesspatialtransformationsby
balancingthetensionbetweenindividualandgeneralinterests.
TheparagraphexploresthreeexperiencesinBiella,aterritorythathasbeenlargely
studiedwithinalong-lastingresearchthatIcarriedoutinthelastsevenyearsthrough
independentresearch,educationalandresearchprojects,localactivism,andengage-
ment.Thecasesunderlinehowthespatialtensionbetweenlargemetropolitanareas
(Turin and Milan) catalyzes a new alliance between the second and third sectors.
Moreover, this way of allying is based on both a business and social tension that
defines the relations between the stakeholders and their effectiveness in triggering
projectsfortheterritory.
Biella lies in Italy, in between Milan, Turin, and the Alps. On the Eastside, the
dense Milan area is the capital of the Italian business and finance market that is
dynamicallyincreasingitsnationaleconomicrelevance(Perulli2012).OntheSouth-
Westside,thedensemetropolitanareaofTurinisafamouscompanytowndefinedby
carproduction,anditisnowlookingforanintensere-definitionofitspostindustrial
identity (Vassallo 2019). In the north, the mountain environment of Alpine Inner
Areasisstrugglingbetweenperipheralneedsandtouristicaspirations(Lucatellietal.
2013;Barberaetal.2019;DeRossi2018).Inbetweenthosediverseterritorialforces,
Biellaisanoperationallandscapecharacterizedbytwocenturiesofluxuriouswool
textileproduction.ItswooldistrictisoneofthemostancientItaliandistrictsbornin
1816(Barca2010).Thediversecrisisofthelast30yearsstronglyaffectedboththe
economicalandsocietalenvironmentoftheterritory:in2014,Biellalost50%ofits
economicweightsince2001(CerrutiBut2018).Thisshrinkisaradicalchangesince
theenterprisesdecreasefrom3000to1000andthedistrictoperatorsfrom30,000to
lessthan15,000(Maggioni2008),leavingontheterritoryanamazinglyenormous
amountofabandonedfactories,industrialarcheology,andinfrastructuralsurplusthat
doesnotseemitcouldberecycledanymore.Togetherwiththeshrinkingeconomy,
thedemographyisanotherkeychallenge:theagingsocietyisadeepsocialproblem
involvingbothvillagesinthemountainousterritoryandtheoutskirtsofthemaincity:
thepublicinstitutionsandthefabricofsocietystruggleforfindingalternativepathsof
developmentandnewformsofwelfare(CerrutiBut2014)(Fig.14.1).Nonetheless,
thestalematereinforcedthesurvivedactivefirmsthatbecamenotonlyeconomically
stronger but also technologically more advanced and socially more solid (Cerruti
ButandMattioli2019).Afewyearsago,itwaspossibletosay(CerrutiBut2018:
306):

AnallianceforUNESCO.Todayanywaythesituationisunexpectedlychanging

manylocalactiveorganizationsaretakingpartinaself-organizedgovernancemodel
called “Opera Demopratica” or “Art of Demopraxy”. The aim of the model is
achieving the original democratic ideal by deliberately bypassing political parties
andrepresentation:
Thispost-ideologicaleraofoursarisesfromtherealizationthatthesystemofrepresentation
ofthepeoplethroughpoliticalpartieshasnotfulfilledthepromiseoffulldemocracy.The
worddemocracyderivesfromtheGreekwordsdemos (people)andkratos (government,
power)andmeanspowerofthepeople.Butthepeopleconsistofalargenumberofindividuals
who,separatelyfromoneanother,cannotexercisepower.Howcanindividualsbeunitedso
thatthepeopleareabletoexercisepower,bypassingthepoliticalparties?ThroughtheArtof
Demopraxy,inwhichthetermpraxis,whichmeanspractice,replacesthetermkratos,power.
Howcanthisshiftfromparty-politicalideologytothepracticeofdemocracybemade?
Itistimeforustotakefullaccountofthefactthatpeoplearealreadyunitedintheorganiza-
tionsthatmakeupsociety:associations,foundations,companies,publicandprivatebodies,
commercialenterprisesandnonprofitinstitutions,consortia,committees,clubs,workteams
andeveryotherformoforganizedcollaboration.Infact,peoplespendhours,daysandyears
inthem,andeverydaymakechoicesanddecisionsthathaveaconcreteimpactontheir
lifeasworkers,entrepreneurs,suppliers,customers,partners,supporters,andmembersof
afamily.Everyorganizationisalreadyinitselfamicro-governmentoramicro-parliament,
spreadaroundthesocialfabric.Thepracticesthatarecarriedoutinitaredefactoexercises
ofpower.ThefunctionofDemopraxyistomaketheseorganizationsvisibleandconscious
oftheirroleandtoconnectuptheirpracticestoformsystemsofgovernmentinwhichthe
wholeofsocietyreallyisinvolved.
PistolettoandNaldini2018:1–2).
The artistic manifesto defines a process in three stages: the first one aims at
mappingandengagingthelocalorganizationalreadyactingintheterritoryfordevel-
opingsolutionsandanswerstolocalissues.Thisphaseendsupwithapublicexhi-
bition aiming at making public the previous research and show the stakeholders’
network.Thesecondone,theforum,aimsatdiscussinglocalissuesandfindsolu-
tionsandpracticestocopewiththecontemporarygovernancequestion.Withinthis
phase “representatives of local, regional, national and international public institu-
tions are also invited to take part” (ivi: 4). The third phase, the workshop, aims at
evolvingthedecisionsthatoccurredduringtheforumfor12monthsandwaitingfor
thefollowingforum.Thisphaseisverypracticalandhasthegoalofimplementing
the collective project by keeping the organizations connected. Even if this stage
evokesastrongerconnectionwithuniversitiesandpublicinstitutions,themodesof
collaborating are not given. Since it is based on a multi-level approach and aims
atdevelopingasustainabledemocracy,themodelisunconsciouslyadoptingasort
of quintuple-helix framework (Etzkowitz and Leydesdorff 1995; Carayannis et al.
2012;Peris-Ortizetal.2016)wherecivilsocietyandenvironmentarealsoengagedto
promoteasustainabledevelopmentcopingwithclimatechangeandglobalwarming.
The plans are subjected to scrutiny by university research departments. The role of the
universitiesistogatheralltherecommendationsoftheorganizationsoperatinginthatfield
andexaminethemfromtheirperspective.Theaimofthisistodeveloptheproposalsofthe
forumandtheworkshopswithinputfromthemostadvancedexpertiseinthespecificfields
ofeconomics,politics,scienceandtechnology,theenvironment,andalltheotherareasof
thesocialfabric.Theconstantinvolvementofprivateandpublicinstitutions,alongwith

thealliancesproducearebasedonreciprocaltrust,cooperation,andcompetition,the
mainaxesoftheentrepreneurialbehavioroftheindustrialdistricts(DeiOttati1994).
