14.3 AlliancesinTensionTriggerSpatialTransformations
Far from the second welfare processes, from “do-gooder” bottom-up participation
activismbutalsofromvariousformsofcorporatesocialresponsibility,thesealliances
betweensecondandthirdsectorsdependonthemutualrecognitionoftheirdecisive
roleinterritorialprocessesandontheadmissionofresponsibilitytowardtheterritory
itself. Notwithstanding the similarity to some relevant Fordism experiences very
commoninEurope,suchasthestoryofOlivettiinIvrea,FIATinTurin,Volkswagen,
or Porsche in Stuttgart, the specificity of these processes lies in the alliance or,
in other words, in the tension between the stakeholders. The XX century heroes
indeedusedtotakewidelycareoftheterritoryandmanytimespromotedsodeeply
thedevelopmentoftheircitiesthatwecouldevenrecognizesome“one-company-
towns.” Those experiences show the power of XX Century Capitalism the Fordist
erapromotedwhenmassproductionandmanufacturingaimedat“makingabetter
world”intheimageandlikenessofthefactoryandtheproductionchains(LlevatSoy
andMartin2020).ContemporaryCapitalistRealism,instead,evolvesanentirelynew
form of the business relationship between stakeholders that pursues profit through
cooperation and competitiveness (Dei Ottati 1994) on the stream of an incessant
chameleonicadaptationattitude(BoltanskiandChiapello1999).
All the experiences in Biella show how the alliances always work according to
privateinterests.FondazioneBIellezza,forinstance,hasbeenprimarilyfoundedand
fundedbyErmenegildoZegnaismainlyworkingonthedirectionofimplementing
tourism numbers. Since Zegna also owns a large reserve dedicated to naturalistic
tourism, leisure, and sports, its main interest is on raising the number of visitors.
Within the same program, Pistoletto Foundation aims instead at evolving a more
comprehensive project for the territory. Also, if Biella Città Arcipelago seems a
large and open territorial project, we must also consider that it is a project strictly

### Page 183

14 OutofthePublic:TheArchipelagoAlternative 185
connectedwiththeworkofanartistanditsfoundation.Inaway,evenifitispossible
to acknowledge to the foundation a large and unselfish effort in taking care of the
entireterritory,theyaimatpromotingtheirprivateinterestandmissionaswell.
Exactly in the same way, in the example of Biella Creative City, every stake-
holderispromotingitsindividualinterest:togiveanexample,theeducationalproject
“WeavesMemory”involvessomeofthemostrelevantstakeholdersconnectedwith
educationandenterprises.Accordingtothedescriptionoftheproject,
Beyondthetraditionalconceptofamuseum,theintentionistocreateadouble“hightech
space/door”inthecitydedicatedtothedisseminationofthethemeofwoolprocessing
andproductionandinthefieldoffashiondesign.Aspacebasedonthegreatarchivesof
thehistoricalbrandsofBiellaMadeinItaly.Theactionpassesthroughthestrengthening
of the Biellese Network Center of Textile and Fashion Archives, with the increase and
diversificationofthemembersandthedevelopmentofacommunicationapparatus(Web,
thematicapps)forscholarsandprofessionalsfromcreativecities.Withtheaimofincreasing
physicalandvirtual(Web)visitorstodiscovertheterritoryanditscreativehistory(wool
millsinoperation,industrialarcheology,traditionoftextilemastery),theprojectstandsasa
modelofpublic–privatecollaborationthatcanalsobereplicatedinothercitiesofthecircuit.
(https://www.biellacittacreativa.it/Progetti,accessed13.06.2021).
The model of private–public collaboration mainly consists of public support to
private initiatives. All the stakeholders involved have instead a direct interest in
hosting an exhibition, promoting an educational activity, promoting the archives,
etc.Theprojectundoubtedly hasarelevantgeneralinterest,anyway,suchasocial
impactisapositiveexternalitythatiseverythingexceptintentional.Whatismissing
isnottheengagementoflocalprivatestakeholdersthatareworkingwithcoherence
anddedication,buttheroleofthepublicinstitution,unabletoguidethecollective
planningcapability.
The examples show how this form of out-of-public alliances is based on stake-
holders’tension.Aroundthetablewherethedecisionsaremade,everystakeholder
bringsitsprivateinterestandeverybodyknowsthatsuccessdependsonthebalance
betweendiverseandcontrastinginterests.Tensionisproducingspacebecausethere
arenopossibilitiestoactdifferently.Everystakeholderneedstoseataroundthetable
andmustcopewiththeunwrittennormsoftensionandnegotiations.Sincethepublic
institutioncannotjointhetable,bothbecauseofthelackofplanningcapabilityand
becauseoftheweakeningofitspower,thespatialtransformationwillnotprimarily
follow the general interest but will instead balance the powerful private and indi-
vidualstakeholders’interests.Eventhoughthepublicinstitutioncouldseataround
thetable,itspoweristooweaktoimposeanalternativedirectionandinaway,this
keepsthetensionalive,withtheriskoflosingthecollectivemeaningofanyspatial
policy.
Withinsuchabackground,alliancesproducedbytensionsarethedecisivefactors
interritorialdevelopmentandtriggerspatialtransformationswhilethepublicmany
times underestimate the phenomenon. Far away from laissez-faire, the territorial
tensionpromotesaredefinitionoftheconceptofgovernancebasedontheprincipleof
subsidiarity(Cotturri2001)andofahybridenterprise(VenturiandZandonai2016),
andthemainriskisreplacingthegeneralinterestoverprivateandindividualinterests.

### Page 184

186 M.CerrutiBut
Thepowerfulalliancesthattransformthespacethroughtensionbutexcludepartially
ortotallythepublic,(eitherbecauseofindividualstakesorbecauseofincapabilityof
thepublicitself)pointoutthenecessityofareflectiononhowispossibletodefend
thegeneralinterestandtoguidethedevelopmenttowardacommondiscourse(Ciaffi
2020).TheEuropeanlawassignstothepublicthisrole.Maytensionbethekeyfor
imagininganewcontemporaryroleforthepublicinterritorialdevelopment?

Nowadays, aging has begun to affect the uses and practices of private and public
spacesinnumerouscities,becominganewurbanquestion(Secchi2010).
Inthelastthirtyyears,thediscussiononthethemeofaginginWesternsocieties
and its repercussions on the structure of cities has exploded in the urban planning
andarchitecturaldisciplinaryliterature.Thedebatehasconsideredprogressiveaging
as an epochal fact intended both as a conquest and a problem. The demographic
process of aging is strongly intertwined with other economic, social, and cultural
processes like life expectancy, health, availability of goods and capital, needs and
demands of diverse segment of the population. Different configurations have been
givendependingontheweightandtheintertwiningoftheseprocessesandissues.On
theonehand,theagingofthepopulationisunderstoodasaconquest,emancipation,
andareversalfromtheattentionthatwaspreviouslytypicallyassistance,andwhich
now rediscovers the third age as a powerful phase of life, and which expresses all
new needs (Hauderowicz and Serena 2020; Bufferl et al. 2018; Gratton and Scott
2016;Simpson2015).
On the other hand, there is still apprehension for the repercussions of a world
excessivelypopulatedbytheelderly.Inbothcases,whetherweconsiderthestrength
orthefragilityoftheelderlypopulation,todayemergesaspatialtopography(Piketty
2013) fragmented and under tension, which requires new forms of economy and
shapesaneworderofsociety.Subjecttothesametensions,theformsoflivingalso
changeaccordingtoaging.Asaresult,theEuropeanterritorieswillhavetorethink
theiridentityradically.Theemergenceofthesetensionsalsorequiresthatthetools
ofurbaninvestigationandspaceresearchchange,favoringanexplorationcapableof
escapinganylinearandstigmatizingnarrativeoftheemergenceoftheagingissue.
Let’slookattheEuropeancontexttoday.Progressiveagingallowsustorecon-
structtheemergenceofamapofthemanyelderlyterritorieswherewecanfindthe
concentrationanddispersionoftheelderlypopulation.Amovementofconcentra-
tionanddispersiondrawsadoublesignofaprogressivelylargerorminorpopulation
density. In the first case, the choice of the elderly population to move in search of
betterconditionsinsomespecificcontextsgeneratesaconcentrationofretirees.In
thesecondcase,thedepartureofayoungpopulationcontractssomeareasinwhich
onlytheelderlyremain.Theresultisanewgeographyofterritoriesdenselyinhab-

reserved settlements, and at the same time with marginal, internal, and shrinking
territories.However,itisageographythatoverlapswithmigratoryflows,processes
ofglobalization,andpoliticalphenomena.
Thisarticleputsthemagnifyingglassononeoftheplacesofconcentrationofthe
elderly population. Doing a double exercise. On the one hand, describe an elderly

phenomenonoftheelderlypopulationinCostadelSol.Thecaseisperhapsthebest

15.2 TheLargestThemeParkintheWorld
Europe has exchanged work for perpetual sun-bathing on the Mediterranean Sea
beaches(Ballard2014).In1968Ballard,inhisshortstory“TheLargestThemePark
intheWorld,”describesadystopianEurope,where30millioninhabitantsdecideto
retireonaperpetualvacationandformalinearmetropolis4500kmlongand300m
wide on the Mediterranean coast. In reality, today, we are not that far from this
dystopianimage.TheEuropeanshoreoftheMediterraneanhasbecomeapuzzleof
infiniterandompiecesinwhich,especiallyinthelastthirtyyears,anever-increasing

### Page 189

192 A.Kërçuku
numberoftheNorthernEuropeanpopulationdecidestoretire.Theperpetualtransfer
tothebeachesistheresultofalongprocess.ItfindsitsoriginsintheEuropeanculture
ofthenineteenthcenturyandelitetourism.Theideaofthebeachchangesradically,
passingfromanegativevaluetoapositiveone(Corbin1988).Itthencontinueswith
the first social movements for tourism focused on the poor class (Lafargue 2011),
and only towards the end of the Second World War was it consolidated thanks to
masstourism.Furthermore,thankstotheachievementofeconomicwell-beingbyan
increasing number of population and the evolution of public and private transport
and networks. After a brief slowdown with the 1973 oil crisis, the phenomenon
continued to develop and transform further until the emergence of the residential
touristphenomenoninthe1980s.Theresidentialtouristsarepeoplewhodecideto
moveforreasonsrelatedtoleisureinaplaceotherthantheirplaceoforiginandwho
decidetobuyasecondhomeinthenewlocationasaconsciouselementoffinancial
strategy to maximize material well-being in old age (Casado Diaz 1999; Casado-
Diaz2004).Throughoutthetwentiethcentury,tourismbecomesoneoftheessential
phenomena of European society, with significant repercussions on the economic,
urban, territorial, and architectural fields in the Mediterranean (Pié i Ninot 2012;
PiéiNinot2013).Andtheperspectiveofresidentialtouristsispartofthiscomplex
frameworkandhasattendedthedevelopmentoftheMediterraneancoastline.
Most resident tourists consist of retirees, generally copies and over the age of
55years(Casado-Diaz2004).Afterretirement,theydecidetomovetoothercontexts
in search of a better quality of life. The flow of migrant retirees has reached such
alargescalethatinthe1980s,wasconceptualizedbysociologistsandgeographers
as one of the various post-retirement options in contemporary society (Williams
et al. 2000). Furthermore, the term International Retirement Migration (IRM) has
been coined to describe the phenomenon of the mobility of American retirees to
warmerplaces(Kingetal.1998).InEurope,oneoftheprimarystudiesinthefield
ofretireemigrationbyCribierin1980focusesonFrance’sinternalmobility(Cribier
1980). Subsequently, other studies expanded the scale and analyzed the flow from
northernandwesternEuropetosouthernEuropeancountries(Williamsetal.1997;
Breuer2005).TheIRMisaprocesswhichinEuropewillcontinuetoincreasesignifi-
cantlyinthecomingdecades(Breuer2005),favoredaboveallbysomedemographic,
economic,health,cultural,environmental,andpoliticalimplications:increaseinlife
expectancy;improvementofhealthconditions;introductionofearlyretirementpoli-
cies; increase in economic capacity; extension of the tourist infrastructure of the
territories;reductionoflanguagebarriers;implementationoftheEuropeanintegra-
tionprocess;thewidespreadpresenceofplaceswithhighenvironmentalandclimatic
qualities;lowcostoflivingintheareasofdestination;accessibilitytobothpublic
andprivatehealthfacilities;greaterfamiliaritywithcontextsandpresenceofmigrant
networksintheplacesofdestination(HallandMuller2004;Casado-Diazetal.2004;
HuberandOreilly2004;Gustafson2008;Williamsetal.2000;Muñoz2012;King
etal.2000).
TheterritoriesoftheEuropeanMediterraneancoastareincreasinglyconcentrating

15.3 CostadelSol:TheLargestEuropeanRetirement
Resort
The Costa del Sol, located in the Spanish province of Andalusia, was dominated
by predominantly rural spaces until sixty years ago and today appears as a result
of speculative pressures from the tourism industry as a vast leisure infrastructure
(Simpson 2015). These are 32 municipalities in the province of Malaga bordering
the Mediterranean coast, where cascos antiguos, resorts, urbanizaciones, beaches,
golf courses, vivendas, malls, and ports constantly alternate (Simpson 2015). The
coastlineisaseriesofpiecesofalargemosaicthat,byadditionandjuxtaposition,
builditsphysicalspace(PiéiNinotandRosaJiménez2013)inalinearmetropolitan
condition(Romero2004).
Thehighdensityoftheelderlypopulationinthearearesultsfromalongprocess
ofaccumulationthathasproducedanintenseurbanizationdevelopmentofthecoast.
Thisisaprocessthatbegantowardstheendofthenineteenthcenturyandcontinued
throughoutthetwentiethcenturyandwhichtodaytakesonradicaldimensions.The
constructionoftheimageofthelargestEuropeanretirementresortisaconsequence
ofaspecificeconomicandterritorialmodel.TheterritoryoftheCostadelSolhas
changed its economy, space, and, more generally, society through an increasingly
complexterritorialinfrastructurebasedontherelationshipestablishedbetweenthe
elderly,theCostadelSol,andtourism(Fig.15.1).
According to the Instituto de Estadística y Cartografía de Andalucía, the total
populationin2015was1,404,116inhabitants,16%over65(219,868),apercentage
even lower than the Spanish average (18%) (Estadística 2015). Even the data
concerningtheforeignpopulationarenotverydifferent:outofatotalpopulationof
foreigners equal to 232,382 units, only 17% of the population belongs to the over

15.4 ACatalog ofSpaces
Observing the new geography of concentration of the elderly population in Costa
del Sol has highlighted the wide variety of uses that the elderly population makes
of the area. This wide variety happens in a place that, on the contrary, is always
treateduniquelyasthelargestEuropeanretirementresort.Theliteraturehasreferred
to Costa del Sol grasping its specialization aspects, sometimes attributing to it a
genericcharacterization.Arestrictivelabelhasoverlapped,whichtendstoconsider
theCostadelSol,inaratherunivocalandhomogeneousway,asaterritoryofand
fortheelderly.Thehousingpracticesoftheelderlyhavesometimesbeendescribed
asutopias(Simpson2015),othersasconformingtoamoretraditionalviewofaging,
exceeding in simplifications that fail to grasp the complexities of the phenomena
affectingurbanization.
Fromthemapoftheconcentrationofelderlypopulation,itispossibletoidentify
nine situations that express a much more complex interaction between space and
aging. A new taxonomy of conditions and articulations emerges, arising through
thevariablesthatbestaccountfortheconditionoftheelderlypopulation:age,state
of health, citizenship, and economic power. Although from the different forms of
housingobservedintheareaandthepropercharacteristicsofthespace.
In the hypertrophic spaces of the Spanish real estate bubble (1997–2007), the
transformationoftheterritorywasdictatedbyinvestmentsintherealestatemarket
andbytheperformanceofthefinancialone.Althoughtheeconomiccrisisof2007
put a strain on the Spanish territory, the real estate market remained active. An
excessinthephysicaldimensionsoftheresidentialagglomerationsandthecapital
involved has made Costa del Sol the most crucial market in Spain. These spaces,
shapedbythemultiplicationofprotectedenclavesandartificialnatures,havebecome
the stage that flaunts abundance. The barren, steep, and rugged soil is replaced by
the golf courses, flat or slightly sloping, maniacally ordered. It appears a space
in which age, citizenship, and health do not seem to count. A gated community
remains,precisely,betweenlimitedandclosedspaces,agatedcommunity.Andthe
demographiccompositiondoesnotseemtoaffectthephysicalcharacteristicsofthe
space,despitethesignificantpresenceofanelderlypopulation.Itisthespaceinwhich
therenewedpoweroftheelderlypopulationisclearlyshown.Aradicallytransformed
spacealsothrougheconomiccapitalandahypertrophicrealestatemarketlinkedto
retirees.
In hedonistic spaces, the street, beach, and public spaces, in general, become
places to exhibit a fit, tanned, sometimes renewed body. The territory specialized
in this sense becomes a place to seek physical and mental well-being, beauty, and
rejuvenationthroughthenumerousclinics.Golfcoursesbecometheanswertothe
increased availability of free time and the new disposition to body care. In this
fragment of space, the elderly as a weak subject no longer exists, and the space
becomes hedonistic due to the new actor that populates it and the new behaviors.
Theelderlybodyisnolongerexcluded,rejected,butexposed.Theelderlyhavefree
timeandaconcentrationofself-careequipment.

### Page 194

15 AgingandSpace.AnEmergingCatalogofSpacesinTension 197
In the interrupted spaces, the broken dreams of elderly populations of having
a house on the Costa del Sol or housing situations of isolation and solitude,
between ruins and abandonment, have materialized. Residential agglomerations,
tourist complexes subdivisions, roads, dug mountains, and abandoned shopping
centersfollowoneanotherwithskeletonsoremptysubdivisionsofwhatmusthave
beenaseductivecomplexintheeyesofinvestmentprojectsandspeculators.There
are,atothertimes,roadsthatendinemptylotsoragainstabaremountain,andat
othertimes,theyaremoreorlesscompletedagglomerationsbutwhichsubsequently
haveneverbeeninhabited.Followingtheeconomiccrisisof2007,somesegmentsof
theAndalusianterritoryhavebecomethesceneofabandonment.Nexttothepaisaje
delucro,onwhichtouristandresidentialagglomerationshavecolonizedvastvirgin
territoriesofthecoastwithinafewyears,thereisaveryhighconcentrationofmodern
ruins(Schulz-Dornburg2012)(Fig.15.3).
In the scarce presence of specialized spaces, the image of the Costa del Sol as
aEuropeanretirementresortisquestioned.Althoughaforeignelderlypopulationis
widespreadthroughouttheterritory,itultimatelydoesnotleadtoplacesconceived
anddesignedaccordingtothespecificcharacteristicsoftheoldagecondition.Instead,
there is a random distribution and concentration, even though most of the foreign
elderly population comes from countries of northern Europe, countries in which
people more commonly retire to nursing homes in the final phase of their lifes.
Theconcentrationofspecializedspaces,therefore,correspondstothoseareasmore
denselypopulatedbynativepopulations.Therearefewprotectedenclavesinthearea
oriented exclusively towards an elderly population: entry to one of these places is
regulatedandrestrictedaccordingtoage.Itisaplannedconcentrationoftheelderly
Fig.15.3 Interruptedspaces.Benalmadena

inaspecificspaceincontextsoflesservalueandgenerallyconcentratedinperipheral
areasoflargecities.Thistypeofspaceispopulatedbyislandsconsistingofmainly

In changing spaces, the scarce presence of specialized spaces and the apparent

athomeandbeingaguest,inacontemporaryfeelingofbeingtouristsandresidents.
Thestateofcontradictionthatanimatesthiswayofexistingontheterritoryhasshaped
achangingspace,notspecializedandindependentofthepopulationthatinhabitsit.
Inthesecontexts,theterritoryhasbecomeanidyllicstagefortheretreat,aspacefor

elderlypopulationandno.Theconcentrationoftheelderlypopulationinaspecific

temporarymovements,sometimesfollowedbynewreturnstotheircountryoforigin
afteryearsinwhichthepensionershadmovedtheirresidencetotheCostadelSol.
ThereisnosinglewaytoorganizeyourretirementontheCostadelSol,andthereis
nosingletypicalretireeinSpain(Simpson2015).Temporarinessisoneofthemain
characteristicsobservedintheorganizationalbehaviorofthissocialgroup.However,

their stay. The populations stand side by side in a changing space that welcomes
themregardlessofage,healthconditions,andnationality.
In the contact spaces between different populations, the Costa del Sol territory
canalsobedescribedasacontainerthatwelcomespeopleofdifferentnationalities.
Aplacewherelanguages,cultures,andreligionsmeetandmix.Supposeinitially,the
foreignpopulationconcentrationhelpedtodefinemono-nationalspaces.Itisincreas-
inglycommoninthelastyearstocomeacrossfragmentsofterritorywhosedemo-
graphic composition has been reshuffled and profoundly modified—this process
of mixing leads to the construction of contact spaces between different popula-
tions. Not so much guided by origin, culture, or religion, but rather by economic
availability. The transition from mono-national spaces to contact spaces between
different populations, which affects these fragments, confirms the observations of
Chin, who considers the differences between human beings not in terms of fixed
andimmutablephysicalbarriers,butmoresimilartofluidconditions.Thedifference
between humans has different life cycles that are changing the forms and ways of
whatcanbedescribedaboveallinEuropeasthewordgameofinfinitepermutation
andcombinations(Chin2017).
In rigid spaces, the physical characteristics of the houses produce the isolation
condition of the elderly population, especially when mobility and consequently
autonomy are lacking. The rigidity of the space and its influence on the lives of
theelderlyemergemoreclearly.Rigidspacesbecomerealtraps,whichdonotadapt
tothephysicalchange,totheagingoftheirinhabitants,whocannotadjustthespace

### Page 196

15 AgingandSpace.AnEmergingCatalogofSpacesinTension 199
tonewneedsandoftenfindthemselvesforcedtoleavetheirhomes.Therefore,the
mainissuerevolvesarounddesigningtheadaptabilityofspacefortheelderlypopu-
lationandnotjusttheprivatelivingspace,butespeciallypublicspacefortheelderly,
thistimeinconditionsoffragility.
Thehyper-infrastructuringoftheterritory,whichtookplaceaboveallinthe1970s
and1980s,builtacomplexurbanlandscape(Romero2004).Alinearcity,withits
150kmrunningalongthelineoftheA7motorwaywithaconcentrationofdifferent
urbanepisodes,resultsfromtherealestatemetastasis.Theprocessofstrengthening
and increasing accessibility has favored the increase in migration of the elderly
population (De Lacour 2014). However, the spaces of the mobility infrastructures
represent,ontheonehand,anelementofconnection;ontheotherhand,alsorepre-
sentanelementofdisconnectionfortheelderlypopulation.Infrastructurebecomesa
physicalandspatialdeviceofexclusionbothbecauseofthetopographicalcharacter-
isticsofthespaceandthephysicalcharacteristicsoftheinfrastructure.Permeability
in the territory is possible only for subjects who have good economic capital and
good health conditions. Accessibility to most of the region can only occur due to
personalvehiclemobilityormovementsthatinvolveconsiderablephysicaleffortdue
tothetopographyanddispersionofthesettlements.
In the adaptable spaces, the articulation of the residential career is one of the
adaptationstrategiesoftheelderlypopulationoftheCostadelSol.Thereis,infact,
adynamicrelationship,observablethroughoutthelifecycle,betweenthephysical
abilitiesofapersonandthehomeenvironment,forms,andhousingpractices.The
dichotomousoptionbetweenaginginplaceintheirownhomeandmovingtoacare
facilitywhenyouarenolongerphysicallyindependentisoutdated.Todayitismore
frequenttoidentifyelderlypeoplewhodecidenottoliveneither“trapped”inrigid
spacesnor“lockedup”inspecializedspaces,buttovoluntarilymovetospacesthat
canbeadaptedtothenewconditions.
15.5 Conclusion
In the nine spatial typologies, how the elderly population actively builds and uses
spaceemerge,helpingtodefinearatherarticulatedconditionoffragmentsintension
thatreactdifferentlytotheinputsimpressedbytheelderlypopulation.Someterri-
tories stiffen and lose strength, and others lose specificity. Some become closed,
andothersremainporousovertime.Somearespecialized,andothersarenot.Some
arerigid,andothersareadaptable.Someseparate,andotherscommunicate.Some
showthestrengthoftherealestatemarket,andothersoffertheneedsandstrengthof
hedonisticliving.TheresultisacatalogofspacesandaCostadelSolmodifiedand
transformed by its “new” inhabitants into a fragmentary space reassembled in the
territoryinaparatacticway,whichgoesbeyondavisionandcompliantmodalitiesof
thelargerEuropeanretirementresort.Assumingtheelderlyterritoryasspecialized
or,onthecontrary,genericmeansnotgraspingtherichnessoftheimplicationsthat
derivefromlivingpractices.TheelderlyterritoryoftheCostadelSolistodayaplace

### Page 197

200 A.Kërçuku
offragmentationthatismodeledaccordingtoinnumerablevariables,whichdescribe
bothaveryvariedconditionoftheagingandspace.
This article emphasizes the need to rethink the figure of the elderly, avoiding
unique descriptions that place the elder in a precise and restrictive category. The
elderlyindividualisnolongerjustafragilefigure.Itmustbereconsidered,holding
boththeelementsthatrestoretheirstrengthandthosethatmakethemfragileindivid-
uals.Beingfragileandpowerfulatthesametimeperhapsreturnsthemostsignificant
difficulty in defining the condition of aging in the contemporary era. A difficulty
increasedbythecontinuousriseinlifeexpectancyandoldageidentificationwithan
extendedtimerangebetween65and115.Halfacenturyofnewlife,oradditionallife,
duringwhichthephysicalconditions,thenetworksofrelationships,andthedegreeof
participationinsociallifewillstillvaryenormouslyinstepwithtechnologicalinno-
vation.Therefore,thenumerousattemptstoreclassifyandre-categorizetheelderly
population into subgroups remain problematic and ambiguous and fail to provide
convincing descriptions about the limits of the new phases of existence and the
multitudeofsocial,economic,cultural,andbiologicalphenomenathatinterest.On
thespatialimplications,whichistheoneprivilegedintheessay,asimilarcomplexity
isexpressed.Thismakesitnecessarytoabandonstudycriteriathatreflecthomoge-
neouscategoriesoftheEuropeanelderlypopulationandthespacestheyoccupyin
ourcitiestounderstandbettertheliquidityofthetransformationsandchangesthey
imprintontheurbanspaceproject.

Chapter 16
Spatial Tensions in Architectural Design:
Strategies, Projects and Visions
GiuliaSetti
Abstract The essay studies the concept of tension in architectural design: how
it could become a tool of intervention and in what extension tensions could be
captured in the recent projects we find in our cities. Milan tells of a continuous
overlap of different tensions that have developed in many forms over the last few
years, precisely after constructing some significant new interventions in the city.
Startingfromtheprojects,nearingcompletion,ofPortaNuovaandCityLifeandfrom
thenewinterventionsthathavebeenrecentlyopened,includingthetransformation
of the former railyard system, the essay tries to describe the latent tensions that
aregeneratedinthedifferentdesignphases.Tensionsthatworkondifferentlevels,
startingfromthecontrastbetweenthetraditionallanguageofthecityandthenewand
iconicarchitecturesthattheseprojectsdefine,uptothetensionsrelatedtothedesign
andconstructionofcontemporarypublicspace.Interpretingthetensionsofthemajor
urbanprojectsalreadyunderwayorabouttobebuiltinMilanmeansunderstanding
whatrelationshipsexistbetweentensions,architecture,anddesignandidentifying
whatformsoftensionsnewinterventionscouldgenerateinthecity.

differentforcesthatcomeintoplay,integratingor,inothercases,generatingconflicts
andoppositions.Theresearchandessayscollectedinthisvolumetrytoobserveif,
and how, the concept of tension can become a tool for contemporary design and
inwhattermstensionscanbecapturedintheinterventionswerecordinourcities.

16.2 Architecture,TensionsandDesign:Places,Strategies
andVisions
ThecaseofMilanwhichappearsemblematicforthepropulsiontochangethatthecity
hasexperiencedinrecentyears,particularlysincethe2000s,withsomeinterruptions
markedbytheeconomiccrisisof2008,and,marginallyorwithresultsstillnotvisible,
bytherecentCOVID-19pandemicisoneofthepossiblecaseswheretensions,more
or less controlled, can be grasped between new urban projects and the fabric of
the traditional city. This volume tries to identify different ways of describing the
conflictualorcontradictoryrelationshipbetweentensions,designandarchitecture.
These are cases where tensions have different genesis, through the conflict, latent
orexplicit,whichisexercised,itispossibletoidentifydifferentdesignresponses.
Theprojectrepresents,inmanycases,atoolfordiscoveryandintegrationbetween
the different tensions present in the field. It serves to build new balances, and for
these reasons, architecture becomes a space for relationships. Many of the essays
collected in this volume have tried to focus on this subtle and delicate passage, to
identifyanddescribethevarioustensionsinthefield,religiousorcultural,between
differentplayers,orintheusesandmanagementofopenspaces,intherelationship
betweenthenewandthepre-existing.Differentanglesthatshowhowtheprojectcan
amplifyexistingtensionsor,onthecontrary,trytobuildnewbalances.
Theintentionofthisvolumeistoputtheprojectatthecentreofreflection,albeitin
apartialwayandwithoutclaimingtobeexhaustive,itisthroughdifferentcasestudies
that we can test the hypothesis that tensions, both intangible and latent, influence
the design and architecture and that, at least, architecture can try to respond to, or
oppose, these phenomena. Ahmedabad, Israel, Costa Rica and Brussels are some
ofthecontextswherethetensions,presentontheground,havestimulatedprojects
andreflectionsoncurrentconditions;inparallelwithinternationalexperiences,the
volumereturnstolookingatItaliancases,incities,suchasMilanandTurin,where
differentformsoftensionslinkedtotheappropriationofrights,suchasthecaseofthe
Cavallerizza,ortothecontrastbetweennewprojectsandexistingcity,asinthecase
ofMilan,showthebirthofconflictsandprecariousbalances,inwhicharchitecture
iscalledtorespondtonewandchangedneeds.Itisacomplexjourney,whichmoves
along different geographies and where the project becomes a key to interpretation
andpossibletransformation.InAhmedabad,tensionsbetweenHindusandMuslims
havecausedconflictsanddivisionsandtheconstructionofmediationarchitectures,

16.3 TransformingMilan:StoriesandProjects
withDifferentEndingsandOpenQuestions
Milan has changed considerably in the last twenty years: new projects have trans-

foryears.Therecoveryoftherailwayyardsisthelatestinalonglineofstoriesabout

that concern the use and appropriation of spaces—public, in particular—or which

16.4 ArchitecturalTensions:APremiseontheRecent
ProjectsinMilan

tensionsthatthenewinterventionsofPortaNuovaandCityLifehavegeneratedin
Milan.Thecasestudieswillbeexploredthroughacriticalcomparisonofarchitectural
elementsappliedineachproject,highlightingsimilaritiesanddifferences,observing
theshapeofbuildingsandopen,orpublicspaces.Amethodologyaimstodescribe
boththecontrast,oftennotsolved,withinthecontext,andunderlinesthedifferent
design choices made. The architectural design has redefined entire portions of the
city,modifyingitsidentitystrongly;if,ononehand,thenewhasgivenbacktoMilan
architectures and public spaces; on the other, the integration between new and old
appearstobeanopenissue(Colomina1994;Gaventa2006).
In this perspective, the powerful transformation that has affected Milan brings
out contradictions, conflicts and tensions in redesigning portions of the city; the
new advances in the iconic buildings that are rising up in Milan and in a mix of
different architectural languages imported by international firms. Recent projects
completedinMilantellofacomplex,andunresolvedrelationshipwiththepast,or
ratherwithtradition.Projectsareshowinglatenttensionsamongtheactorsinvolved
inthedesignofthecontemporarypublicspaceanditsuses(deSolà-Morales2009;

### Page 206

16 SpatialTensionsinArchitecturalDesign:Strategies,Projects… 209
Madanipour 2003). The pilot projects carried out in Milan in recent years have
given rise to powerful transformation processes. Private entities invest capital and
resources to build a new image of themselves: they are large banks, prestigious
qualifiedcompaniesthathavefoundpossibleopportunitiesforrebirthintheempty
andabandonedareasofMilan.
ThetensionsthatsomenewinterventionsinthetransformationofMilanproduce
are of different forms and characters; a radical fracture arises from the increas-
ingly sustained choice to break the horizontality of the city in favour of punctual,
isolatedarchitecturesdesignedtobuildanewverticality.Tensions,therefore,linked
tocompositionalchoices,toadelicatebalancebetweeniconicandsignificantprojects
forthecity,destinedtochangeitsimageinthecomingdecades.
IntherecentinterventionsinMilan,thedesignofthecontemporarypublicspace
istheresultofdaringbutprecise,formalandarchitecturalchoices.Walkingthrough
PortaNuovaorCityLife,wecouldidentifyiconicbuildingsandpowerfullandmarks,
markingthenewhorizonofthecity.Thesechoicescouldinfluencethedesignofits
public spaces and strongly highlight the necessity of public open spaces citizens
haveshown(Carmonaetal.2010).Interventionsandprojectsdescribedheremarka
crucialmomentforMilanandthesizeableeconomiccrisisthatbeganin2008andhas
notyetconcluded.Thus,itisaseriesofimpressivearchitecturaltransformationsthat
startedbeforetheadventofthecrisis.Therefore,theyhadtoredefinetheirambitions
and actual construction times. Time represents, for these projects, a variable to be
takenintoconsiderationbecauselarge-scaletransformations,suchasthosedescribed
inMilan,posequestionsaboutthedurationofeachprojectandtheabilitytoadapt,
overtime,tosuddenchangesthatcouldarrive(Lynch1972).
16.5 PortaNuova:DesigningaNewPublicGround
The transformation of Porta Nuova, which has been developing since 2005 and
which today is close to conclusion, is an intervention that is built thanks to the
juxtapositionofdifferentbuildings,iconicarchitecturesthathaveradicallychanged
the image of the city and restored, in fact, a space that had been abandoned for
years.Itisaprojectthatshowshow,afterafewyears,interestingquestionsemerges
relatedbothtotheconstructionandmanagementofcontemporarypublicspaceand
tothecompositionalanddesignchoicesthatdetermineditsdesign.ThePortaNuova
interventionhighlightstwopossibleformsoftension,ontheonehand,itisinconflict
and in contrast with the architecture of the traditional fabric of the historic city of
Milan,ontheotherhand,wefindlatentbutequallyimportanttensions,indefining
therelationshipbetweenarchitectureandnewpublicspaces.
The Porta Nuova project describes, with great relevance, a possible process for
theconstructionofthecontemporarycitywhereprivatelenders,mostlybanksand
insurance companies, build their offices through large real estate transactions and
compensate the city through the creation of a system of public spaces. This is the
synthesisofthePortaNuovaprojectanditsgenesiswhichrecognisesagoodquality

16.6 CityLife:TheIconicArchitecturesofaNewFuture
DevelopmentoftheCityLifeprojectbeganin2004,andasmentionedatthebegin-
ning of this essay, the advent of the economic crisis of 2008 actually slowed the
start of the works. The project, which recovers the space dedicated to the former
Trade Fair, reinforces some important issues on the processes and development of
the project. City Life was born, in fact, thanks to the support and contribution of
private actors, in particular the Generali Group, which financed and managed the
interventioninitsvariousphases,fromconceptionrightthroughtodevelopment.
Theprojectworksontwodifferentbutequallydecisivethemes;ontheonehand,

ofalargeareaofpublicland,opentocitizens,whichconstitutesafertilesupportfor
buildings, as well as a decisive element because it is capable of consolidating and
accommodatingthearchitecturalobjectsthatarefreelydisposedthere.
The City Life project is ambitious, sees the integration of offices, tertiary and
residentialactivities,whichdefineitsedge;therefore,acomplexprojectwhereakey
roleisplayedbythearchitecture,intheconstructionofthethreeskyscrapers,and
bythedesignoftheopenspaceandthelargesurroundingpark.Thenearconclusion

managestoreconcile,inthedesignofitsopenspaces,amoredomesticmeasure.Itis
certainlysignificanttotrytodescribethespatialtensionsthattheconstructionofCity
Lifehasraisedondifferentaspectsandlevels(Fig.16.3).Thefirstconflictconcerns
therelationshipthatthenewdistrictestablisheswiththetraditionalcity.Itisevident
that the project works on an extra-urban scale through iconic architecture, partly
the result of the increasingly widespread standardisation on an international level
(PonziniandNastasi2016).Itisaformoftensionbetweenthenewandtheexisting
thatstronglyappearsinCityLifeandinthedesignchoicesmadeovertheyearsthat
opens up a very important debate for Milan, and beyond, on the relationship and
legacybetweencontemporaryprojectsandthecity(Fig.16.4).Thesecondformof
tensionconcerns,asinthecaseofPortaNuova,therelationshipbetweenpublicand
private which are expressed in different forms, both in the process of construction
ofthepublicspaceofCityLife,alsointhiscasesupportedbyprivateplayers,and
inthebalancebetweentheiconicformsofthenewarchitectureandthemuchmore
traditionalusesofthepublicurbanspace.
ThenewimageofCityLifeisthatofagrowingMilan,attractedbythevertical
growthandthestrengthofthenewarchitecturesthatarechangingthecity;Ibelieve
that City Life’s intervention highlights the latent tension that exists, today more

poraryarchitectureofteninfluencedbythestandardisationpromotedbytheglobal
market,whereitseemstogrowbesynonymouswithgoodarchitecture.Theproject

16.7 NewPerspectives:TheRoleofTensionsasaTool
forArchitecturalDesign
LookingatnewprojectsforMilanisimportanttoreflectontheroleoftensionsin
theproject,sometimestensioncanbecomeadesigntool,tobalanceopposingforces
actinginacontextor,inothercases,tensionsemergeaftertheconclusionofaproject,
whenitispossibletorecognisethefirsteffectsofnewinterventionsandtheirability
tosettleandgrow.
More generally, the tensions work on different scales, from the urban space to
thearchitecturalscale,understandingthattheprojectis,veryoften,theplacewhere
differentconflictsarefaced.
The projects described in this essay try to outline an open field of research on
therelationshipbetweentensionsandarchitecture,inparticularbyidentifyingsome
possible definitions and grasping the real implications. The critical debate on the
PortaNuovaandCityLifeprojectsallowsustodefineandindicatesomevariations
ofthetensionsinrelationtocontemporaryarchitecture.
Firstly,wecouldgraspatension,whichoftenleadstoconflict,betweenthe‘new’
representedbytheiconicarchitecturesthataregrafted,notwithoutclashes,intothe
traditionalcityandthe‘old’,thatis,thehistoricanddensecity.Itisacontrastthat
couldbeseenbothinthePortaNuovainterventionandintheoneofCityLife.Inthis
case,tensionsarelinkedtodesign,andinviteustoreflectontheiconicitythatthe
newarchitectures,signedbyinternationalfirms,declare.Itisaninterestingscenario
fordiscussionanddebatebecausetheseprojectshavenotonlyreturnedabandoned
spacestothecitybut,infact,havechangeditsimage.Afterdecadesofprojectswith
alternatingstories,andnotalwayssuccessfulasmentionedabove,Milanhasopened
anewseasonthathasalreadytransformedseveralplacesinthecity.
Another type of tension isthose that emerge inthe relationship between public
spacesandprivatepropertiesoractors;thoserepresentadifferenttypologycompared
tothepreviousonesbecausetheydependontheconstructionprocessesofthenew
projects.BothinPortaNuovaandinCityLifetheinvestmentsoflargeprivateactors
(banks, insurance companies) have guaranteed not only the success of the project
anditsqualitybuthavecompensatedthecitythroughthedesignandmaintenanceof
newpublicspacesandparks,todayverysuccessful.
ThestoriesofPortaNuovaandCityLifeprojects,andevenmorethosethatare
being defined now for the transformation of the former railyards, describe a lively
scenario, where architectural design is the protagonist of a new season of changes
whoseconsequences areallstilltobewrittenand imagined.This essayhighlights
somepossibleinterpretationstounderstandthenewongoingtransformationandtheir
architecturesandtofuelanessentialdebateforthefutureofMilan.

Chapter 17
Common Spaces: Collective Practices
for Places of Transition
IaniraVassallo
Abstract Inthelasttwodecades,theconceptofthecommongoodhasincreasingly
enteredthedebatearoundtheprocessesofurbanregeneration.Sometimes,ithasbeen
usedasamanifestotoactivatepoliticalconsensus(especially,inItaly),othersasatool
todesignnewalliancesbetweenpublicandprivatesectorscapableofmakingsocio-
spatialtransformationmoresustainableeconomicallyandatamanageriallevel,and
still others, it is a value that is claimed by informal groups in the processes of
occupation of public or private spaces as a declaration of a collective right to act,
transform, take part in the processes of cities transformation. These are just some
ofthemeaningsthatthetermhastakenoninsomerecentsituations(especially,in
Turin),showing,ontheonehand,animportantcapacityforinnovationbutalsoall
its slipperiness. It can be said that it is certainly a term that introduces the idea of
negotiation into the phenomena of spatial tensions by focussing on some relevant
issueswithrespecttotheroleofthesubjectsandtheabilityoftheprojecttosynthesise
withincomplexsocialprocesses.Theambitionis,therefore,nottoeliminateorpacify
spatialtensionsbuttomakethemgenerativeofnewdynamicsanddesignsensibilities.

toact,transform,and take partinthetransformation processes ofcities (Stavrides

In fact, when producing common assets space represents the demand itself,
whetheritbedefinedbytheintentionsofapublicinstitutionoracollectivity.Inthis
respectthedesignprocesstakesonnewrelevance,butalsoaninterestingcatharsis.
Theprocessdoesnotstartwiththedesiderataoftheclient(eitherpublicorprivate)
or with the economic feasibility of the project, and much less with its implemen-
tation; the core issue is the relational nature of space. In fact, common assets can
be defined as such only when the interest of the collectivity is defined and guar-
anteedduringdevelopmentoftheproject,onewhichacknowledgesthisinterestas
such,quiteapartfromtheprincipleofproperty(Rodotà2013).It’sclear,therefore,
thatspacebecomesasortof‘tradingzone’(Balducci2015)leadingtoaprocessof
‘transactions between different actors’ (Bentley and Dewey 1974, Crosta 2000) in
ordertodefinecommonlanguages,momentsofdiscussion,andmarginsofrelations
anddesigning.Aspatialprojectthatadmitstensionsthereforemeansconsideringits
use,ratherthanitsdesignorthedefinitionofitsfunction,asitscoreissue.Itisan
indeterminate,open,dynamicproject.Andaboveall,oneinwhichtensiongenerates
unusualperspectives.Interestsareseriouslyassessedwithintheseexperiences,not
individually,butasanopportunitytocreateanewcollectivevision.Theresponsi-
bilityoftheactorsinvolvedintheprojectisthedimensionwithinwhichtheirrole
isdefined(Arena2011).Traditionalhierarchiesareabandonedinordertobuildnew
horizontalbalances,questioningindividualinterestsandskillsestablishedbydefault.
Thismakesitpossibletoobserve“thebecomingoftheactor”andunderstandifthis
arenaistherightoneinwhichtobuildprocessesofvirtuousactorshipseveninspatial
repercussions.
Using this filter to observe Turin allows us to highlight how the shift from the
Fordist to the post-industrial city was crucial for the construction of a political-
culturalcontextthatnowconsidersthedebatearoundcommonassetsasapossible
keystoneintheprocessestotransformthecity.
17.1.1 Turin.ACityinTransition
Likemostindustrialcities,inthelateseventies,evenTurinhadtotackleaslow‘urban
transition process’ after the industrial crisis linked to the automobile sector. The
city—onceacompanytown,spatialtranspositionofthecloserelationshipbetween
capital and labour, cradle of the workers’ society—suddenly found itself bereft of
the factory1 which for decades (from the twenties onwards) had defined its urban,
demographic,political,culturalandeconomicsetup(Amari1980;Bagnasco1986).
Fromtheeightiesonwards,Turinwasforcedtoshedtheopaque,protectiveimage
ofafactorycityandforgeanewimage(Vanolo2008).Althoughextensiveliterature

### Page 215

17 CommonSpaces:CollectivePracticesforPlacesofTransition 219
describestheprocessasvirtuous(Governaetal.2009;DurbianoandDeRossi2006),
thetransitionwasnotreallylinear,butinstead,itwentthroughintermittentphases,
rethinksandimportantmomentsofacceleration(Vassallo2015).
ThedraftingofanewGeneralUrbanDevelopmentPlan2byGregottiandCagnardi
Studiomarkedthebeginningofthetransformation;itwasanopportunitytoreverse
the approach to the decentralisation of production, demographic contraction and
reducedemploymentcapacity(Petsimeris1998)thathadstronglycharacterisedthe
cityinthepreviousdecade.3Duringthatperiod,Turinsucceededindevelopinginter-
esting, innovative planning strategies regarding its planning and political profile,
wellinadvanceofwhatwashappeninginItalyandEurope.Manytoolswereused
to revive and renew the city. The ‘season of urban regeneration’ began with the
Special Suburbs Project (1997), followed by four Neighbourhood Contracts, three
UrbanRecoveryProgrammes(PRU),twoUrbanProgrammes,threeIntegratedLocal
DevelopmentProgrammesandotherurbanregenerationinitiativesandlocalpartic-
ipated design initiatives. This approach enabled the city to move beyond the great
transformationprojectsofabandonedareas(e.g.theSpine)anddeveloparea-based
projectsinseveralmarginalcityneighbourhoods.Transformationoftheseareashas
always been accompanied and backed by important social accompaniment initia-
tives and the active involvement of citizens. To underscore the importance of this
approach,Turinestablishedanadhocgovernmentalbody:thesuburbssector.The
latterwasresponsiblefornotonlyinvettingthecandidatesinterestedintheopportu-
nitiesprovidedbytheEuropeanStructuralFunds,butalsoinoverseeingthethirteen
projectsalreadyunderwayinseveralpartsofthecity(foratotalinvestmentofroughly
500millioneuro).Thecreationofadedicatedpublicofficenotonlyallowedthecity
tobecomepartofaEuropeannetworkofcitieswithwhichtoexchangeinformation
abouturbanregenerationinsimilarenvironments,butalsoenhancedthelocalexper-
tiseofpublicadministrationofficials.Thisprocessofexperimentation,networking
and training also had a direct effect on the territory; it led to the establishment of
aclose-knitnetworkoflocalassociationshomogeneouslydisseminatedthroughout
the territory and divided per specific sector of expertise. It also created a support
structureforsocialpoliciesfocussinginparticularonadirectrelationshipofknowl-
edge and trust with the territory. The working class neighbourhoods—symbol of
exclusion, marginalisation and social and spatial urban decay—became ‘places of
redemption’, emblems of a new way to intervene in the city using a capillary and
inclusiveapproachcapableofcreatinganetworkfortheterritorieswhichuptillthen
hadbeenexcludedfromthecentraldynamicsofthecitywheredevelopmentprojects
centredaroundanarrowcircuitofterritorialaffection.Duringthisflourishingseason
ofurbanprojects—boostedbyanotorietythatspreadbeyondnationalborders—the
citysubmitteditscandidacyasavenueforthe2006WinterOlympics.Theeventwas
yetanotheropportunitytospeeduptheongoingtransformationprocess.Increased
funding allowed the city to not only complete (albeit shortly after the due date)
several big infrastructure projects envisaged by the General Urban Development
Plan (1995), but also spark an economic growth linked to the cultural events that
madeitanattractivedestinationforinternationaltourism.Inaddition,thepolitical
continuityofthatperiod4 allowedthecitytoseamlesslycontinueitseconomicand

### Page 216

220 I.Vassallo
cultural ‘repositioning’ process5 both in Italy and abroad.6 A process is primarily
supportedbythefirstStrategicPlanforthecity,notonlyforTurin,butthroughout
Italy.7(Denteetal.2005).
Thisprocessofrapid,celebratedtransformationostensiblycontinuedwithouta
hitch until 2007,8 the year of the advent of the global economic crisis that put a
suddendamperonadevelopmentplanthatwasalreadyallegedlywell-established.
The crisis brought to light the weak points of a system that appeared long-lasting.
Suddenly,however,thoseweakpointscouldnolongerbeignored.
17.2 ApparentStalemate.SpatialTensionsasaRestarting
Point
The 2007 global economic crisis undoubtedly marked the advent of an epochal
change,especiallyinurbanenvironments.Thetwentiethcenturywasunmistakably
over(Bianchetti2011):althoughthegradualdistancingfromtheconcept,ideasand
formsofthemoderncitywasafaitaccompli,itwasneverthelesshardtodescribeand
understandthenewcomplexityrequiredbytheadventoftheincipientcentury.Asin
otherpartsoftheworld,inTurin,theseasonofgreattransformations,animportant
period related to the redesign of whole areas of the city, imploded due to the lack
ofeconomicandpoliticalresources.Duringthisperiodofintensechange,thepost-
Olympiccityproject,supportedbytheSecondStrategicPlan(2006),nevergotoff
the ground. Despite having downsized the vision of an international city in order
to concentrate on the construction of a more compact metropolitan area (socially,
economically and politically), its vocation as a flexible vehicle of aggregation and
compositionofcollectiveinterestswasrevealedasflawedanddifficulttoimplement.
This gave rise to numerous cases of stalemate and a burdensome Olympic legacy,
fraughtwithseriousdisregardedexpectations.Turinwasacitythathadalwaysbeen
studied,analysedandmoderatelycelebrated;itbegantoloseitsshinenotonlylocally,
butalsonationally.Thisparabolaalsomarkedtheendofpoliticalunity,andin2016,
itledtothedemiseofthemuchhypedTurinsystem.9
During this situation of accumulated and implemented ‘crises’ (Doglio and
Zardini2021),unusualformsofreappropriationofurbanspacehavebecomeincreas-
inglyvisible,extensiveandnumerous.Theyarecollectiveprocessesofoccupation
andredesignofcityareasthatarecurrentlydefenceless,waitingforthemuchtouted
transformation. These small, improvised situations are very different, not only as
regardstheirform,butalsoastheirintents/objectives.Theydo,however,shareone
trait: all the parties involved demand greater involvement in urban policy choices.
They are chiefly silent events, small projects and hopeful actions; an attempt to
become visible in a world is where its structure and services (welfare) are falling
apart.
Resemblancewiththesocialmovementoftheeightiesandninetiesissometimes
verystrong,butinactualfact,theymoveinacompletelydifferentcontextanddisplay

### Page 217

17 CommonSpaces:CollectivePracticesforPlacesofTransition 221
substantial differences. Indeed, using the word movements would be misleading.
Theyareextemporaryprocesses,createdbygroups/collectiveswithvariablegeome-
tries,withinterestedpartiesthatareeitherapprovedorinsearchofaffirmation;what
counts, but above all what they highlight, is an answer to a process that had gone
awry.Infact,theobjectiveistoreactivateapublicdebateontheformsandmethods
usedtotransformthecontemporarycity.
Inanoutwardlyinactivecity,frozenbyanabruptchangeofdirectionwhenthe
trajectoryofdevelopmentseemedwell-established,theseprojectshavethepowerto
declare that everything must be reinvented. One of the answers lies in the actions
performedintheseareas.Itisaquietanswer,butonewhichclearsapaththrough
theloosenetworkofactionsleftbyadormantandnolongerrepresentativepolitical
force.Theseprocessescontainimportantelements.Theygiverisetospatialprojects
and social alliances. They are visible evidence of a shift from the right to the city
(Lefebvre 1968; Harvey 2001) to a city of rights, where multiple individual and
collectiveintereststriggerchange.Infact,thecityofTurinhasrecentlywitnesseda
reappropriationofseveralspacesinwaiting;bytryingtoestablishtheirboundaries,
wecanbuildanewgeographyofterritorialdevelopment(Sennet2012).
Inthiscase,thetensionisexpressedbytwo‘city-shaping’models:ontheonehand,
thegreat,unstoppabletransformationthatispartofasystemofactors,competences
and traditional urban planning tools and, on the other, a process based on local
customsandneedsthatareaccuratelyanduniquelydefinedinspacebyplayerswho
areusuallyleftoutofdecision-makingprocesses.
The emblematic places of the fragility of a model so obviously in crisis, thus,
become opportunities to highlight a different, possible value, attributed by a care-
takingprocesswhichhasnothingtodowiththatofthemarket.Infact,internallystruc-
turedprocessesdonotshyawayfromthedisintegrationofthedesignofthatspace
anditsfunctioning,butinsteadaccompanyitandinternallystructurethemselveson
it.Theygenerateprotectedspacesincovetedplaces.
The players active inthese transformations preside over the space; their goal is
tomakeitaccessiblewithoutdenyingitsuseorselectingitsfruitionandusers.The
ensuing inclusiveness or exclusiveness is not important; it is more a sort of self-
government, of DIY welfare, where some spaces represent the chance to design
the city whilst ignoring market rules. They appear to be specific niche issues or
situations, but after careful scrutiny, the image that emerges reveals that they are
internally anchored to the ongoing social changes. This highlights at least three
importantaspects:
(cid:129)
Theyarevisibleevidenceofthecurrentrevolutionregardingtheprogrammingof
servicesandresponsibilities:internally,theydemandprofessionalismandspecific
knowledge;theycreateclosenessbasedoninterestsandvocations;theywishtobe
partofthetransformationsinasituationofimpotencecomparedtoconditionsthat
change;theylookforspaceswheretheycandialogueandreflectonapossible,
alternative and strictly do-it-yourself future. The new economies that develop
withinthesespacesdependonandsurvivethankstotheirabilitytocreatealink

### Page 218

222 I.Vassallo
withthecitythatisbeingstructuredoutsidetheirboundaries,butatthesametime,
theydisplayawilltobeseparatedfromwhatthemarkethastooffer.
(cid:129)
They represent the claims of a cultural elite that feels excluded from decision-
makingvenuesandthedebateaboutthefutureandcitypolicies.Theyusetheir
own knowledge to experiment with new forms of listening, express their own
viewsandaffirmtheirownopinions.Thedynamics inthesespacesoftenbreak
out from the boundaries of these areas and become a topic for debate in other
contexts(universities,collectives,politicalparties,professionalrolls,etc.).
(cid:129)
Spaceisaconcrete,tangibleandabove allavailableelement.Itisonceagaina
hottopicofpublicdebate,anopportunityforcollectivethought.
Forthefirsttime,thedemandovercommonassetsemergeswithintheseprocesses.
17.3 ObservingTurinUsingExperimentalForms
ofConstructionofCommonAssets
As described above, in recent decades, the environment in Turin has adapted well
to very diverse actions of occupation and reappropriation of several urban spaces.
Themainsreasonsforthisundoubtedlyinclude:theavailabilityofmanyabandoned
spacesinthecity(eveninthecentre)afterde-industrialisationandthecrisisofthe
internalindustrialsystem;theimplosionofanurbanmodelindifficultyfromboth
an economic and political point of view (no vision); and the ability and desire by
civilsocietytoreviveadebateontheformsandmethodswithwhichtotransformthe
territory.Withintheseactions,spaceundoubtedlyplaysadualrole:sometimes,itis
theobjectofthedemand(e.g.theMurazzi,thesocialcentre,theoccupiednursery
ortheCavallerizzaReale);atothers,itisthemediumembodyingadifferentwayof
designingthecity(here,IwouldcitetheexperienceofViaFoggiaandthenthatof
theBunkerbythesameorganisation/groupofpeople).
Inthelasttenyears,duringaperiodofausterityurbanism(Peck2012),someof
theseself-makingprocesses(Cellamare2014)usedthetermcommonasset.
Whatisinterestingisthattheenergyusedbyinformalactorstoexpresstheimpor-
tanceofthisconcept(exploitingitasacommunicativesloganoftheirpoliticaland
socialdemands)wasthesameenergyusedbyinstitutionstoestablishthemeaning
theyhaveassignedtotheterm.Wecancitethreecases,threesituationsthatinsome
waysareradicallydifferentduetotheplayersinvolved,theobjectivesandtheinstitu-
tionalframeworksinwhichthecommonassetconcepthasbeenused(rhetoricallyand
sometimespretentiously)todemandinnovativeandoriginalelementstobeincluded
withintheurbanregenerationprocess.
ThefirstistheCavallerizzaReale.10 “TheCavallerizzaRealebelongstoevery-
one”. This slogan is displayed at the entrance to the complex from the main road
leading into it (Via Po). The placard represents the statement announcing the start
of the occupation (23 May 2014) of a public asset that had been on the UNESCO
HeritageListsince2014(andwhichwasoneofthecity’sstate-ownedassetsbeing

soldoff).Theoccupationisrecentlyendedwhenthecomplexwasboughtbyaphil-
anthropicbankfoundationandtheUniversityofTurin.Theoccupationwasinitially
initiatedby:averyheterogeneousgroupofworkersactiveintheworldofartandthe
theatre;freecitizens;professionals;andstudentsandteachersatthepolytechnicand
university. It immediately became an opportunity to experiment with a model that
could be used to create a common asset for the city. Participation in the collective
of university teachers and students who were sensitive to this issue is undeniably
one of the reasons for this choice. The space very quickly became an important
politicalandsocialworkshop.Thedesireforanautonomousprocessanddemands
regarding competences and abilities to create a new model of urban regeneration
(rejecting the selling-off process and focussing on finding a single investor) was
severaloftheclaimsthatclearlyemergedduringtheweeklymeetingsofthethematic
groups(culturalprogramming,economicsustainability,securingthebuilding,etc.),
thepublicassembliesheldonSundaysandtheopen,seamlesscommunicationwith
thecity.Themediadebateonthisissuebecameamajortopicinthearea,triggering

inMilan);italsorapidlygeneratedverymixedvisionsamongstpoliticiansandparty

severaldirectionsandendedinthesaleofthebuilding.Theprocessisinterestingnot
onlybecauseitwasthefirstlocalexperienceregardingthedemandthatapublicasset
becomeacommonasset,butalsobecauseithighlightedatleasttwoveryimportant
issues(partiallyalreadyillustrated):(1)theabilityofthiskindofinformalexperience
togeneratevalue(initiallysymbolicandregardinguse,butlateralsoregardingreal
estate)comparedtourbanassets(inthiscaseamonument)thathadlostanychance
(appeal)ofbeingreinsertedinthelifecycleofthecity;(2)theintentionofseveral
playerstobeinvolvedinthedecision-makingprocessesregardingthetransformation
of the city; these players opposed a neo-liberal model, brought to its knees by the
crisis,infavourofaplace-basedprocessinvolvingthecreationofanactivecommu-
nitytodevelopusesandprojectscapableofensuringgeneralinterest(inthenameof
themunicipality).Therewasonlyoneflaw:duringnegotiations,bothassertedtheir
ownrole,strictlyspeakingpartofthepublic–privatedichotomy,withoutsucceeding
inrevealingtheaspectsandpossiblenatureofthisrelationship.(Figs.17.1,17.2and
17.3).
Thesecondevent,becauseitiswhatitwas,thatIwouldliketohighlightwasthe
evacuationofthebusysocialcentreAsiloOccupatoinViaAlessandria.InFebruary
2019,twenty-fouryearsafteritwascreated,thissocialcentrewasdismantledduring
a massive, nocturnal raid by the police. For days, the area was cordoned off and
surveilledbythearmytoensurethattheoccupantsdidnotreturn.Despitenumerous
demonstrationsandsolidarityinitiativesbythelocalcommunity(inboththeneigh-
bourhoodandthecityatlarge),theverdictremainedunaltered.Inthiscase,thelocal

question was a common asset (it guaranteed a public interest service for the city)

busysocialcentreAsiloOccupatorepresentedanurbancounterculture,aplacefor

anopenandfrankdebateaboutcertainissuesregardingthecity,aspaceofmedia-

remain within their framework of action, are unable to develop new competences
andembracenewrolesofco-planningandcollaborationand,instead,staygluedto
theirownstandpoints.
Thisisastrikingexampleofthepoliticalrelationshipbetweentheconceptofa
commonassetandurbanregenerationprocesses.
The co-city project11 is in stark contrast to the previous case. This European
projectwontheUrbanInnovativeActioncontractnoticeandwasperformedduring
thethree-yearperiod2018–2020.Theaimwastocreateapolycentric,community-
generatingwelfarepolicytoenhancethedevelopmentofcommunitylinkedtospace
requalification projects. The administration immediately made abandoned public
propertyandspacesavailableasthefirstassetofthenewsocialenterpriseprojects;

### Page 222

226 I.Vassallo
it also created a new administrative tool, the “collaboration pact”,12 to replace the
traditionalconcessionprocedureandredefine,inanotexclusivemanner,thecollab-
orationcontractbetweenadministrationandactivecitizenswhopromotedjointcare
andreuseinitiatives.
Thechallengewastostopthegrowthofurbanpovertyandtheemploymentcrisis
byexperimentingwithnewsocialenterprisemodelsthathadastrongimpactonthe
territory; this involved using abandoned heritage in the city and encouraging and
supportingtheinitiativesofactivecitizens13 inordertoshowtherewasadesireto
createanewprotagonisminurbandynamics.Aprotagonismisbasedonresponsi-
bility and reciprocal trust between public and private actors, but also unrelated to
communityserviceprojectsandthecagesofthevoluntarysectorsoastoencourage
andsupportnewformsoftheeconomicsectorandentrepreneurship.
However,thisbegsthequestion:whatroledocommonassetsplayinthisprocess?
Thedecisiontouseabandonedpubicpropertyasboththetopicandobjectiveof
theprojectcan,ontheonehand,boostthecompletionoftheproject,butontheother
hand,itrevivesamoretraditionalwayofconsideringurbanregeneration.Creatinga
commonassetisinfactaprocessofre-significationandco-planningofaspacebya
specificcommunity;assuch,itishardtoconveysinceitisdefinedaboveallthanksto
uniqueandpersonalvalues(whethertheybeaffectiveorinvolveuseoravailability)
thathavelittletodowiththevalueofexchangeandtherealestatemarket.
These three urban experiences, although very different from each other, clearly
showhowtheconceptofthecommongoodentersintoprocessesofurbantensionsas
awaytoclaimdifferentusesandvaluesofspacebydifferentactors.Unfortunately,
this opportunity often doesn’t generate the definition of a co-design process with
innovative alliances but rather is reduced to an ‘exchange’ in which the subjects
involvedmaintaintheirroleandtheirposition.
Notes
1. In 1988, local investors and entrepreneurs pooled their resources to found
the Fabbrica Italiana Automobili Torino (FIAT). The path-breaking factory,
inspiredbyHenryFord’sDetroitmodel,ledtothecity’spopulationgrowing
bynearly50%inthefollowing15years,whilsttheexplodingworkingpopu-
lation of the Fiat factories multiplied fivefold and was packed into crowded
slums.Mostearlyimmigrantscamefromthecountrysideandmountainvalleys
surrounding.AftertheSecondWorldWar,Fiatcapturedthelargestshareofthe
post-warMarshallfundsandmorethandoubleditsworkforcebetween1951
and 1971 up to 115,000 workers, producing 95% of Italy’s cars as the post-
wareconomyboomed.Italy,asafoundingmemberoftheEuropeanCommon
Market, was a strong partner and Torino benefited hugely from its strategic
location,historicconnectionsandindustrialprowess.
2. Belonging to the so-called third generation of urban planning plans, charac-
teristicofthelate1980s,theGeneralUrbanDevelopmentPlanofTurinwas
developed(startingin1986)bytheOfficeoftheUrbanDevelopmentPlanof
theCitydirectedbyFrancoCampia,FrancoFarinaandGiuseppeCazzaniga,

### Page 223

17 CommonSpaces:CollectivePracticesforPlacesofTransition 227
a section of the Urban Planning sector, actively involved with the Milanese
designers Gregotti Associati Studio, Augusto Cagnardi, Pierluigi Cerri and
Vittorio Gregotti. This joint effort led to a new plan for the city (1995) that
had been modified two hundred times involving small and big changes. The
goal was to tackle the transformations of the city, above all envisaging “the
replacement, reorganisation and modernisation of the dilapidated and aban-
doned areas left over from an industrial period that had seemingly ended”
(Spaziante2008,p.18).
3. The Lingotto factory and its assembly chain was closed in 1982; since the
thirties, after the construction of Mirafiori, it was no longer needed and was
convertedtoaccommodateadministrativeoffices.However,theclosureofthe
symboloftheindustrialinnovationofTurincoincidedwiththeendofaseason.
4. From1993to2016,theSistemaTorinowasimplementedinvariouspartsofthe
city.Itwasaperiodofpoliticalcontinuity,markedbyaseriesofcentre-leftcity
councils:ValentinoCastellani1993–2001-SergioChiamparino2001–2011-
PieroFassino2011–2016.
5. ThetransitionfromaFordistmodeltoapost-industrialmodelwasdescribedin
literatureasaprocessofeconomicrepositioning(Berta2016)whichinTurin,
apartfromthetraditionaloutsourcingprocesses,hadinvolvedareinforcement
oftheindustrialsystemoftheautomobilesector(albeitradicallytransformed)
and had opened up to a tourist-cultural economy which up until the nineties
hadnotbeenpresentinthecity.
6. ComparedtotherestofItaly,in1998,thecitylaunchedaprocesstodevelop
aStrategicPlan.AForumforDevelopmentwassetup,chairedbythemayor
Valentino Castellani; after receiving advice from a group of local and inter-
nationalexperts,theForumestablishedguidelinestodefinethe“futurecity”.
TheFirstStrategicPlan,publishedin2000,wasdedicatedtotheinternation-
alisation of Turin. The plan had 6 strategic guidelines, 20 objectives and 84
actions.
